What Is Kalam?
beginner
5 min
The Theologians' Division of Substance: Body and Atom
Defines substance according to theologians and divides it into body and atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians define substance as space-occupying and divide it into body (divisible) and indivisible atom.

Core Idea
For theologians, substance is either a body or an indivisible atom — no third option.

Theologians (mutakallimūn) approach substance from the perspective of spatial occupation. They define substance as that which occupies space (mutaḥayyiz). Based on whether it accepts division, it falls into one of two categories: body (jism) or indivisible atom (jawhar fard). This simple division is foundational to their cosmology and arguments for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the theologians' definition of substance
The passage states that substance is 'mutaḥayyiz bi al-dhāt' — intrinsically occupying space. Understand this key attribute.
This definition excludes non-physical entities like angels or God.
2
Understand the two categories
If the substance can be divided, it is a body. If not, it is an indivisible atom. This binary division is unique to the theologians.
Philosophers reject the indivisible atom.
3
Consider why this matters for kalam
The division is used in arguments for God's existence, such as the proof from accidents and atoms.
Reflect on how this shapes their worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (المقصد الرابع في الجوهر) وفيه مقدمة ومقالتان . أما المقدمة فهي أن الجوهر عندنا إن كان منقسماً فجسم، وإلا فجوهر فرد، وعند جمهور الفلاسفة إن كان حالاً في جوهر فصورة، أو محلاً له فهيولي، أو مركباً منهما فجسم، وإلا فإن تعلق بجسم تدبيراً وتصرفاً فنفس، وإلا فعقل أو يقال إن كان مفارقاً في ذاته وفعله فعقل، أو في ذاته فقط فنفس، وإن كان مقارناً فإما حال أو محل أو مركب أو يقال إن له أبعاد ثلاثة فجسم، وإلا فإما جزء له هو به بالفعل أو لا، وإما خارج متعلق به أو لا، ومبني التقسيم على ما تقرر عندهم من نفي الجوهر الفرد واثبات جوهر حال به الجسم بالفعل، إلى غير ذلك من القواعد، إلا أن الوجه الأخير أولى لاشتماله على ما يوجب تباين الجسم والهيولي، فلا يدخل فيها الجسم الذي هو محل الصورة النوعية . وعند الأقدمين الجوهر إن كان متميزاً فجرماني وهو الجسم لا غير وإلا فروحاني وهو النفس والعقل] . قد سبق تعريف الجوهر على رأي المتكلمين والحكماء، وهذا المقصد مرتب على مقدمة لتقسيمه، وما يتعلق بذلك، ومقالتين بمباحث الأجسام، ومباحث المجردات، أما تقسيمه على رأي المتكلمين، هو أن الجوهر لما كان عبارة عن المتحيز بالذات . فإما أن يقبل الانقسام وهو الجسم أو لا وهو الجوهر الفرد، وعلى رأي المشائين من الحكماء هو أنه إما عقل أو نفس أو جسم أو هيولي، أو صورة، ولهم في بيان ذلك طرق مبناها على ما يرون من نفي الجوهر الفرد وتجرد العقل والنفس، وتحقق جوهرين حال ومحل هما حقيقة الجسم ونحو ذلك من قواعدهم وإلا فعليها إشكالات لا يخفي .
He said: (The fourth objective: on substance) and it contains an introduction and two essays. As for the introduction: that substance according to us [theologians] if it is divisible then it is a body, otherwise it is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard). According to the majority of the philosophers: if it is inherent (ḥāll) in a substance then it is a form (ṣūra), or if it is a substrate (maḥall) for a substance then it is hyle (hayūlā), or if composed of the two then it is a body, otherwise if it is attached to a body by way of direction and disposition (tadbīr wa taṣarruf) then it is a soul (nafs), otherwise it is an intellect (ʿaql). Or it is said: if it is separate (mufāriq) in essence and act then it is an intellect, or only in essence then it is a soul, and if it is concomitant (muqārin) then either inhering (ḥāl) or substrate (maḥall) or composite (murakkab). Or it is said: if it has three dimensions then it is a body, otherwise either a part of it (juzʾ lahu) which it actually possesses or not, or an external thing attached to it or not. The basis of the division is what is established among them of denying the indivisible atom and affirming an inhering substance by which the body actually exists, among other principles, except that the last aspect is preferable because it includes what necessitates the distinction between body and hyle, so the body that is a substrate for the specific form does not enter into it. Among the ancients: substance, if it is distinct (mutamayyiz), then it is corporeal (jurmānī) which is the body only, otherwise it is spiritual (rūḥānī) which is the soul and intellect. The definition of substance from the perspective of the theologians and philosophers has preceded, and this objective is arranged with an introduction to its division and what pertains to that, and two essays: on investigations of bodies and investigations of abstract entities. As for its division according to the theologians: that substance, since it is that which is intrinsically occupant of space (mutaḥayyiz bi al-dhāt), either accepts division, which is the body, or not, which is the indivisible atom. And according to the Peripatetic philosophers: it is either an intellect, or a soul, or a body, or hyle, or a form. They have means of explaining that based on what they hold: denying the indivisible atom, the abstractness of intellect and soul, the reality of two substances (inhering and substrate) which are the reality of the body, and similar principles of theirs; otherwise there are objections that are not hidden.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification lesson. The author begins the fourth objective by stating it contains an introduction and two essays. The introduction defines substance (jawhar) according to two schools: the theologians (mutakallimūn) and the philosophers (falāsifa). For the theologians, substance is that which occupies space (mutaḥayyiz). If it is divisible, it is a body (jism); if not, it is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard). The author then presents the philosophers' division in several ways. First, they classify substance based on whether it inheres in another substance (form, ṣūra), serves as a substrate (hyle, hayūlā), is composed of the two (body), is attached to a body through direction and disposition (soul, nafs), or is separate (intellect, ʿaql). Another philosophical division is based on separateness: if separate in essence and act, it is an intellect; if only in essence, it is a soul; if not separate (muqārin), it is either inhering, substrate, or composite. Another division uses three dimensions: if it has three dimensions, it is a body; otherwise it is either a part of it, or an external thing. The author notes that the philosophers build their division on principles like denying the indivisible atom and affirming an inhering substance that makes a body actual. Among these, the last aspect (based on three dimensions) is preferred because it distinguishes body from hyle, so the body that is a substrate for the specific form is not included in hyle. The passage then mentions the ancient division into corporeal (body only) and spiritual (soul and intellect). Finally, the author recaps: the objective has an introduction and two essays (bodies and abstract entities), and elaborates that theologians define substance as space-occupying and divide into body and atom, while Peripatetics divide into five categories: intellect, soul, body, hyle, form. Their methods are based on their principles, despite objections.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
متحيز (mutaḥayyiz)
جسم (jism)
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theologians and philosophers agree on the division of substance.
Correction: They differ fundamentally: theologians divide into body and atom; philosophers divide into form, hyle, body, soul, intellect.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians, what are the two divisions of substance?
A) Body and indivisible atom
B) Body and soul
C) Intellect and soul
D) Hyle and form

Key Takeaway
For the theologians, substance is either a body (divisible) or an indivisible atom (indivisible).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the theologians affirm the indivisible atom as a fundamental reality. If you genuinely believe that Allah created each atom, why do you still act as if some parts of your life are outside His control?
?
The division of substance into body and atom is exhaustive—no third option. Is your faith in Allah also so clear-cut, or do you allow compromises that blur the line between belief and neglect? What step will you take today to remove one such compromise?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
7 min
The Philosophers' Multiple Divisions of Substance
Presents the philosophers' multiple divisions of substance: by inherence, separateness, and dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various ways philosophers classify substance, including the Peripatetic fivefold division and other criteria.

Core Idea
Philosophers classify substance by inherence, composition, separateness, and dimensions — not by divisibility.

Unlike the theologians' simple binary, the philosophers (especially Peripatetics) divide substance in multiple ways. They classify based on whether a substance inheres in another (form), serves as a substrate (hyle), is composed (body), or is separate (soul/intellect). They also use criteria like dimensions and separateness. This complex taxonomy reflects their metaphysical system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first philosophical division
The passage lists: if a substance is inherent (ḥāl) in another, it is form; if it is a substrate (maḥall), it is hyle; if composed, body; if attached to body by direction, soul; otherwise, intellect.
This covers all categories.
2
Examine the second classification based on separateness
Here, if a substance is separate (mufāriq) in essence and act, it is intellect; if separate only in essence, it is soul; if concomitant (muqārin), it is either inherent, substrate, or composite.
This overlaps with the first.
3
Consider the third classification based on three dimensions
If a substance has three dimensions, it is body; otherwise it is either a part of it (e.g., an atom) or an external thing (e.g., a form).
The author prefers this because it distinguishes body from hyle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (المقصد الرابع في الجوهر) وفيه مقدمة ومقالتان . أما المقدمة فهي أن الجوهر عندنا إن كان منقسماً فجسم، وإلا فجوهر فرد، وعند جمهور الفلاسفة إن كان حالاً في جوهر فصورة، أو محلاً له فهيولي، أو مركباً منهما فجسم، وإلا فإن تعلق بجسم تدبيراً وتصرفاً فنفس، وإلا فعقل أو يقال إن كان مفارقاً في ذاته وفعله فعقل، أو في ذاته فقط فنفس، وإن كان مقارناً فإما حال أو محل أو مركب أو يقال إن له أبعاد ثلاثة فجسم، وإلا فإما جزء له هو به بالفعل أو لا، وإما خارج متعلق به أو لا، ومبني التقسيم على ما تقرر عندهم من نفي الجوهر الفرد واثبات جوهر حال به الجسم بالفعل، إلى غير ذلك من القواعد، إلا أن الوجه الأخير أولى لاشتماله على ما يوجب تباين الجسم والهيولي، فلا يدخل فيها الجسم الذي هو محل الصورة النوعية . وعند الأقدمين الجوهر إن كان متميزاً فجرماني وهو الجسم لا غير وإلا فروحاني وهو النفس والعقل] . قد سبق تعريف الجوهر على رأي المتكلمين والحكماء، وهذا المقصد مرتب على مقدمة لتقسيمه، وما يتعلق بذلك، ومقالتين بمباحث الأجسام، ومباحث المجردات، أما تقسيمه على رأي المتكلمين، هو أن الجوهر لما كان عبارة عن المتحيز بالذات . فإما أن يقبل الانقسام وهو الجسم أو لا وهو الجوهر الفرد، وعلى رأي المشائين من الحكماء هو أنه إما عقل أو نفس أو جسم أو هيولي، أو صورة، ولهم في بيان ذلك طرق مبناها على ما يرون من نفي الجوهر الفرد وتجرد العقل والنفس، وتحقق جوهرين حال ومحل هما حقيقة الجسم ونحو ذلك من قواعدهم وإلا فعليها إشكالات لا يخفي .

Misconception: Philosophers have only one division of substance.
Correction: They have multiple overlapping divisions, as shown in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the Peripatetic philosophers, which of the following is NOT one of the five categories of substance?
A) Intellect
B) Soul
C) Body
D) Atom

Key Takeaway
Philosophers classify substance using multiple criteria, resulting in categories like form, hyle, body, soul, and intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers used multiple criteria to classify substance because a single framework could not capture all realities. Where in your own understanding of existence—whether of God, the soul, or the world—are you relying on only one criterion? What might you be missing by not considering other perspectives?
?
In the philosophers' classification, the soul is a substance attached to a body through direction and disposition. Do you live as if your soul is merely an add-on to your body, or do you recognize its separate essence? How would your daily priorities change if you truly believed the soul is a distinct substance that can exist apart?
?
The ancients' simple division into corporeal and spiritual is appealing, but the added nuances of form, hyle, and intellect reveal a deeper complexity. Are you content with oversimplified categories of the spiritual realm, or are you willing to study the details to better understand the nature of your own intellect and soul? What step will you take this week to deepen that knowledge?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
5 min
First Philosophical Division of Substance (Peripatetic)
Peripatetic classification of substance into five categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of substance according to the first Peripatetic way and the logical criteria that distinguish them.

Core Idea
Substance is classified into five ranks based on dependence and independence.

Sanusi begins by presenting the first of two philosophical methods for dividing substance, attributed to the Peripatetics. This method uses a sequential 'if-then' logic to sort any substance into one of five exclusive categories: form, hyle, body, soul, or intellect. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping the later discussion of divine existence in philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for flow, then the English translation. Note how the author uses a series of negations and affirmations.
The passage is tightly structured; each condition excludes the previous.
2
Identify the five categories
As you read, list the five categories in order: form, hyle, body, soul, intellect. Notice the logical pattern: each category is defined by what it is not.
The classification is exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
3
Understand the criteria
The criteria revolve around inherence (ḥulūl) and substrate (maḥall). Understand each term: form inheres; hyle is the substrate; body is composite; soul directs a body; intellect is separate.
4
Reflect on the trajectory
Notice the movement from most dependent (form) to most independent (intellect). This hierarchy is important for later proofs of God's existence.
The intellect is the most 'noble' substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الأول. أن الجوهر إن كان حالاً في جوهر آخر فهي الصورة وإلا فإن كان محلاً له فهي الهيولي وإلا فإن كان مركباً من الحال والمحل فهو الجسم، وإلا فإن تعلق بالجسم تعلق التدبير والتصرف فالنفس، وإلا فالعقل
The first way: that substance, if it is inhering (ḥāll) in another substance, then it is the form (ṣūra); otherwise, if it is a substrate (maḥall) for it, then it is the hyle (hayūlā); otherwise, if it is composed of the inhering and the substrate, then it is the body (jism); otherwise, if it is attached to the body with the attachment of direction and disposition (tadbīr wa taṣarruf), then it is the soul (nafs); otherwise, it is the intellect (ʿaql).
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two philosophical ways (ṭarīq) of dividing substance, attributed to the Peripatetics. It uses a sequential classification based on the relation of a substance to other substances. The division proceeds as follows: if a substance inheres in another substance (i.e., it exists in another substance as an accident in a subject), it is called a form (ṣūra). If it does not inhere but instead serves as a substrate for another substance, it is called hyle (hayūlā), which is the underlying matter. If it is composed of both an inhering principle and a substrate, it is a body (jism). If it is not a body but is attached to a body by way of direction and disposition (like the soul directing the body), it is the soul (nafs). If it is none of these, i.e., it is entirely separate from bodies and does not inhere, it is the intellect (ʿaql). Thus the five categories are: form, hyle, body, soul, and intellect. The classification is exhaustive, moving from the most dependent (form) to the most independent (intellect).

Key Terms
صورة
هيولي
جسم
نفس
عقل
حال
محل
تدبير وتصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This is the only philosophical division of substance.
Correction: The author presents a second way with different criteria later. This is the first of two Peripatetic divisions.

Checkpoint
In the first philosophical way, what is a substance that is inhering in another substance called?
A) Hyle
B) Body
C) Form
D) Soul

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetics classify all substances into five exclusive categories: form, hyle, body, soul, and intellect, based on their mode of relation to other substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this classification, the intellect is the most independent substance, not attached to any body. Where in your life do you find your mind being overly attached to physical comforts or social validations, preventing it from functioning with true independence? What concrete step can you take today to detach your thinking from that attachment?
?
The soul is defined by directing and disposing the body. Are you consciously directing your body according to your intellect and faith, or is your soul being led by bodily desires? Which of your daily habits reflect that your soul is in charge?
?
If every substance in this world falls into one of these five categories, consider your own existence: you are a body with a soul. How often do you reflect on your intellect as the highest substance within you, and what does that imply for your purpose in life?

The Second Way of Dividing Substance: Separateness in Essence and Act
Peripatetic division of substance by separateness in essence and act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Peripatetic classification of substances based on whether they are separate in essence and act, and the resulting categories of intellect, soul, inhering quality, substrate, and composite.

Core Idea
A substance's separateness in essence and act determines whether it is intellect, soul, or a material component.

This lesson introduces the second way the Peripatetic philosophers divide substance. Instead of looking at composition (form/matter/composite), this approach focuses on whether a substance is independent of another substance (separate in essence) and whether it acts independently (separate in act). This yields a five-fold classification that correlates with the first division but uses different criteria.

Guided Path
1
Understand separateness in essence
Recognize that a substance separate in essence (mufāriq fī dhātihi) is independent of another substance for its existence.
This is the primary dividing factor.
2
Differentiate separate in act
Among substances separate in essence, check if they are also separate in act (i.e., do not need a body to act).
3
Identify intellect and soul
If separate in both essence and act, it is the intellect (ʿaql). If separate in essence but not act, it is the soul (nafs).
Both are separate in essence, but differ in act.
4
Study concomitant substances
If not separate in essence (muqārin), the substance is either inhering (ḥāl), a substrate (maḥall), or composed (murakkab) of both.
These correspond to form, hyle, and body.
5
Relate to the first division
Note how this second way correlates with the earlier composition-based division: inhering and substrate are form and hyle, composite is body, separate are soul and intellect.
This shows different perspectives on the same reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني: إن الجوهر إن كان مفارقاً في ذاته بأن يكون مستغنياً عن مقارنة جوهر آخر. فإما أن يكون مفارقاً في فعله أيضاً وهو العقل أو لا وهو النفس وإن لم يكن مفارقاً في ذاته ، بل مقارناً لجوهر آخر ، فإما أن يكون حالاً أو محلاً أو مركباً منهما
The second way: that substance, if it is separate (mufāriq) in its essence, meaning it is independent of conjunction with another substance, then either it is separate in its act as well, which is the intellect (ʿaql), or not, which is the soul (nafs). If it is not separate in its essence, rather it is concomitant (muqārin) to another substance, then either it is inhering (ḥāl) or a substrate (maḥall) or composed (murakkab) of the two.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the second way of dividing substance according to the Peripatetic philosophers. The classification is based on the concept of separateness (mufāraqa) in essence and act. The division proceeds as follows: first, consider whether the substance is separate in its essence, meaning it does not need to be conjoined with another substance for its existence. If it is separate in essence, then further consider its act. If it is also separate in its act (i.e., it does not need a body for its actions), it is called the intellect (ʿaql). If it is separate in essence but not in act (i.e., it still needs a body to act), it is called the soul (nafs). If the substance is not separate in essence, meaning it is concomitant (muqārin) with another substance, then it must be either an inhering quality (ḥāl) in another substance, a substrate (maḥall) for another substance, or a composite (murakkab) of both inhering and substrate. This second way thus correlates with the first: the inhering and substrate are form and hyle, the composite is body, and the separate ones are soul and intellect, but the criteria are now distinctiveness.

Key Terms
مفارق (mufāriq)
ذات (dhāt)
فعل (fiʿl)
مقارن (muqārin)
حال (ḥāl)
محل (maḥall)
مركب (murakkab)
عقل (ʿaql)
نفس (nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is not separate at all.
Correction: The soul is separate in essence but not in act; it needs a body for its actions.

Checkpoint
According to the second way, what distinguishes the intellect from the soul?
A) The intellect is separate in essence while the soul is not
B) The intellect is separate in both essence and act, while the soul is separate in essence only
C) The intellect is separate in act only, while the soul is separate in essence
D) The intellect is concomitant while the soul is separate

Key Takeaway
The second division of substance uses separateness in essence and act to produce five categories: intellect, soul, inhering quality, substrate, and composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life are you like the intellect—acting independently of material desires—and in what areas are you like the soul, still dependent on bodily conditions? What one change this week could elevate your actions to reflect your true essence?
?
If your essence is separate (like the soul) but your actions are tied to this body, what does that say about your ultimate purpose? Are you living as if your reality ends with the body, or are you preparing for a state where your actions become separate too?

The Theologians' Division of Substance
Al-Taftazani states the theologians' definition and division of substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians define substance and how they divide it into body and indivisible substance.

Core Idea
Substance is defined as that which occupies space; it is either divisible (body) or indivisible (atom).

Al-Taftazani begins by recalling the definition of substance used by theologians, which serves as the foundation for their classification. Theologians see substance as anything that occupies space, and from this they derive two categories: body and indivisible substance. This lesson will clarify that fundamental division, which is essential for understanding later discussions on bodies and atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the theologians' view
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice that it first gives the definition and then splits it into two types.
The definition is 'that which occupies space by its essence' (al-mutahayyiz bi al-dhat).
2
Identify the two categories
The division is based on whether the substance accepts division. Find the two categories: body (jism) and indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-fard).
Body is divisible; indivisible substance is not.
3
Understand the significance
This binary classification is foundational for kalam. Ask yourself why indivisible atoms are necessary in Kalām cosmology.
It relates to debates about space, motion, and the composition of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد سبق تعريف الجوهر على رأي المتكلمين والحكماء، وهذا المقصد مرتب على مقدمة لتقسيمه، وما يتعلق بذلك، ومقالتين بمباحث الأجسام، ومباحث المجردات، أما تقسيمه على رأي المتكلمين، هو أن الجوهر لما كان عبارة عن المتحيز بالذات . فإما أن يقبل الانقسام وهو الجسم أو لا وهو الجوهر الفرد، وعلى رأي المشائين من الحكماء هو أنه إما عقل أو نفس أو جسم أو هيولي، أو صورة، ولهم في بيان ذلك طرق مبناها على ما يرون من نفس الجوهر الفرد وتجرد العقل والنفس، وتحقق جوهرين حال ومحل هما حقيقة الجسم ونحو ذلك من قواعدهم وإلا فعليها إشكالات لا يخفي .
The definition of substance (al-jawhar) has preceded according to the theologians (al-mutakallimun) and the philosophers (al-hukama), and this objective is arranged on an introduction for its division, and what relates to that, and two articles on the investigations of bodies (ajsam) and the investigations of abstract entities (mujarradat). As for its division according to the theologians: substance, since it is that which occupies space by its essence (al-mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), then either it accepts division, and that is body (jism), or it does not, and that is the indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-fard). And according to the Peripatetics (al-masha'in) of the philosophers, it is either intellect (aql), or soul (nafs), or body (jism), or prime matter (hayula), or form (sura). And they have in explaining that methods (turuq) built on what they hold regarding the denial of the indivisible substance, the abstractness of intellect and soul, the reality of two substances: accident (hal) and substrate (mahall) which are the reality of body, and the like of their principles; otherwise upon them are problems that are not hidden.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of the classification of substance (jawhar) as a foundation for the rest of the book's objective. It first recalls the definition of substance according to theologians (mutakallimun) as 'that which occupies space by its essence' (al-mutahayyiz bi al-dhat). Based on this, theologians divide substance into two: body (jism), which is divisible, and indivisible substance (jawhar fard), which is not divisible. Then it presents the division according to the Peripatetic philosophers (masha'in), who recognize five types of substance: intellect (aql), soul (nafs), body, prime matter (hayula), and form (sura). Al-Taftazani notes that the philosophers have three methods (turuq) to establish this division, which are based on their denial of the indivisible substance, the abstractness of intellect and soul, and their view that body is composed of accident (hal) and substrate (mahall). He hints that these methods have problems, but he will not elaborate here.

Key Terms

الجسم
الجوهر الفرد
المتحيز بالذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'substance' in Kalām refers only to body.
Correction: The theologians include indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-fard) as substances, which are not bodies.

Checkpoint

B) That which occupies space by its essence.
C) That which is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Theologians divide substance into body (divisible) and indivisible substance (atom), based on the property of occupying space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What change must you make in your daily contemplation to truly recognize the Creator behind every atom?
?

?

How can you carry this awareness into your next action, reminding yourself that Allah is the Creator of all, from the macrocosm to the microcosm?

The Peripatetic Philosophers' Division of Substance
Al-Taftazani states the Peripatetic division of substance into five types and mentions their methods and problems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five types of substance according to the Peripatetic philosophers and the philosophical basis of their classification.

Core Idea
Peripatetics divide substance into five: intellect, soul, body, prime matter, and form.

After presenting the theologians' binary division, al-Taftazani turns to the Peripatetic philosophers (the Mashsha'i). Their classification is more elaborate, recognizing five distinct types of substance: intellect, soul, body, prime matter, and form. This lesson explores these categories and the philosophical principles underpinning them, including the denial of the indivisible atom and the composition of body from accident and substrate. Al-Taftazani notes that these views are not without problems, but he sets them out as part of the broader discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the philosophers' view
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it lists five types of substance and then mentions methods and problems.
The five types are: intellect, soul, body, prime matter, form.
2
Identify the five categories
Make a mental list of the five substances. Understand that 'intellect' and 'soul' are immaterial, while 'body' is material composed of 'prime matter' and 'form'.
These categories are foundational for Peripatetic metaphysics.
3
Reflect on the basis and challenges
The passage indicates that their classification is based on specific principles (denial of atom, abstractness of intellect/soul, etc.). Al-Taftazani hints that there are problems with these views. Reflect on why these principles might be controversial.
The 'problems' refer to objections from theologians and other philosophers.

The First Method of Classifying Substance
Al-Taftazani lists five types of substance by conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical method for classifying substance into five categories based on conditional properties.

Core Idea
Substance is classified by its relation: form, matter, body, soul, or intellect.

Philosophers have various ways to categorize substance. Al-Taftazani presents one method that proceeds step by step using conditional statements. This classification helps distinguish between material and immaterial substances, from the most concrete to the most abstract.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the pattern 'if ... then ... otherwise if ...'.
The text uses a series of conditional disjunctions.
2
Identify each category
Go through each condition and identify which substance type it yields: form, prime matter, body, soul, or intellect.
The order is from most concrete to most abstract.
3
Understand the key terms
Review the key terms such as hal (accident), mahall (substrate), and the five types. See how they relate to the classification criteria.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to test your grasp of the classification.
The question is directly from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الأول. أن الجوهر إن كان حالاً في جوهر آخر فهي الصورة وإلا فإن كان محلاً له فهي الهيولي وإلا فإن كان مركباً من الحال والمحل فهو الجسم، وإلا فإن تعلق بالجسم تعلق التدبير والتصرف فالنفس، وإلا فالعقل
The first method: that substance, if it is an accident (hal) in another substance, then it is form (sura); otherwise, if it is a substrate (mahall) for it, then it is prime matter (hayula); otherwise, if it is composed of the accident and the substrate, then it is body (jism); otherwise, if it is attached to the body with the attachment of management and disposition (al-tadbir wa al-tasarruf), then it is soul (nafs); otherwise, it is intellect (aql).
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical method (al-tariq al-awwal) for classifying substance. It proceeds by a series of conditional disjunctions. The starting point is substance in general. The first division: if substance is an accident (hal) inhering in another substance, then it is form (sura). If not, then check if it is a substrate (mahall) for another substance; if yes, it is prime matter (hayula). If not, then check if it is composed of an accident and a substrate; if yes, it is body (jism). If not, then check if it is attached to a body with the relationship of management and disposition (tadbir wa tasarruf); if yes, it is soul (nafs). If none of the above, then it is intellect (aql). This method systematically covers all possibilities, starting from the most concrete (form inhering) to the most abstract (disembodied intellect).

Key Terms
الصورة
الهيولي
الجسم
النفس
العقل
الحال
المحل
التدبير والتصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This classification proves the existence of these substances.
Correction: It is a logical classification based on assumed properties, not a proof of existence. It helps categorize substances we already accept.

Checkpoint
According to the first method, what is substance called if it is a substrate (mahall) for another substance?
A) Form (sura)
B) Prime matter (hayula)
C) Body (jism)
D) Soul (nafs)

Key Takeaway
The first method classifies substance into five mutually exclusive categories based on conditional properties: form, prime matter, body, soul, and intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson classifies your soul (nafs) as a substance that manages and disposes of your body. Do you truly feel that your soul is in charge of your physical actions? Is there any area of your life where your body overrides your soul's management? What change can you make today to reassert that connection?
?
The intellect (ʿaql) is described as detached from the body. How does this align with your experience of thinking through physical challenges? Are you willing to trust your intellect over your physical desires, even when they conflict? What is one decision you need to make that requires this trust?

Second Method for Classifying Substance
Al-Taftazani presents the second philosophical method for classifying substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second philosophical method for classifying substance using the criterion of separateness in essence and action.

Core Idea
Substance is classified by whether it is separate in essence and action.

Philosophers developed multiple ways to categorize substances. This second method uses the concept of 'separateness' (mufaraqa) in both essence and action to distinguish between intellect, soul, and material substances. It provides a clear logical tree for understanding different types of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure starting with 'if... then...'
This is a binary classification tree.
2
Identify the first criterion
The first division is whether a substance is separate in its essence, i.e., independent of another substance. Note what follows from each branch.
Separate in essence means it does not need a body to exist.
3
Trace the subdivisions
For substances separate in essence, check if they are separate in action. For those not separate in essence, classify them as accident, substrate, or composed of both.
Intellect is separate in both; soul is separate in essence but not action.
4
Reflect on the categories
Consider how this classification maps to intellect, soul, accident, substrate (prime matter), and body. Why is this useful for theology?
This method helps analyze the nature of different created beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني: إن الجوهر إن كان مفارقاً في ذاته بأن يكون مستغنياً عن مقارنة جوهر آخر. فإما أن يكون مفارقاً في فعله أيضاً وهو العقل أو لا وهو النفس وإن لم يكن مفارقاً في ذاته ، بل مقارناً لجوهر آخر ، فإما أن يكون حالاً فيه، أو محلاً له ، أو مركباً منهما
The second method: that substance, if it is separate (mufariq) in its essence by being independent of association with another substance, then either it is separate in its action also, and that is intellect (aql), or not, and that is soul (nafs). And if it is not separate in its essence, but rather associated with another substance, then either it is an accident (hal) in it, or a substrate (mahall) for it, or composed of both.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical method for classifying substance. It uses the criterion of separateness (mufaraqa) in essence and action. First, consider whether substance is separate in its essence, i.e., independent of association with another substance. If yes, then check its action: if also separate in action (i.e., it does not need a body to act), then it is intellect (aql); if not separate in action (i.e., it manages a body), then it is soul (nafs). If the substance is not separate in essence, i.e., it is always associated with another substance, then it must be either an accident (hal) inhering in another, or a substrate (mahall) for another, or composed of both (i.e., body). This method neatly distinguishes between abstract intellect, soul (which is abstract but acts on body), and material substances (body, form, prime matter).

Key Terms
مفارق
متعلق
العقل
النفس
الحال
المحل
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the soul is not separate in essence because it relates to the body.
Correction: The soul is separate in essence (it can exist independently) but not separate in action (it manages a body).

Checkpoint
According to the second method, what is substance called if it is separate in essence but not separate in action?
A) Intellect (aql)
B) Soul (nafs)
C) Body (jism)
D) Accident (hal)

Key Takeaway
This method classifies substance into intellect, soul, accident, substrate, and body based on separateness in essence and action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul is separate in essence but not in action—it manages your body. Do you consciously let your soul guide your actions, or do your physical desires dominate? What one change can you make today to let your soul lead?
?
Intellect is completely separate from matter in both essence and action. When was the last time you used your intellect to think about something purely spiritual, without any worldly distraction? How can you create more space for such thinking?
?
The body is a composite of substrate and accident. Which part of you is more real: your physical form or the soul that animates it? How does your answer affect how you treat your body and your soul?

Third Method: Classification by Receptivity
Al-Taftazani presents the first formulation of the third method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first formulation of the third philosophical method for classifying substance based on receptivity to three dimensions and part-whole relations.

Core Idea
Substance classification begins with whether it can receive three dimensions.

Al-Taftazani begins the third method by presenting a classification that starts with the property of being receptive to the three dimensions (length, width, depth). This leads to a binary tree distinguishing body, form, matter, soul, and intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice the use of 'qabil' (receptive) as the first criterion.
2
Trace the decision tree
Identify the first division: receptive to dimensions? Yes → body; No → then ask if it is part of a body.
The second division uses 'juz’' (part) and distinguishes actual vs. potential.
3
Understand the subtypes
For parts: actual → form, potential → matter. For non-parts: disposing → soul, otherwise → intellect.
The term 'mutasarrif' (disposing) implies governance, not mere presence.
4
Connect to the reference
Note that this formulation is attributed to Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, setting up the next formulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث : إن الجوهر إن كان قابلاً للأبعاد الثلاثة فجسم، وإلا فإن كان جزءاً منه هو به بالفعل فصورة، أو بالقوة فمادة، وإن لم يكن جزءاً منه فإن كان متصرفاً فيه فنفس ، وإلا فعقل وهذا ما قال في الشفاء. إن الجوهر إن كان مركباً فجسم، وإن كان بسيطاً ، فإن كان داخلاً في تقويم المركب. فإما دخول الخشب في وجود الكرسي فمادة ، أو دخول شكل الكرسي فيه فصورة، وإن لم يكن داخلاً فيه، بل مفارقاً فإن كان له علاقة ما في الأجسام بالتحريك فنفس وإلا فعقل .
The third method: that substance, if it is receptive (qabil) of the three dimensions, then it is body (jism). Otherwise, if it is a part of it (i.e., of the body) which is actual (bi al-fi'l), then it is form (sura); or potential (bi al-quwwa), then it is matter (madda). And if it is not a part of it, then if it is disposing (mutasarrif) in it, then it is soul (nafs); otherwise, it is intellect (aql). And this is what was said in al-Shifa: that substance, if it is composite (murakkab), then it is body; if it is simple (basit), then if it enters into the constitution (taqwim) of the composite, then either like the entry of wood in the existence of a chair, then it is matter (madda), or like the entry of the shape of the chair in it, then it is form (sura); and if it does not enter into it, but is separate (mufariq), then if it has some relation to bodies by way of moving (tahrik), then it is soul (nafs); otherwise, it is intellect (aql).
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the third philosophical method for classifying substance, attributed to Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. It first considers whether substance is receptive (qabil) of the three dimensions (length, width, depth). If yes, it is body. If not, then check if it is a part of a body: if actual (bi al-fi'l), it is form; if potential (bi al-quwwa), it is matter. If it is not a part of a body, then check if it is disposing (mutasarrif) in the body (i.e., governing it), then it is soul; otherwise, it is intellect. The method then gives an alternative formulation from al-Shifa: if substance is composite, it is body; if simple, then if it enters into the constitution of a composite, it is either matter (like wood for a chair) or form (like the shape of the chair); if it does not enter but is separate, then if it has a relation to bodies by moving them, it is soul; otherwise, intellect.

بالقوة
مادة

Misconception: The three dimensions refer only to length, width, and depth as physical measurements.
Correction: That is correct; the passage does not introduce any other interpretation.

Checkpoint

B) Matter
C) Soul
D) Intellect

Key Takeaway
Substance classification begins with receptivity to dimensions; non-receptive substances are classified by whether they are parts of bodies and their actuality or potentiality.

This passage defines the soul as a non-body substance that disposes within the body. Do you live as if your soul governs your body, or are you governed by your physical desires? What specific action can you take today to let your soul lead?
?
Intellect is described as a substance independent of bodies and disposition. Does your intellectual life reflect that independence, or do material concerns dominate your thinking? How can you cultivate pure intellect in your daily study?
?
Ibn Sina's classification was used to understand the self. If your essence is not your body but something that receives or disposes, how does that change your view of death and your ultimate return to God? What will you do differently knowing this?

Third Method: Classification by Composition (al-Shifa)
Al-Taftazani presents the al-Shifa formulation of the third method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second formulation of the third philosophical method from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, which classifies substance based on composition, constitution, and separation.

Core Idea
Substance is either composite (body) or simple, and simple substances are divided by their role in composites.

This lesson presents the exact formulation from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, using the criteria of composition (murakkab vs. basit) and whether a simple substance enters into the constitution of a composite or is separate and moves bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the shift from the previous formulation.
This formulation uses 'murakkab' (composite) and 'basit' (simple).
2
Identify the first division
The passage asks: is the substance composite? If yes, it is body. If simple, then proceed.
Composite here means having parts; body is the result.
3
Understand the constitution criterion
For simple substances: does it enter into the constitution (taqwim) of a composite? If yes, it is either matter (like wood) or form (like shape).
The analogy of a chair clarifies matter as the material and form as the structure.
4
Handle separate substances
If the simple substance does not enter into a composite but is separate (mufariq), then check if it moves bodies. If yes, soul; otherwise, intellect.
This covers immaterial substances like souls and intellects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث : إن الجوهر إن كان قابلاً للأبعاد الثلاثة فجسم، وإلا فإن كان جزءاً منه هو به بالفعل فصورة، أو بالقوة فمادة، وإن لم يكن جزءاً منه فإن كان متصرفاً فيه فنفس ، وإلا فعقل وهذا ما قال في الشفاء. إن الجوهر إن كان مركباً فجسم، وإن كان بسيطاً ، فإن كان داخلاً في تقويم المركب. فإما دخول الخشب في وجود الكرسي فمادة ، أو دخول شكل الكرسي فيه فصورة، وإن لم يكن داخلاً فيه، بل مفارقاً فإن كان له علاقة ما في الأجسام بالتحريك فنفس وإلا فعقل .
The third method: that substance, if it is receptive (qabil) of the three dimensions, then it is body (jism). Otherwise, if it is a part of it (i.e., of the body) which is actual (bi al-fi'l), then it is form (sura); or potential (bi al-quwwa), then it is matter (madda). And if it is not a part of it, then if it is disposing (mutasarrif) in it, then it is soul (nafs); otherwise, it is intellect (aql). And this is what was said in al-Shifa: that substance, if it is composite (murakkab), then it is body; if it is simple (basit), then if it enters into the constitution (taqwim) of the composite, then either like the entry of wood in the existence of a chair, then it is matter (madda), or like the entry of the shape of the chair in it, then it is form (sura); and if it does not enter into it, but is separate (mufariq), then if it has some relation to bodies by way of moving (tahrik), then it is soul (nafs); otherwise, it is intellect (aql).
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the third philosophical method for classifying substance, attributed to Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. It first considers whether substance is receptive (qabil) of the three dimensions (length, width, depth). If yes, it is body. If not, then check if it is a part of a body: if actual (bi al-fi'l), it is form; if potential (bi al-quwwa), it is matter. If it is not a part of a body, then check if it is disposing (mutasarrif) in the body (i.e., governing it), then it is soul; otherwise, it is intellect. The method then gives an alternative formulation from al-Shifa: if substance is composite, it is body; if simple, then if it enters into the constitution of a composite, it is either matter (like wood for a chair) or form (like the shape of the chair); if it does not enter but is separate, then if it has a relation to bodies by moving them, it is soul; otherwise, intellect.

Correction: The wood-chair analogy is illustrative; matter and form are principles applicable to all composite substances.

Checkpoint

B) Form

Key Takeaway
The al-Shifa formulation classifies substance as composite (body) or simple, with simple substances divided by whether they constitute composites (matter/form) or are separate and move bodies (soul/intellect).

Objection and Answer on Potentiality of Matter
Al-Taftazani responds to an objection about matter's potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defends the philosophical distinction between matter and form by clarifying that matter gives potential existence and form gives actual existence, despite their inseparability.

Core Idea
Matter gives potential existence; form gives actual existence.

The philosophers classify substance into matter and form, where matter is potential and form is actual. But an objector raises a concern: since matter and form are inseparable, how can matter be purely potential when it always exists with actual form? In this passage, al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing two perspectives: the composite is potential with respect to matter alone, but actual with respect to form. This answer preserves the logical distinction without denying the inseparability of matter and form in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection (fa'in qila)
Notice how the objector argues that body is always actual with matter, because matter cannot be separated from form. This challenges the claim that matter is merely potential.
The objector assumes that if matter is always accompanied by form, it must be actual, not potential.
2
Read the answer (qulna)
Al-Taftazani clarifies that the claim 'matter is potential' refers to the composite with respect to matter itself, not to the composite as a whole. The actuality of the composite comes from form, not matter.
The phrase 'bi al-quwwa' (potential) applies only when considering matter in isolation, not the composite.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider how the answer resolves the objection: it shows that potentiality of matter does not contradict the actuality of the composite, because the composite's actuality is attributed to form. This preserves the philosophical division without logical inconsistency.
Al-Taftazani cites al-Shifa to reinforce that form is what makes the composite 'identical' (huwa huwa) in actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الجسم يكون مع الهيولي أيضاً بالفعل البتة لامتناع انفكاكها عن الصورة كما سيجيء. قلنا : المراد أن وجود المركب بالنظر إلى المادة نفسها من حيث أنها مادة لا يكون إلا بالقوة، وبالنظر إلى الصورة بالفعل على ما قال في الشفاء. إن المادة هي ما لا يكون باعتباره وحدة للمركب وجود بالعقل بل بالقوة، والصورة إنما يصير المركب هو هو بالفعل بحصولها، حتى لو جاز وجود الصورة بدون المادة لكان مستلزماً لحصول المركب بالفعل البتة .
If it is said: body is also actually (bi al-fi'l) with prime matter (hayula) absolutely, because its separation from form (sura) is impossible, as will come. We say: what is meant is that the existence of the composite with respect to matter itself, inasmuch as it is matter, is only potential (bi al-quwwa), and with respect to form it is actual (bi al-fi'l), as was said in al-Shifa: Matter is that by whose consideration the composite does not have existence in actuality (bi al-fi'l) but in potentiality (bi al-quwwa), and form is that by whose attainment the composite becomes identical (huwa huwa) in actuality, such that if the existence of form without matter were possible, it would necessitate the attainment of the composite in actuality absolutely.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (fa'in qila) to the philosophers' division. The objector points out that body always exists with prime matter (hayula) in actuality (bi al-fi'l), since matter cannot be separate from form. Therefore, the claim that matter is potential while form is actual might be problematic. The answer (qulna) clarifies by distinguishing two perspectives: with respect to matter itself, the composite exists only potentially; with respect to form, it exists actually. Al-Taftazani cites al-Shifa to explain that matter is that which gives the composite only potential existence, while form actualizes it. Even if form could exist without matter, that would still entail the composite's actual existence. Thus, the potentiality of matter does not contradict the actuality of the composite, because the composite's actuality derives from form, not matter alone.

الهيولي
الصورة
بالفعل
بالقوة
المركب
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if matter is inseparable from form, then matter cannot be potential—it must be actual.
Correction: The passage clarifies that matter is potential when considered by itself, while the composite as a whole is actual due to form. Potentiality and inseparability are not contradictory.

Checkpoint

A) Actually (bi al-fi'l)
B) Potentially (bi al-quwwa)
C) Both actually and potentially
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Matter gives potential existence; form gives actual existence; the composite's actuality stems from form, not matter alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that form (sura) gives actuality to the composite. In your own life, what is the 'form' that actualizes your potential for good? Are you relying on your own 'matter' (potential) without seeking the activating form of divine guidance?
?
The passage distinguishes between potential existence (matter) and actual existence (form). Consider your faith: Are you content with potential belief, or have you actualized it through consistent action? What is the form that makes your faith actual?
?
The objector thought that inseparability of matter and form means matter is actual. Reflect: Do you mistakenly assume that being close to good things (like knowledge, community) automatically makes you good? The lesson says actuality comes only from the form itself. What 'form' is missing in your life that would make your potential actual?

Definition of Body and Dispute with al-Qadi
Defines body and rebuts al-Qadi's opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of body (jism) as a substance receptive of division and the argument that the composite whole, not its parts, is the body.

Core Idea
Body is the composite whole, not its parts.

This passage introduces one of the foundational definitions in Islamic theology: what is a body? The author presents the definition accepted by the verifiers, and then defends it against a contrary view by al-Qadi. The discussion hinges on a precise analysis of the meaning of 'composition' (taʾlīf). Pay attention to how the author distinguishes two senses of 'composed' to resolve the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the core definition and objection
Find the author's definition of body (al-qābil lil-inqisām) and al-Qadi's objection that each part is a body because it is a composed substance.
Al-Qadi's reasoning is based on the premise that every composed substance is a body.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
Notice how the author refutes al-Qadi by explaining that composition cannot inhere in two parts simultaneously and by distinguishing two meanings of 'composed' (from a thing vs. with a thing).
The grammatical analogy helps clarify the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الأول : الجسم عندنا القابل للانقسام فيتناول المؤلف من الجزئين فصاعداً، لا كل واحد منهما، على ما زعم القاضي تمسكاً بأنه الذي قام به التأليف، فيكون مؤلفاً، وكل مؤلف جسم للفرق الظاهر بين المؤلف من الشيء ومع الشيء. لا خفاء ولا نزاع في أن لفظ الجسم في لغة العرب وكذا ما يرادفه في سائر اللغات موضوع بازاء معنى واحد واضح عند العقل من حيث الامتياز عما عداه، لكن الخفاء حقيقته، وتكثر لوازمه كثر النزاع في تحقيق ماهيته، واختلفت العبارات في تعريفه، وأدى ذلك إلى اختلاف في بعض الأشياء إنه هل يكون جسماً أم لا؟ فعند المحققين من المتكلمين هو الجوهر القابل للانقسام من غير تقييد بالأقطار الثلاثة. فلو فرضنا مؤلفاً من جوهرين فردين كان الجسم هو المجموع لا كل واحد منهما كما زعم القاضي تمسكاً بأنه جوهر مؤلف، وكل جوهر مؤلف جسم وفاقاً، ومبني الصغري على امتناع قيام التأليف بالجزأين لامتناع قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين، بل لكل جزء تأليف قائم به وهو معنى المؤلف. والجواب : أن التأليف معنى بين الشيئين يعتبر استناده إلى المجموع من حيث هو المجموع فيكون مؤلفاً من الشيء وإلى كل واحد فيكون مؤلفاً مع الشيء كما يقال في النحو الكلام هو المركب الذي فيه الإسناد والمعرب المركب الذي لم يشبه مبني الأصل، فالجسم هو المؤلف بالمعنى الأول والجزء بالمعنى الثاني فلا تكرر للوسط.
The first investigation: The body, in our view, is what is receptive of division. So it encompasses the composite of two parts and more, not each one of them individually, contrary to what al-Qadi claimed, clinging to the fact that it is that in which composition inheres, so it is composed, and every composed thing is a body, due to the clear difference between 'composed from the thing' and 'composed with the thing'. There is no concealment nor dispute that the word 'body' in the Arabic language, and likewise its equivalents in other languages, is placed in correspondence to a single meaning clear to the intellect in terms of its distinction from other things. However, hiddenness of its reality and the multiplicity of its concomitants led to much dispute in the investigation of its quiddity, and expressions differed in its definition, and that led to disagreement regarding some things as to whether they are a body or not. So according to the verifiers among the theologians, it is the substance receptive of division without restriction to the three dimensions. For if we suppose something composed of two individual substances, then the body is the whole, not each one of them, as al-Qadi claimed, clinging to the fact that it is a composed substance, and every composed substance is a body by agreement. The basis of the minor premise is the impossibility of the inherence of composition in the two parts because it is impossible for a single accident to inhere in two loci; rather, each part has a composition inhering in it, and that is the meaning of 'composed'. And the answer: Composition is a relation between two things; its attribution to the whole as whole makes it 'composed from the thing', and to each one makes it 'composed with the thing', as is said in grammar: 'Speech is the composite that contains predication, and the declinable word is the composite that does not resemble the base of the declension.' So the body is the composed in the first sense, and the part is the composed in the second sense; thus the middle term is not repeated.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-argument unit. It first states the definition of body (jism) according to the author's school: 'what is receptive of division' (al-qābil lil-inqisām). From this, the body encompasses the composite of two or more parts, not each part individually. This contradicts al-Qadi's view that each part is itself a body because it is a composed substance. The author then notes that the word 'body' is commonly understood but its reality is disputed. The verifiers define it as 'substance receptive of division without restriction to three dimensions' (i.e., not necessarily extended in length, width, depth). The argument proceeds: if we have two individual substances (jawharayn fardayn), the body is the whole composite, not each part. Al-Qadi's reasoning is that each part is a composed substance, and every composed substance is a body. But the minor premise is false because composition (ta'līf) cannot inhere in two parts simultaneously—each part has its own composition. The author then refines the meaning of ta'līf: it is a relation (maʿnā bayna l-shayʾayn) that when attributed to the whole yields 'composed from the thing' (muʾallaf min al-shayʾ) and when attributed to each part yields 'composed with the thing' (muʾallaf maʿa al-shayʾ). An analogy from grammar is given: speech (kalām) is a composite that contains predication, while the declinable word (muʿrab) is a composite that does not resemble the base. Thus, body is composed in the first sense and part in the second, avoiding repetition of the middle term (takarruh al-wasaṭ).

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
انقسام (inqisām)
جوهر (jawhar)
تأليف (taʾlīf)
مؤلف (muʾallaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each part of a composite is itself a body because it is a composed substance.
Correction: Only the composite whole is a body; the part is 'composed with the thing' not 'composed from the thing'. Composition cannot inhere in two parts simultaneously.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the definition of body (al-jism)?
A) The substance that has three dimensions.
B) The substance receptive of division.
C) The composite of two or more atoms.
D) The individual substance that is composed.

Key Takeaway
The body is defined as a substance receptive of division, and only the composite whole qualifies as a body, not its individual parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define yourself—by your career, relationships, or achievements—do you focus on individual 'parts' and miss the 'whole' that Allah sees? How can you shift your self-perception to align with the holistic view of being a servant of Allah?
?
The passage shows that even clear terms like 'body' can be misunderstood. In your own faith, are you certain that your understanding of core concepts like tawhid or prophethood is complete? What would it cost you to admit that you might be missing something?
?
Al-Qadi clung to a plausible but flawed argument. Is there an area in your life where you defend a comfortable belief despite evidence? How can you cultivate the intellectual courage to let go of error for the sake of truth?

Objection and Analysis of the Dispute on Composition
Addresses objection that composition is a specific accident and analyzes whether the dispute is verbal or real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dispute over composition is analyzed into verbal and real dimensions, and the differences between al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila on inherence.

Core Idea
The dispute about composition may be verbal or real — and the difference matters.

This passage presents an objection about whether composition is a specific accident, then discusses how al-Amidi and the author of al-Mawaqif analyzed the dispute. It also clarifies al-Qadi's position and notes a problem in framing the debate. Understanding this helps you see how kalam scholars distinguish between semantic and ontological disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the objection that composition is a specific accident, and the brief answer denying the major premise.
The objection uses technical terms like 'ʿaraḍ khāṣṣ'.
2
Identify the two framings
Find where al-Amidi and the author of al-Mawaqif are mentioned. Note how they characterize the dispute: one as verbal, the other as real.
Al-Amidi focuses on the meaning of the word 'body'.
3
Understand al-Qadi's position
See how al-Qadi affirms composition but differs on inherence. He says composition inheres in each part individually, not in the two parts together.
This makes each part a body, like matter and form.
4
Note the critical remark
The author points out that the majority of his school also affirm composition and non-inherence in two parts, so the dispute with al-Qadi is unclear. Also, the dispute with Mu'tazila is often misconstrued.
This shows the complexity of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المراد بالتأليف عرض خاص مغاير لمعناه اللغوي المشعر بالانضمام المقتضى للتعدد وهو السبب عند المعتزلة لصعوبة الانفكاك. فالجواب : حينئذ منع الكبرى وجعل الآمدي النزاع لفظياً عائداً إلى أن لفظ الجسم بإزاء أي معنى وضع، وصاحب المواقف معنوياً عائداً إلى أنه هل يوجد عند اجتماع الأجزاء وحصول الجسم عرض خاص هو التأليف والاتصال، والسبب الصعوبة الانفكاك على ما يراه المعتزلة أم لا بل الجسم هو نفس الأجزاء المتجمعة، فالقاضي يحكم بوجوده ، لكن يزعم أنه ليس قائماً بالجزئين كما هو رأى المعتزلة، بل لكل جزء تأليف يقوم به فيكون جسماً لما سيجيء من أن الجزء بمنزلة المادة، والتأليف بمنزلة الصورة، وفيه نظر لأن جمهور الأصحاب أيضاً قائلون به وبعدم قيامه بجزئين، وإن جعل النزاع بينه وبين المعتزلة بمعنى أنهم قائلون بالتأليف دونه ففساده أكثر لأن القاضي يقول بالتأليف وهم لا يقولون بجسمية الجوهرين.
For if it is said: What is meant by composition is a specific accident different from its linguistic meaning that implies conjunction necessitating multiplicity, and it is the reason according to the Mu'tazila for the difficulty of separation. Then the answer: In that case, the major premise is denied, and al-Amidi made the dispute verbal, returning to the question of what meaning the word 'body' is assigned to; and the author of al-Mawaqif made it real, returning to whether when parts come together and a body exists, there is a specific accident that is composition and connection, which is the reason for the difficulty of separation as the Mu'tazila hold, or not, but rather the body is the parts themselves collected. So al-Qadi affirms its existence, but he claims that it does not inhere in the two parts as is the view of the Mu'tazila—rather, each part has a composition inhering in it, so it becomes a body, because it will come that the part is like matter and composition is like form. And there is consideration in this because the majority of our companions also affirm it and affirm its non-inherence in two parts. And if the dispute between him and the Mu'tazila is taken to mean that they affirm composition while he does not, then its corruption is greater, because al-Qadi affirms composition and they do not affirm that two substances form a body.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer, followed by analysis of the dispute. The objection claims that composition (taʾlīf) should be understood as a specific accident (ʿaraḍ khāṣṣ), distinct from its linguistic meaning of mere conjunction; this accident is what makes separation difficult, as the Mu'tazila hold. The answer denies the major premise of that objection. Then the author reports two ways of framing the dispute. Al-Amidi considered it verbal (lafẓī): the disagreement is about what meaning the word 'body' is assigned to. The author of al-Mawaqif considered it real (maʿnawī): whether upon aggregation of parts there exists a specific accident called composition and connection, or not. Al-Qadi affirms the existence of that accident but differs from the Mu'tazila in saying it inheres in each part individually (rather than in the two parts together), making each part a body. The author then raises a critical note (fīhi naẓar): the majority of the author's own school (al-aṣḥāb) also affirm composition and its non-inherence in two parts, so the dispute with al-Qadi is not clear-cut. Moreover, if the dispute with the Mu'tazila is that they affirm composition while al-Qadi does not, that is even more corrupt because al-Qadi does affirm composition, while the Mu'tazila deny that two substances form a body.

Key Terms
تأليف (taʾlīf)
عرض خاص (ʿaraḍ khāṣṣ)
لفظي (lafẓī)
معنوي (maʿnawī)
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila disagree on whether composition exists at all.
Correction: Actually, both affirm composition, but they differ on inherence: al-Qadi says it inheres in each part individually, while the Mu'tazila hold it inheres in the two parts together (or perhaps not at all).

Checkpoint
How did al-Amidi characterize the dispute about the definition of body?
A) As a real dispute about the existence of a specific accident.
B) As a verbal dispute about the meaning of the word 'body'.
C) As a dispute about whether body is material or spiritual.
D) As a dispute about the number of dimensions.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over composition can be interpreted as either verbal (about the meaning of 'body') or real (about the existence of a specific accident), and the key difference between al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila is in the inherence of composition, not its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement, do you first ask whether it is about words or reality? This passage shows that mislabeling the nature of a dispute leads to confusion. In your own debates, will you commit to distinguishing verbal from real disagreements before taking sides?
?
Al-Qāḍī and the Muʿtazila differed on where composition inheres, yet both believed in it. Are there beliefs you hold that are closer to those you oppose than you think? This lesson invites you to examine your own positions honestly rather than assuming total opposition.
?
The critical note (fīhi nazar) reveals that even within a school, scholars disagree. Do you seek to understand the nuances of theological positions, or do you settle for partisan labels? This passage challenges you to engage deeply with texts and avoid superficial judgments.

The Mu'tazila Definition of Body and Its Critique
Al-Taftazani presents the Mu'tazila definition of jism and an objection to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of body as a proper property (khassa) and the objection that it is neither comprehensive nor necessary.

Core Idea
A body defined by potential dimensions fails actual tests.

The Mu'tazila, known for their rational theology, defined the body not by essence but by a distinctive property: being long, wide, and deep. Al-Taftazani presents this definition, explains its basis as a 'proper property' (khassa), and then subjects it to a sharp critique. This lesson walks through the definition, its defense, and the powerful objection that it is neither comprehensive (not all bodies have actual dimensions) nor necessary (dimensions can change while corporeality remains). You will also see the beginning of an answer that may deepen your understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the complete passage in Arabic and its literal translation. Notice the structure: definition, explanation, objection, answer.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the definition's nature
The Mu'tazila define body as 'the long, wide, deep'. They do not claim this is a definition by essence (hadd) but a drawing by a proper property (rasm bi-l-khassa). Why? Because they do not affirm a mathematical body, so the three dimensions are not essential but characteristic.
Note the distinction between 'hadd' (limit/definition) and 'rasm' (drawing/description).
3
Identify the objection's two prongs
The objection says a proper property must be comprehensive and necessary. This definition fails: comprehensiveness is violated by spheres (no actual line) and infinite bodies (no actual surface); necessity is violated by wax that changes dimensions.
Consider why the sphere example works: a sphere has no actual straight line, yet is a body.
4
Examine the beginning of the answer
The answer begins, 'And it was answered, after conceding that the absence of line and surface in act necessitates that the body is not described with them...' The text ends here. Reflect on what a complete answer might need to address.
The answer is incomplete; we may need to consult later pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وعند المعتزلة) هو الطويل العريض العميق، وهذا التعريف بالخاصة اللازمة الشاملة، بناء على أن المراد قبول تلك الأبعاد على الإطلاق، فلا يضر انتفاؤها بالفعل كما في الكرة، ولا تبدلها مع بقاء الجسمية كما في الشمعة على أن ذلك عندهم عائد إلى ترتيب الأجزاء من غير إثبات المقادير زائدة على الجسمية ، وهذا جعلوه خالية (١) بدون افتقار إلى ذكر الجوهر، وأما قيد العرض والعمق، فاحتراز عن المركب، الذي هو واسطة بين الجسم والجوهر الفرد، وذلك بأن يكون تركب أجزائه على سمت أو سمتين فقط، أو يكون عددها أقل من أدنى ما يتركب منه الجسم أعني ثمانية أو ستة أو أربعة على اختلافهم في ذلك . المشهور بينهم في تعريف الجسم أنه الطويل العريض العميق، ولا نزاع لهم في أن هذا ليس بحد بل رسم بالخاصة ، ومبني كونها خاصة، على أنهم لا يثبتون الجسم التعليمي الذي هو كم له الأبعاد الثلاثة لتكون هذه عرضاً عاماً يشمله، فيفتقر إلى ذكر الجوهر احترازاً عنه، ويكون المجموع خاصة مركبة للجسم الطبيعي كالطائر الولود للخفاش، ولا يضره كون الجوهر جنساً لأن المركب من الداخل والخارج خارج على أنه يحتمل أن يراد بالطويل مثلاً ما يكون الطول أي الامتداد المفروض أولاً عارضاً له فلا يشمل الجسم التعليمي لأن هذه الأبعاد أجزاؤه . واعترض بأن الخاصة إنما تصلح للتعريف إذا كانت شاملة لازمة. وهذه ليست كذلك أما الشمول فلأنه لا خط بالفعل في الكرة ولا سطح فيما يعرض من الجسم الغير المتناهي فإنه جسم وإن امتنع بدليل من خارج بخلاف ما إذا فرض أربعة لیست بزوج. فإن الزوجية من لوازم الماهية ، وأما اللزوم فلأن الشمعة المعينة قد يجعل طولها تارة شبراً وعرضها أصابع، وتارة ذراعاً وعرضها إصبعاً، فيزول ما فيها من الأبعاد مع بقاء الجسمية . وأجيب بعد تسليم أن انتفاء الخط والسطح لفعل يستلزم عدم اتصاف الجسم :
He said: (And according to the Mu'tazila) it is the long, wide, deep. This definition is by the necessary comprehensive proper property, based on the fact that what is meant is the acceptance of those dimensions absolutely, so their actual absence does no harm, as in the sphere, nor their change while corporeality remains, as in the wax, provided that according to them that returns to the arrangement of parts without affirming quantities additional to corporeality. And they made this a unique property without need to mention the substance. As for the restriction of width and depth, it is a precaution against the composite that is intermediate between the body and the individual substance, which is that its parts are arranged in only one or two directions, or their number is less than the minimum from which the body is composed, namely eight or six or four according to their disagreement on that. The well-known among them in defining the body is that it is the long, wide, deep, and there is no dispute among them that this is not a limit but a drawing by proper property. And the basis for it being a proper property is that they do not affirm the mathematical body (jism ta'limi) which is a quantity with three dimensions, so that these (dimensions) would be a general accident including it; thus they need to mention the substance as a precaution against it, and the sum becomes a composite proper property for the natural body like the flying young for the bat. And it does no harm that the substance is a genus because the composite of internal and external is external; however it is possible that what is meant by 'long' for example is what has length—i.e., the extension presupposed first—as an accident for it, so it does not include the mathematical body because these dimensions are its parts. And it was objected that the proper property is only suitable for definition if it is comprehensive and necessary. But this (property) is not so: as for comprehensiveness, because there is no actual line in the sphere and no surface in what occurs from the infinite body, for it is a body even if impossible by an external proof, unlike if one posits four that are not a pair, for parity is among the necessities of quiddity; and as for necessity, because a particular candle may sometimes have its length a span and its width fingers, and sometimes a cubit and its a finger, so the dimensions are removed while corporeality remains. And it was answered, after conceding that the absence of line and surface in act necessitates that the body is not described with them:
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila definition of body (jism) as 'the long, wide, deep', and then an objection to it. It explains that this definition is by a necessary comprehensive proper property (khassa), meaning that the body is defined by its potential to receive three dimensions, not actual presence (so a sphere has no actual line but still is a body). They avoid mentioning substance (jawhar) because they do not affirm a mathematical body; the dimensions are just arrangements of parts. The well-known position is that it is a drawing (rasm) by proper property, not a limit (hadd). The objection argues that a proper property for definition must be comprehensive and necessary, but this one is not: it is not comprehensive because infinite bodies have no actual surface, and not necessary because wax changes dimensions while remaining a body. The answer begins by conceding that the absence of line and surface in act entails that the body is not described with them, but the answer is incomplete as the text ends.

عميق
خاصة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila definition of body as 'long, wide, deep' is a definition by essence (hadd).
Correction: The passage explicitly states it is a drawing by proper property (rasm bi-l-khassa), not a limit. They do not claim the three dimensions are essential; they are characteristic.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection claim that the Mu'tazila definition is not comprehensive?
A) Because a sphere has no actual line.
B) Because a candle changes dimensions.
C) Because it includes mathematical bodies.
D) Because it excludes composite bodies.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila definition of body by the proper property of being 'long, wide, deep' fails to be comprehensive and necessary, as shown by the sphere and the wax examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila defined the body by what is not essential—a property that can change. What external, changeable properties do you use to define your own worth or identity? Are you willing to let go of them and be defined solely by your unchanging relationship with Allah? Commit to one concrete step this week to detach from a worldly label.
?
The sphere has no actual line, yet it is still a body. What invisible spiritual realities (like taqwa, sincerity, or love for Allah) do you possess potentially but fail to actualize? Name one and write down a specific action to make it actual today.
?
The wax changes dimensions yet remains the same body. When your life circumstances change drastically—loss of wealth, health, or status—what remains of your faith? If all external 'dimensions' were stripped away, would your core identity as a servant of Allah still hold firm? What can you strengthen today to ensure it does?
