Body as Receiver of Three Dimensions
Explains dimensions, defines body, and answers two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of body (jism) in kalam as 'what receives the three dimensions' and how the author defends it against objections concerning imagination and hyle.

Core Idea
A body is not just anything with dimensions; it must receive them in external existence, not mere imagination.

This lesson examines a foundational definition in kalam: body (jism) is defined as 'what receives the three dimensions.' Al-Sanusi clarifies the nature of dimensions and then defends this definition against two critical objections. The first objection concerns the imagination (wahm) supposing dimensions in non-bodies like the soul, and the second concerns hyle (hayula) as a substance that seemingly receives dimensions. Through precise distinctions, the author shows why the definition holds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of dimensions
Read the opening explanation where the author distinguishes linear distance (bu'd khatti) from a line, and surface distance (bu'd satihi) from a surface. Notice that length and width can exist without corresponding lines or surfaces in a body without internal separation.
This sets the stage for the definition.
2
Grasp the definition of body
Identify the definition: 'qabil al-ab'ad al-thalatha' (receiver of the three dimensions). Note that the author adds the qualification that this is with respect to external existence (al-wujud al-khariji), not mere supposition.
The definition is not about imaginary dimensions.
3
Analyze the first objection and answer
The Imam's objection: imagination can suppose three dimensions in a non-body (e.g., the soul). The answer: the definition applies to external existence, not to what is merely imagined. The soul is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) and thus not a body.
This clarifies the scope of the definition.
4
Analyze the second objection and answer
The objection about hyle (hayula): hyle is a substance that receives the three dimensions, but only conditionally via form. The answer: hyle receives forms, not dimensions; dimensions belong to the form (sura) which is substantial extension (imtidad jawhari). The objection might be more relevant to form.
This preserves the definition by distinguishing the roles of hyle and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون بين نهايتين غير متلاقيتين. ومن شأنه أن يتوهم فيه نهايات من نوع تينك النهايتين، فقد يكون بعد خطي من غير خط وسطحي من غير سطح كما في الجسم الذي لا انفصال في داخله بالفعل فإنك إذا فرضت فيه نقطتين فبينهما بعد خطي ولا خط ، وإذا فرضت خطين متقابلين فبينهما بعد سطحي ولا سطح، وذلك البعد الخطي طول والسطحي عرض فيظهر الفرق بين الطول والخط ، وبين العرض والسطح حيث يوجد الأول بدون الثاني، وإن لم يوجد خط بلا طول وسطح بلا عرض . قال (والمراد قبول أعيانها) أورد الإمام أن الوهم يصح فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيه، وليس بجسم فأجاب بأن المراد ما يكون كذلك بحسب الوجود الخارجي كما في قولهم الرطب ما يقبل الإشكال بسهولة، ولا خفاء في أنه تحقيق للمقصود بحيث لا يرد الاعتراض بالنفس التي هي جوهر مجرد يقبل الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة، وإلا فظاهر أن الوهم خارج بقيد الجوهرية، والحاصل أن المراد صحة فرض الأبعاد بحيث يتحقق الاتصاف بها ، وذلك في الوجود المتأصل لا غير ومن اعتراضاته أن الهيولي جوهر يصح فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيها غايته أن قبولها للأبعاد يكون مشروطاً بقبولها للصورة الجسمية، ولا يجوز أن تكون الصورة جزءاً من القابل لما تقرر عندهم من أنها مبدأ الفعل، والحصول دون الإمكان والقبول، بل الجوهر القابل هو الهيولي لا غير وجوابه أن ما اختص الهيولي بقبوله هو الصور لا الأعراض من الكميات والكيفيات وغيرها كيف وقد صرحوا بأنه لاحظ للهيولي من المقدار : وإنما ذلك إلى الصورة فإنها امتداد جوهري به قبول الامتدادات العرضية على أنه قد سبق أن المراد بهذا القبول ما يعم الفعل ولو لزوماً، ولعل هذا الاعتراض بالنسبة إلى الصورة أوجه .
It exists between two non-meeting endpoints. And it is of the nature that endpoints of the type of those two endpoints can be supposed in it. So there may be a linear distance without a line and a surface distance without a surface, as in a body that has no actual internal separation. For if you suppose two points in it, there is between them a linear distance but no line; and if you suppose two opposite lines, there is between them a surface distance but no surface. That linear distance is length and the surface distance is width, so the difference appears between length and line, and between width and surface, where the first exists without the second, although no line exists without length nor surface without width. He said: (And what is meant is the reception of their essences). The Imam objected that the imagination (wahm) can validly suppose the three dimensions in what is not a body. He answered that what is meant is that which is such according to external existence (al-wujud al-khariji), as in their statement: 'moist (ratb) is what readily receives shapes.' And there is no doubt that this is a verification of the intended meaning such that the objection does not arise concerning the soul, which is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) that receives the three intersecting dimensions. Otherwise, it is apparent that the imagination is excluded by the restriction of substantiality (jawhariyya). The conclusion is that what is meant is the validity of supposing the dimensions such that the qualification by them is actualized, and that is only in autonomous existence (al-wujud al-muta'asil). And among his objections is that hyle (hayula) is a substance in which it is valid to suppose the three dimensions, except that its reception of dimensions is conditional upon its reception of corporeal form (sura jismiyya). And it is not permissible for the form to be part of the recipient, according to what is established with them that it is the principle of act (mabda' al-fi'l) and attainment (husul) without potentiality (imkan) and reception (qabul). Rather, the receptive substance is hyle alone. Its answer is that what hyle is specialized in receiving is forms (suwar), not accidents (a'rad) of quantities (kammiyyat), qualities (kayfiyyat), and others. How could it be, when they have explicitly stated that hyle has no share of magnitude (miqdar)? Rather, that belongs to the form, for it is substantial extension (imtidad jawhari) by which receptivity of accidental extensions (imtidadat 'aradiyya) is actualized, especially since it has preceded that what is meant by this reception includes act necessarily. And perhaps this objection is more appropriate concerning the form.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory and defensive discussion of the definition of body (jism) as 'what receives the three dimensions.' It begins by clarifying the nature of dimensions: linear distance (bu'd khatti) and surface distance (bu'd satihi) can exist without actual lines or surfaces, using the example of a body without internal separation. This establishes that length and width are not identical to line and surface. The author then states the definition (قابل الأبعاد الثلاثة). An objection is raised: the imagination (wahm) can suppose three dimensions in something that is not a body, such as the soul (nafs). The answer distinguishes that the definition refers to external existence (al-wujud al-khariji), not imaginary supposition. It is like the definition of 'moist' as readily accepting shapes. The soul is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) and thus not a body; the restriction of substantiality excludes it. A second objection concerns hyle (hayula), which is a substance that receives the three dimensions conditionally via form. The answer is that hyle receives forms, not dimensions; dimensions belong to the form, which is substantial extension (imtidad jawhari) that receives accidental extensions. The passage concludes that the objection might be more appropriately directed at form, not hyle.

جسم (jism)
بعد خطي (bu'd khatti)
بعد سطحي (bu'd satihi)
قابل الأبعاد الثلاثة (qabil al-ab'ad al-thalatha)
وجود خارجي (wujud khariji)
وهم (wahm)
جوهر مجرد (jawhar mujarrad)
هيولي (hayula)
صورة (sura)
امتداد جوهري (imtidad jawhari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition 'receiver of three dimensions' includes anything that can be imagined with dimensions, such as the soul or abstract space.
Correction: The author clarifies that the definition applies only to external existence (al-wujud al-khariji), not to mere supposition by the imagination (wahm). The soul, as a simple substance, does not have external existence as a body.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that the imagination can suppose three dimensions in a non-body like the soul?
A) He says the soul is a body but different.
B) He says the definition applies only to external existence, not imaginary supposition.
C) He says the imagination cannot suppose dimensions in a soul.
D) He says the soul does not receive dimensions at all.

Key Takeaway
The definition of body as 'receiver of three dimensions' is precise: it requires external existence, and neither the soul nor hyle qualify as bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is not a body and does not occupy physical dimensions, then your true self is not your physical form. Are you living today as if you are only a body, or are you actively nurturing the reality of your soul? What concrete step can you take within the next hour to prioritize your soul over your body?
?
Your body is real and has dimensions, but it is a temporary vessel. Does the way you care for your body reflect that it is a trust from Allah, or does it reveal an excessive attachment to physical appearance? What would it mean for you to treat your body as a means of worship rather than an end in itself?
?
The hyle objection reminds us that even though hyle receives forms, it does not directly receive dimensions. Similarly, you may receive many influences in life, but not all become part of you. Are you careful about which 'forms' you allow to shape your character? Name one influence you need to either embrace or reject starting today.

Imam al-Razi's Arguments Against the Definition as Hadd
Al-Razi's three arguments against the definition being a hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's three main arguments against the definition of body as a proper definition (hadd).

Core Idea
Al-Razi shows that 'substance' cannot be a genus for body.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a powerful critique of the standard definition of body. He argues that calling it a proper definition (hadd) is problematic because the alleged genus 'substance' fails to meet logical requirements. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's first argument
Al-Razi argues that 'substance' is not a proper genus because it is interpreted as 'existing not in a subject', which involves superadded existence.
2
Read the second argument
If substance were a genus, then differentiae would be needed; they would either be substances (infinite regress) or accidents (impossible).
3
Read the third argument
Receptivity (qabiliyya) is a consideration, not a real attribute, so it cannot serve as a differentia.
This leads to an infinite regress if taken as real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وكلامهم متردد) في أن هذا التعريف حد أو رسم (١)، وأبطل الإمام كونه حداً، بأن ليس الجوهر جنساً له لكونه مفسراً بالموجود لا في موضوع، والوجود زائد، بل من المعقولات الثانية، ولا في موضوع عدمي، ولأنه لو كان جنساً لكان تمايز الجواهر بفصول، وهي إما جواهر فيتسلسل أو أعراض فيتقوم مع الجوهر بالعرض، ولا القابلية وما في معناها بفصل لكونها من الاعتباريات التي لا ثبوت لها في الأعيان، وإلا لقامت بمحل قابل ولزم التسلسل فيما له ترتب ووجود بالفعل، وهو باطل اتفاقاً . وأجيب بأن الموجود لا في موضوع رسم للجوهر لاحد، وصدق الجنس على الفصل عرضي لا يفتقر إلى فعل آخر، وليس الفصل هي القابلية، بل القابل . أعني الأمر الذي من شأنه القبول، وكونه في الوجود نفس ذات الجسم غير قادح كما في سائر الفصول. الظاهر أن التعريف المذكور رسم بالخاصة المركبة إذ على تقدير جنسية الجوهر فالقابل للأبعاد أعم منه من وجه ولا كذلك حال الفصل، ولهذا اتفقوا على أن المركب من أمرين بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه ماهية اعتبارية. وأيضاً تحصل حقيقة الجسم بالأبعاد المفروضة غير معقول. وأما التمسك بأن تركب الجسم إنما هو من الهيولي والصورة لا من الجوهر، وقابل الأبعاد ليكون التعريف بهما حداً فضعيف لما عرفت من الفرق بين الأجزاء الخارجية والأجزاء العقلية التي هي الذاتيات .(1) الحد منتهى الشيء ويطلق على السطح أو الخط او النقطة التي تفصل بين منطقتين متجاورتين أو على النقطة التي تفصل بين زمانين تقول : حدود الدولة وحدود الأزمنة وللحد بحسب هذا التعريف معنى مجازي وهو دلالته على النقطة التي ينتهي عندها إمكان الفعل، والرسم عند المنطقيين مقابل للحد وهو قسمان رسم تام ورسم ناقص، فالتام ما يتركب من الجنس القريب والخاصة كتعريف الانسان بالحيوان الضاحك والناقص ما يكون بالخاصة وحدها او بها وبالجنس البعيد كتعريف الإنسان بالضاحك او بالجسم الضاحك والرسم عند الاصوليين أخص من الحد، لأنه قسم منه ، وعند الصوفية هو العادة والخلق وصفاته لأن الرسوم هي الآثار وكل ما سوى الله تعالى آثار ناشئة عن أفعاله .
He said: And their statement is hesitant) concerning whether this definition is a proper definition (hadd) or a description (rasm) (1). The Imam invalidated it being a hadd, because substance (jawhar) is not a genus for it, since it is interpreted as 'existing not in a subject' (al-mawjud la fi mawdu'), and existence (wujud) is superadded, rather it is from the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya), and it is not in a privative subject. And because if it were a genus, then the differentiation of substances would be by differentiae (fusul), which are either substances, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), or accidents, in which case the substance would be constituted by an accident. And receptivity (qabiliyya) and what is in its meaning cannot be a differentia (fasl) because they are from the considerations (i'tibariyyat) that have no stability in external entities; otherwise, they would inhere in a receptive substrate and lead to infinite regress in what has order and actual existence, which is invalid by consensus. And it was answered: 'existing not in a subject' is a rasm for substance with no genus (la hadd?), and the applicability of the genus to the differentia is accidental, not requiring another act. And the differentia is not receptivity (qabiliyya), but rather the receptive thing (qabil) – that is, the thing that has the disposition to receive. And its being in existence the very essence of the body does not harm, as in other differentiae. The apparent is that the mentioned definition is a description by the compound property (rasm bi al-khassa al-murakkaba), for on the assumption of substantiality as a genus, 'what receives dimensions' is more general than it from one aspect, whereas the differentia is not. For this reason, they agreed that what is composed of two things having generality and specificity from one aspect is an conceptual essence (mahiyya i'tibariyya). Also, the attainment of the reality of body by supposed dimensions is unintelligible. As for the insistence that the composition of body is from hyle and form, not from substance, and that 'receiver of dimensions' would then be a hadd through them, it is weak, given what you know about the difference between external parts and intellectual parts which are the essential properties (dhatiyyat). (1) Al-hadd is the end of a thing, and it is applied to the surface, or line, or point that separates two adjacent regions, or to the point that separates two times. You say: the limits (hudud) of a state and the limits of times. And al-hadd according to this definition has a metaphorical meaning, which is its indication of the point at which the possibility of action ends. And al-rasm according to the logicians is opposed to al-hadd, and it is of two types: complete rasm (rasm tam) and incomplete rasm (rasm naqis). The complete is what is composed of the proximate genus (jins qarib) and the property (khassa), like the definition of man as 'laughing animal'. The incomplete is what is by the property alone or by it and the distant genus, like the definition of man as 'the laugher' or as 'the laughing body'. And al-rasm according to the usuliyyun is more specific than al-hadd, because it is a part of it. And according to the Sufis, it is habit (ada) and character (khuluq) and its attributes, because the drawings (rusum) are the effects (athar), and everything other than God are effects arising from His actions.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of whether the definition of body as 'what receives three dimensions' is a proper definition (hadd) or merely a description (rasm). It begins by noting that there is hesitation on this point. The Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argues that it cannot be a hadd for several reasons: First, 'substance' is not a genus for body because it is defined as 'existing not in a subject,' and existence is superadded, belonging to secondary intelligibles. Second, if substance were a genus, then differentiae (fusul) would be needed to distinguish substances; these differentiae would either be substances (leading to infinite regress) or accidents (making substance constituted by accident). Third, 'receptivity' is an i'tibari (considerational) attribute without concrete existence, and if taken as a differentia, it would require a receptive substrate, leading to regress. The response argues that 'existing not in a subject' is a rasm for substance with no genus (or 'no hadd'?), and the applicability of the genus to the differentia is accidental. The differentia is not 'receptivity' but 'the receptive thing' (qabil), which is the thing disposed to receive, and its being the essence of the body does not invalidate it, as with other differentiae. The passage concludes that the definition is a rasm bi al-khassa al-murakkaba (description by compound property) because on the assumption that substance is a genus, 'what receives dimensions' is more general than substance in one aspect, unlike a true differentia. They agree that a composite of two things with generality-specificity from one aspect yields an conceptual essence (mahiyya i'tibariyya). The passage also rejects the alternative that body is composed of hyle and form, and therefore the definition could be a hadd, because it confuses external and intellectual parts. The footnote provides definitions of hadd and rasm from logical terminology, clarifying the distinction and types.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
فصل (fasl)
قابلية (qabiliyya)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
ماهية اعتبارية (mahiyya i'tibariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Receptivity is a real attribute that can serve as a differentia.
Correction: Al-Razi explains that receptivity is a mental consideration (i'tibari), not a real attribute, so it cannot be a differentia.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Razi argue that 'substance' cannot be a genus for body in a proper definition?
A) Because substance is an accident.
B) Because existence is superadded and 'substance' is a secondary intelligible.
C) Because body is not a substance.
D) Because the definition is a rasm, not a hadd.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that the definition of body cannot be a proper definition because its components do not satisfy logical requirements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi systematically dismantles a definition by showing hidden assumptions. Are there beliefs or definitions you hold that you have never examined for logical consistency? What would it mean to hold yourself to the same standard of scrutiny as al-Razi applies here?
?
The argument relies on the impossibility of infinite regress. Can you identify an area of your own thinking—perhaps in your understanding of causation or morality—where you are implicitly assuming an infinite chain? How would you resolve it if you found one?
?
Al-Razi rejects 'receptivity' as a mere mental consideration. In your daily life, do you often treat mental constructs (like categories or labels) as if they were real, objective facts? What would change if you consistently distinguished between mental considerations and external realities?

Responses and Conclusion: The Definition as Rasm
Responses to al-Razi and conclusion that the definition is a rasm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars respond to al-Razi's critique and conclude that the definition is a description (rasm).

Core Idea
The definition is a rasm by compound property, not a proper hadd.

After al-Razi's powerful critique, the passage presents responses that salvage the definition. The response reinterprets the differentia and argues that the definition is actually a 'rasm bi al-khassa al-murakkaba' (description by compound property). Let's unpack these responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the response to the genus objection
The response argues that 'existing not in a subject' is a rasm for substance without a genus, and the applicability of genus to differentia is accidental.
2
Read the response to the differentia objection
The differentia is not 'receptivity' but 'the receptive thing', which is a real attribute.
3
Read the conclusion that it is a rasm
The definition is a rasm by compound property because 'what receives dimensions' is more general than substance in one aspect.
Also, the alternative using hyle and matter is rejected.

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of whether the definition of body as 'what receives three dimensions' is a proper definition (hadd) or merely a description (rasm). What is the final conclusion about the definition of body according to the passage?
A) It is a proper definition (hadd)

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that the definition of body is only a 'rasm' and not a proper 'hadd' affect your confidence in knowing what a body truly is? Are you comfortable with the idea that some things can only be described by their properties, not by their essence?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'qabil' (the receptive thing) and 'qabiliyya' (receptivity). In your own life, do you focus more on your potential (qabiliyya) or on actively being the one who receives (qabil)? How does that distinction shape your actions?
?
The author rejects the alternative view that body is composed of hyle and form. Reflect: Do you ever cling to explanations that are familiar but ultimately weak because they seem more 'scientific'? What would it take for you to accept a humbler description (rasm) over a seemingly comprehensive but flawed account?

Clarification of Logical Terms: Hadd and Rasm
Explanation of the logical terms hadd and rasm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical meanings of 'hadd' (definition) and 'rasm' (description) in logic, including their types.

Core Idea
Hadd is a definition by essential parts; rasm is by accident or property.

The footnote provides a concise explanation of the logical terms 'hadd' and 'rasm', which are central to the debate. Understanding these terms is essential for grasping the arguments. Let's examine the definitions and types.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of hadd (limit/definition)
The footnote gives the literal meaning of hadd as 'limit' and also its technical use in logic as proper definition.
2
Read the definition of rasm
Rasm is opposed to hadd and has two types: complete (with genus and property) and incomplete (with property alone or with distant genus).
3
Note additional usages
The footnote also mentions how usuliyyun and Sufis use the term rasm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And because if it were a genus, then the differentiation of substances would be by differentiae (fusul), which are either substances, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), or accidents, in which case the substance would be constituted by an accident. And receptivity (qabiliyya) and what is in its meaning cannot be a differentia (fasl) because they are from the considerations (i'tibariyyat) that have no stability in external entities; otherwise, they would inhere in a receptive substrate and lead to infinite regress in what has order and actual existence, which is invalid by consensus. And it was answered: 'existing not in a subject' is a rasm for substance with no genus (la hadd?), and the applicability of the genus to the differentia is accidental, not requiring another act. And the differentia is not receptivity (qabiliyya), but rather the receptive thing (qabil) – that is, the thing that has the disposition to receive. And its being in existence the very essence of the body does not harm, as in other differentiae. The apparent is that the mentioned definition is a description by the compound property (rasm bi al-khassa al-murakkaba), for on the assumption of substantiality as a genus, 'what receives dimensions' is more general than it from one aspect, whereas the differentia is not. For this reason, they agreed that what is composed of two things having generality and specificity from one aspect is an conceptual essence (mahiyya i'tibariyya). Also, the attainment of the reality of body by supposed dimensions is unintelligible. As for the insistence that the composition of body is from hyle and form, not from substance, and that 'receiver of dimensions' would then be a hadd through them, it is weak, given what you know about the difference between external parts and intellectual parts which are the essential properties (dhatiyyat). (1) Al-hadd is the end of a thing, and it is applied to the surface, or line, or point that separates two adjacent regions, or to the point that separates two times. You say: the limits (hudud) of a state and the limits of times. And al-hadd according to this definition has a metaphorical meaning, which is its indication of the point at which the possibility of action ends. And al-rasm according to the logicians is opposed to al-hadd, and it is of two types: complete rasm (rasm tam) and incomplete rasm (rasm naqis). The complete is what is composed of the proximate genus (jins qarib) and the property (khassa), like the definition of man as 'laughing animal'. The incomplete is what is by the property alone or by it and the distant genus, like the definition of man as 'the laugher' or as 'the laughing body'. And al-rasm according to the usuliyyun is more specific than al-hadd, because it is a part of it. And according to the Sufis, it is habit (ada) and character (khuluq) and its attributes, because the drawings (rusum) are the effects (athar), and everything other than God are effects arising from His actions.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of whether the definition of body as 'what receives three dimensions' is a proper definition (hadd) or merely a description (rasm). It begins by noting that there is hesitation on this point. The Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argues that it cannot be a hadd for several reasons: First, 'substance' is not a genus for body because it is defined as 'existing not in a subject,' and existence is superadded, belonging to secondary intelligibles. Second, if substance were a genus, then differentiae (fusul) would be needed to distinguish substances; these differentiae would either be substances (leading to infinite regress) or accidents (making substance constituted by accident). Third, 'receptivity' is an i'tibari (considerational) attribute without concrete existence, and if taken as a differentia, it would require a receptive substrate, leading to regress. The response argues that 'existing not in a subject' is a rasm for substance with no genus (or 'no hadd'?), and the applicability of the genus to the differentia is accidental. The differentia is not 'receptivity' but 'the receptive thing' (qabil), which is the thing disposed to receive, and its being the essence of the body does not invalidate it, as with other differentiae. The passage concludes that the definition is a rasm bi al-khassa al-murakkaba (description by compound property) because on the assumption that substance is a genus, 'what receives dimensions' is more general than substance in one aspect, unlike a true differentia. They agree that a composite of two things with generality-specificity from one aspect yields an conceptual essence (mahiyya i'tibariyya). The passage also rejects the alternative that body is composed of hyle and form, and therefore the definition could be a hadd, because it confuses external and intellectual parts. The footnote provides definitions of hadd and rasm from logical terminology, clarifying the distinction and types.

Key Terms
حد (hadd)
رسم (rasm)
رسم تام (rasm tam)
رسم ناقص (rasm naqis)
خاصة (khassa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rasm is always an incomplete or inferior definition.
Correction: Rasm has a complete type that combines proximate genus and property, which is a valid definition in logic.

Checkpoint
What is a complete rasm (rasm tam) composed of?
A) Distant genus and property
B) Proximate genus and property
C) Only property
D) Property and differentia

Key Takeaway
Hadd is a definition by essential parts; rasm is a description using properties, with complete and incomplete variants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you accept incomplete descriptions (rasm naqis) of yourself or your faith? What would it cost you to seek the essential definition (hadd) instead?
?
When you describe your relationship with Allah, do you rely more on your actions (rasm) or on the essential reality of your servitude (hadd)? How can you move from incomplete to complete understanding?
?
This lesson shows that even a complete description (rasm tam) still uses property, not essence. Where might you be confusing God's attributes (sifat) with His essence (dhat)? How does this affect your worship?

Why the Definition of Body is a Rasam, Not a Hadd
Argues that the definition of jism is a rasam with a composed property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of jism as 'jawhar qabil li al-ab'ad' is considered a rasam with a composed property rather than a complete definition (hadd).

Core Idea
The definition of body is a description, not a true essence-defining definition.

In this lesson, we examine a key logical argument about how to define 'jism' (body). The passage explains that the classic definition 'jawhar qabil li al-ab'ad' is not a full hadd (definition by genus and differentia) but a rasam (description) with a composed property. We'll see why this matters for understanding the nature of physical bodies in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Let the author's own wording set the stage.
Notice the technical terms: رسم بالخاصة المركبة, ماهية اعتبارية, etc.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that the definition is a rasam bi al-khassa al-murakkaba. Understand what that means in the context of logical definitions.
A rasam is a descriptive definition, not a true essence-defining hadd.
3
Follow the argument
Work through why it cannot be a hadd: if jawhar were a genus, 'qabil li al-ab'ad' would be more general in some ways, so it can't serve as a differentia. Hence the compound is an intentional quiddity.
The reasoning hinges on the relationship between jawhar and qabil li al-ab'ad.
4
Consider the alternative
The author rejects the idea that jism is composed of matter and form (hyle and form) to claim the definition is a hadd. Understand why this alternative is weak.
The distinction between external and mental parts is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظاهر أن التعريف المذكور رسم بالخاصة المركبة إذ على تقدير جنسية الجوهر فالقابل للأبعاد أعم منه من وجه ولا كذلك حال الفصل، ولهذا اتفقوا على أن المركب من أمرين بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه ماهية اعتبارية. وأيضاً تحصل حقيقة الجسم بالأبعاد المفروضة غير معقول. وأما التمسك بأن تركب الجسم إنما هو من الهيولي والصورة لا من الجوهر، وقابل الأبعاد ليكون التعريف بهما حداً فضعيف لما عرفت من الفرق بين الأجزاء الخارجية والأجزاء العقلية التي هي الذاتيات .
It is apparent that the mentioned definition is a rasam with a composed property, because on the assumption of jawhar being a jins, al-qabil li al-ab'ad is more general than it in some respects, and such is not the case for the fasl. Therefore they agreed that the compound of two things that have general and specific in some respects is an intentional quiddity. Also, attaining the reality of jism by supposed dimensions is unintelligible. As for the argument that the composition of jism is from hyle and form, not from jawhar and qabil al-ab'ad, so that the definition by these would be a hadd, it is weak because of the known difference between external parts and mental parts which are essences.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage argues that the definition of body (jism) as 'jawhar qabil li al-ab'ad' is a rasam (description) with a composed property, meaning it combines a genus-like element and a property. It explains that if jawhar were a genus, then 'qabil li al-ab'ad' would be more general in some ways, so it cannot be a differentia. Therefore the compound is an intentional quiddity (mahiyya i'tibariyya). Then it says that the reality of body cannot be attained by supposed dimensions. Finally, it rejects the alternative that body is composed of matter and form, saying the distinction between external and mental parts shows the definition is not a hadd.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of jism as 'jawhar qabil li al-ab'ad' is a complete hadd (definition by genus and differentia).
Correction: The passage shows it is only a rasam because the predicate 'qabil li al-ab'ad' does not have the exclusivity of a true differentia; it is more general in some respects.

Checkpoint

A) Hadd
B) Rasam with composed property
C) Rasam with simple property
D) None

Key Takeaway
The definition of jism as 'jawhar qabil li al-ab'ad' is a rasam bi al-khassa al-murakkaba, not a hadd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that even a precise definition like 'jawhar qabil li al-ab'ad' is an intentional quiddity—a mental construct. Are you willing to examine other things you take for granted (your identity, your beliefs) and ask if they too are mere descriptions rather than essences? What would it cost you to admit that?
?
The author rejects the idea that jism is composed of matter and form as a weak argument. How often do you cling to weak arguments to protect your worldview? What would it take for you to let go of a comfortable but unsound explanation? Can you name one such belief in your life right now?

The Term 'Substance': Multiple Meanings in Islamic Philosophy and Theology
Al-Maqasid's marginal note defines 'substance' in philosophical and theological contexts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct ways the term 'substance' (جوهر) is defined by philosophers and by mutakallimun, and why this difference matters.

Core Idea
'Substance' means something different for philosophers than for theologians—one is broad, the other is atomic.

This lesson unpacks the term 'جوهر' (substance) as it appears in classical Islamic thought. You'll see how philosophers like Ibn Sina defined it in multiple senses related to self-subsistence, while the mutakallimun narrowed it to the indivisible atom. Understanding this distinction is crucial for reading later theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Pay attention to the different contexts: first the philosophers, then Ibn Sina, then the mutakallimun.
The passage uses the verb 'يطلق' to indicate that 'substance' is used in multiple senses.
2
Identify the philosophers' four definitions
The passage lists four distinct meanings among philosophers. List them: (1) self-subsistent existent, (2) essence receiving opposite attributes, (3) quiddity not in a subject, (4) existent independent of a substrate.
Note that these definitions are not mutually exclusive—they emphasize different aspects.
3
Analyze Ibn Sina's concise definition
Ibn Sina defines substance as 'everything whose essence's existence is not in a subject.' Understand the key phrase 'not in a subject' and its implications for independence.
The text references al-Najat; you may look it up for further context.
4
Understand the mutakallimun's definition
The mutakallimun define substance as the indivisible atom, and they call the divisible a 'body'. This is a major departure from the philosophical definition.
This explains why theologians avoid calling God a substance—He is not composed of atoms.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why such terminological precision matters. Different definitions lead to different theological conclusions, especially regarding God's nature.
Compare with previous lessons on accidents and bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يطلق الجوهر عند الفلاسفة على معان منها الموجود القائم بنفسه حادثاً كان أو قديماً ويقابله العرض، ومنها الذات القابلة لتوارد الصفات المتضادة عليها ومنه الماهية التي إذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في موضوع ومنها الموجود الغنى عن محل يحل فيه. قال ابن سينا الجوهر هو كل ما وجود ذاته ليس في موضوع أي في محل قريب قد قام بنفسه دونه لا بتقويمه (راجع النجاة ص ١٢٦). والجوهرية : مذهب من يقول بوجود الجوهر، أعني الشيء القائم بنفسه وهي ضد الظواهرية. والجوهري هو المنسوب إلى الجوهر أو المقوم له كما في قولنا الصورة الجوهرية. والجوهر عند المتكلمين : هو الجوهر الفرد المتميز الذي لا ينقسم، أما المنقسم فيسمونه جسماً لا جوهراً ولهذا السبب يمتنعون عن إطلاق اسم الجوهر على المبدأ الأول.
'Substance' is used by the philosophers in several senses: among them, the existent that subsists by itself, whether originated or eternal, opposed to accident; and among them, the essence that admits the succession of opposite attributes; and among them, the quiddity which, when existing in individuals, is not in a subject; and among them, the existent that is free from need of a substrate in which it inheres. Ibn Sina said: Substance is everything whose essence's existence is not in a subject, i.e., in a proximate substrate that has subsisted by itself without it, not by its constitution (refer to al-Najat, p. 126). And substantialism is the doctrine of those who affirm the existence of substance, i.e., the thing subsisting by itself, and it is opposed to phenomenalism. And 'substantial' is attributed to substance or what constitutes it, as in our saying 'substantial form'. And substance according to the mutakallimun is the individual atom that is distinguished and does not divide; as for the divisible, they call it a body, not a substance, and for this reason they refrain from applying the name 'substance' to the First Principle.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This marginal note provides definitions of the term 'جوهر' (substance). For philosophers, it has multiple meanings: self-subsistent being (opposed to accident), the essence that receives opposite attributes, the quiddity not in a subject, and the existent independent of a substrate. Ibn Sina is quoted defining it as that which exists not in a subject, citing his work al-Najat. The note also defines 'الجوهرية' (substantialism) as the belief in substances, opposed to phenomenalism (الظواهرية). For the mutakallimun (theologians), 'جوهر' specifically means the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) which does not divide; that which divides is called a body (جسم), not a substance. Thus they avoid calling God a substance, as He is not composed of atoms.

ماهية
جوهر فرد
جسم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think 'substance' has one meaning across Islamic thought.
Correction: Actually, philosophers used it in multiple senses (self-subsistent, essence, quiddity, etc.) while mutakallimun restricted it to the indivisible atom, and thus refrained from calling God a substance.

Checkpoint

B) Atom
C) Body
D) Accident

Key Takeaway
The term 'substance' (جوهر) has different technical meanings: for philosophers it is a self-subsistent being, while for mutakallimun it is an indivisible atom, leading to different theological applications.

Refutation of the Two Arguments for Substantial Forms
Refutes the two arguments for substantial forms by proposing alternative explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation denies that the differentiator between bodies must be a substance indwelling in matter, and the two alternative possibilities considered.

Core Idea
The differentiator need not be a substance in matter; it could be an accident or a separate substance.

This passage builds on the preceding arguments for substantial forms by conceding several points but then denying the crucial conclusion. The author shows that even if bodies differ and have effects, the differentiator does not have to be a substance dwelling in matter. Two alternative possibilities are examined and pushed back, revealing the flexibility of the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Concede the premises
Read the initial concession: the refutation accepts that bodies differ in reality, effects emanate from them, and hyle and corporeal forms are uniform.
These concessions narrow the point of contention.
2
Identify the main denial
The refutation denies the necessity that the differentiator be a substance indwelling in the hyle to be a form. This is the core of the refutation.
3
Examine the first alternative
Consider why the differentiator could be an accident subsisting in one part, not in the whole body. Note the pushback: an accident is posterior and cannot be constitutive.
This shows the limits of the accident possibility.
4
Examine the second alternative
Consider why the differentiator could be a separate substance not indwelling in matter. The pushback: then it is not a form and no indwelling relation is needed.
This completes the refutation by exhausting alternatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد على هذا التقريرين بعد تسليم اختلاف الأجسام بالحقيقة، وكون الآثار صادرة عنها، وكون هيوليتها متفقة للحقيقة وكذا صورها الجسمية. المادة إنا لا نسلّم لزوم كون ما به الاختلاف جوهراً حالاً في الهيولي ليكون صورة، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون عرضاً قائماً بأحد جزئيه لا بالجسم نفسه، ليدفع بأن العرض الحال في الجسم متقوم به متأخر عنه، فلا يكون مقوماً له متقدماً عليه، أو يكون جوهراً غير حال في مادته، فلا يكون صورة، ولا يكون الاحتياج فيما بينه وبين الجزئين الآخرين على وجه آخر غير الحلول،
And it is replied to these two reports, after conceding the difference of bodies in reality, and that effects emanate from them, and that their hyle is uniform in reality, as well as their corporeal forms. The matter: We do not concede the necessity that that by which there is difference be a substance indwelling in the hyle to be a form. Why is it not permissible that it be an accident subsisting in one of its two parts, not in the body itself? So it is pushed by saying that the accident indwelling in the body is constituted by it and posterior to it, so it cannot be constitutive of it and prior to it. Or that it be a substance not indwelling in its matter, so it is not a form, and there is no need between it and the other two parts in another manner besides indwelling.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (الرد) of the two preceding arguments for substantial forms. The author begins by conceding certain premises: that bodies differ in reality, that effects emanate from them, and that their hyle and corporeal forms are uniform. Despite these concessions, the author denies the conclusion that the differentiator must be a substance (جوهر) indwelling in the hyle to be a form. The refutation then considers two alternative possibilities:

First, the differentiator could be an accident (عرض) that subsists in one of the two parts (presumably matter or form) rather than in the whole body itself. However, this is pushed back by arguing that an accident indwelling in the body is constituted by the body and posterior to it, so it cannot serve as something constitutive and prior. Second, the differentiator could be a separate substance (جوهر غير حال في المادة) that does not indwell in the matter, in which case it would not be a form, and there would be no need for it to have a relation of indwelling with the other two parts (matter and corporeal form). Thus the refutation shows that the two arguments do not necessarily prove a substantial form inhering in matter; the differentiator could be something else.

Key Terms
تقرير
جوهر
عرض
هيولي
صورة جسمية
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation rejects any differentiation between bodies.
Correction: The refutation only rejects the necessity of a substantial form in matter; it does not deny differentiation itself.

Checkpoint
What does the refutation concede before rejecting the arguments?
A) The existence of substantial forms.
B) The difference of bodies in reality, the emanation of effects, and the uniformity of hyle and corporeal forms.
C) The necessity of a substance in matter.
D) That accidents cannot be differentiators.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that the differentiator between bodies need not be a substance inhering in matter; it could be an accident or a separate substance, thus undermining the arguments for substantial forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a disagreement with someone, do you automatically assume that the cause is a fundamental, unchangeable difference (like a 'substance'), or are you open to the possibility that it could be something accidental and changeable? How does this passage challenge you to reconsider a current conflict in your life?
?
The author concedes many premises but still questions the conclusion. In your own beliefs, are there points where you accept premises out of habit but have never critically examined the conclusion? What would it cost you to question something you have always assumed is necessary?
?
If the differentiator between bodies need not be an inhering substance, then what seems inevitable in your life might actually be due to factors you have overlooked. What is one area where you are assuming a single cause, and how would it change your actions if that cause turned out to be merely accidental?

Classification of Motion in Ancient Philosophy
Al-Taftazani classifies motion into three types with a definition from Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of motion according to ancient philosophers and how mutakallimun restrict the term 'motion' to locative change.

Core Idea
Motion is not just movement; it includes change in quantity, quality, and location.

The ancients had a broad concept of motion that goes beyond simple locomotion. This lesson introduces their classification as transmitted by al-Taftazani, starting with a definition from Ibn Sina. Understanding these distinctions helps clarify why Islamic theologians (mutakallimun) restrict 'motion' to change of place.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of motion
Begin with Ibn Sina's definition: motion is gradual change of a settled state toward something, with arrival being potential. Understand the key terms: 'settled state' and 'gradual'.
The definition emphasizes that motion is a process, not an instantaneous change.
2
Identify the three types
The passage lists three categories: motion in quantity, in quality, and in 'where' (location). Note the examples: growth/withering for quantity, heating/cooling for quality, and change of place for location.
Each type corresponds to a different category of accident (quantity, quality, location).
3
Explore the subcategory of psychic motion
Under qualitative motion, there is a special case: psychic motion, which is the soul's movement among intelligibles (thinking) or sensibles (imagination). Recognize that this extends motion to mental processes.
4
Understand the mutakallimun's usage
The passage concludes that when theologians (mutakallimun) use 'motion' without qualification, they mean only locative motion. This is important for later discussions in kalam.
This restriction avoids ambiguity in arguments about atoms and accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د - وتقال الحركة على تبدل حالة قارة في الجسم يسيراً يسيراً على سبيل اتجاه نحو شيء، والوصول بها إليه هو بالقوة لا بالفعل ابن سينا. النجاة ص ١٦٩) وللحركة عند القدماء أيضاً أقسام مختلفة وهي : ۱ - الحركة في الكم وهي انتقال الجسم من كمية الى أخرى كالنمو والذبول . ۲ - الحركة في الكيف وهي انتقال الجسم من كيفية الى أخرى كتسخن الماء وتبرده، وتسمى استحالة، والحركة الكيفية النفسانية هي حركة النفس في المعقولات، وتسمى فكراً أو حركتها في المحسومات وتسمى تخيلاً . ٣ - الحركة في الأين، وهي حركة الجسم من مكان الى آخر وتسمى نقلة والمتكلمون اذا اطلقوا الحركة أرادوا بها الحركة الأينية فقط .
D - Motion (al-haraka) is said of the gradual change of a settled state (hāla qārra) in a body little by little in the direction of something, and arrival at it is potential (bil-quwwa) not actual (bil-fi‘l) (Ibn Sina, al-Najat, p. 169). According to the ancients (al-qudamā’), motion also has different divisions, which are: 1- Motion in quantity (al-kamm): it is the transfer of a body from one quantity to another, like growth and withering. 2- Motion in quality (al-kayf): it is the transfer of a body from one quality to another, like water heating up and cooling down, and it is called alteration (istihāla). Psychic qualitative motion (al-haraka al-kayfiyya al-nafsāniyya) is the movement of the soul among intelligibles, called thinking (fikr), or its movement among sensibles, called imagination (takhayyul). 3- Motion in 'where' (al-ayn): it is the movement of a body from one place to another, and it is called translocation (naqla). The mutakallimun, when they use the term 'motion' absolutely, mean only locative motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya).
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage serves as a classification of motion according to the ancient philosophers. First, it provides a definition of motion from Ibn Sina: motion is the gradual change of a settled state in a body, directed toward something, with arrival being potential. Then it divides motion into three categories. The first is quantitative motion (al-haraka fi al-kamm), which involves change in size, like growth and shrinkage. The second is qualitative motion (al-haraka fi al-kayf), involving change in quality, such as heating and cooling, called alteration. A subcategory is psychic qualitative motion, which is the soul's movement among intelligibles (thinking) or among sensibles (imagination). The third is locative motion (al-haraka fi al-ayn), which is movement from place to place, called translocation. The passage concludes with a note on terminology: when the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) use 'motion' without qualification, they intend only locative motion, as this is the relevant type for discussions of bodies and accidents in kalam.

Key Terms
الحركة (motion)
الكم (quantity)
الكيف (quality)
الأين (where/location)
الاستحالة (alteration)
التخيل (imagination)
الفكر (thinking)
النقلة (translocation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion in kalam includes all types of change (quantitative, qualitative, and locative).
Correction: The text explicitly states that mutakallimun, when using the term 'motion' absolutely, mean only locative motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the mutakallimun mean when they say 'motion' without qualification?
A) Any change in a body
B) Locative motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya) only
C) Quantitative and qualitative motion
D) Psychic motion

Key Takeaway
The ancients recognized three types of motion—quantitative, qualitative, and locative—but mutakallimun use 'motion' exclusively for locative change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul is constantly in motion: thinking, imagining, desiring. In the past hour, what have your thoughts (فكر) and imaginations (تخيل) been moving toward? Are they directed toward Allah or away from Him? What will you do to redirect them starting today?
?
The mutakallimun focused only on locative motion, but your life is filled with all three: you grow, your character changes, and you move about. Which of these types of change are you most negligent in holding yourself accountable for? How can you check your growth in faith and character today?
?
Ibn Sina says motion's arrival is potential, not actual. In your journey to meet Allah, you are always in a state of motion—never fully arrived. Does this truth humble you or make you complacent? What is one concrete step you will take today to turn your potential closeness into actual obedience?

Definition of Direction as Endpoint
Al-Taftazani defines direction as the extremity and terminus of extension and indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's definition of direction (jiha) as the endpoint of extension and its distinction from space (hayyiz).

Core Idea
Direction is the endpoint of motion and indication, not a container.

After establishing the necessity of direction, al-Taftazani now provides its precise definition. He distinguishes direction from the path of motion and from space, clarifying that direction is the terminus of extension and the goal of indication. This lesson unpacks that definition and the accompanying philosophical explanation from Ibn Sina.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about every detail. Notice that it contains a main statement followed by a footnote with further explanation.
The footnote marked (3) includes Ibn Sina's definition and a contrast with space.
2
Identify the core definition
Find where al-Taftazani defines direction as 'the extremity of every extension and the terminus of every indication'. Write down this definition in your own words.
Note the two key terms: 'taraf' (extremity) and 'muntaha' (terminus).
3
Contrast direction with space
Read the final part of the passage where direction is contrasted with space (hayyiz). Understand that space is a container, while direction is the endpoint towards which motion aims.
This distinction is crucial for later arguments about God's relation to direction.
4
Reflect on the two opinions
The passage mentions 'all the determiners of directions, the true one is the second'. Consider what the two opinions might be about the location of direction (on the surface or beyond it).
Al-Taftazani sides with the opinion that direction is at the end of the extension, not on the surface.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي الكلام في أن طرف كل امتداد، ومنتهى كل إشارة جهة (۳) حتى لا يكون إلا على سطح الامتدادات، جميع محدد الجهات الحق هو الثاني. (۳) الجهة في الأصل هي الجانب والناحية والموضع الذي تتوجه إليه وتقصد قال ابن سينا : إننا نعني بالجهة شيئاً إليه مأخذ حركة أو إشارة (جامع البدائع ١٥٤) والجهة والحيز متلازمان في الوجود، لأن كلا منهما مقصد للمتحرك الأين إلا أن الحيز مقصد للمتحرك بالحصول فيه، والجهة مصدر له بالوصول إليها والقرب منها فالجهة منتهى الحركة لا ماء
There remains the discussion: The extremity (taraf) of every extension (imtidad) and the terminus (muntaha) of every indication (ishara) is a direction (jiha) (3); such that it is only on the surface of extensions; all the determiners of directions, the true one is the second. (3) The direction originally is the side (janib), the quarter (nahiya), and the place (mawdi‘) towards which one turns and intends. Ibn Sina said: 'We mean by direction something towards which there is the domain of motion or indication' (Jami‘ al-Bada’i‘ 154). Direction and space (hayyiz) are concomitant in existence, because each of them is a goal for the moving object in terms of 'where' (ayn), except that space is a goal for the moving object in terms of being contained in it, while direction is a source for it by arriving at it and being near it. So direction is the end of motion, not water (?)
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a definition of direction (jiha) after the previous proof. The author begins with 'There remains the discussion' (baqiya al-kalam), indicating this is a separate point. He defines direction as the extremity (taraf) of every extension (imtidad) and the terminus (muntaha) of every indication (ishara). This means direction is not the path but the endpoint of motion and spatial reference. He then says 'such that it is only on the surface of extensions' and mentions a disagreement: 'all the determiners of directions, the true one is the second.' This refers to two opinions about whether direction is on the surface of the body or at the end of its extensions. He sides with the second opinion that direction is the endpoint beyond the surface. The footnote (3) provides an etymological and philosophical definition from Ibn Sina: direction is that towards which motion or indication is made. It contrasts direction with space (hayyiz): space is the goal of containment, while direction is the goal of arrival and proximity. Thus direction is the end of motion, not a containing space.

Key Terms
الجهة (direction)
الامتداد (extension)
الإشارة (indication)
الطرف (extremity)
المنتهى (terminus)
الحيز (space)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That direction is the same as space (hayyiz).
Correction: The text clarifies that direction is the endpoint of motion, not a container; space contains the moving object, while direction is what it moves towards.

Checkpoint
What does al-Taftazani identify as the 'extremity of every extension and terminus of every indication'?
A) Space (hayyiz)
B) Direction (jiha)
C) Motion (haraka)
D) Body (jism)

Key Takeaway
Direction is the endpoint of motion and indication, distinct from space which is a container.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where are you heading in life right now? Is your goal a true endpoint you strive for, or just a comfortable space you've settled into? How does this lesson's distinction between direction and space force you to reconsider the difference between the two in your own spiritual journey?
?

?
The passage teaches that direction is the endpoint of every indication. Think of the things you indicate—your words, your gestures, your time. What is the ultimate endpoint they are pointing to? Is it pleasing Allah, or is it something else? Be honest: what would a close friend say your life's direction actually is?

Origination of Bodies: The Milliyun View vs. Philosophers
Matn statement on body origination with philosophical refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim of the milliyun that bodies are originated in both essence and attributes, and how later and earlier philosophers differed on the eternity of matter.

Core Idea
Bodies are fully originated; philosophers fragment eternity.

This passage from the matn of al-Maqasid presents the central theological stance of the people of religions (milliyun) that all bodies are originated in their very essence and attributes. It then contrasts this with the views of later and earlier philosophers, who proposed various forms of eternal matter. Understanding these positions is crucial for grasping the Islamic theological argument against philosophical doctrines of pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the matn passage
Begin by reading the Arabic matn and its literal translation. Notice the claim that bodies are 'muhdatha' in both dhāt (essence) and ṣifāt (attributes).
The term 'milliyun' refers to adherents of divinely revealed religions.
2
Identify the philosophical doctrines
The passage lists two groups of philosophers: later (Aristotelians) and earlier (pre-Socratics). Later philosophers held that celestial bodies and elemental bodies are eternal in certain respects. Earlier philosophers posited an eternal matter with various identifications.
Notice the exceptions for movements and positions among celestial bodies.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: If any part of the universe is eternal, it challenges the doctrine that everything other than God is created. The milliyun's position safeguards divine sovereignty.
This passage sets the stage for arguing that all contingent beings require a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها) الأجسام محدثة بذواتها وصفاتها عند المليين أن الأجسام محدثة بذواتها وصفاتها كما هو رأى المليين خلافاً للمتأخرين الفلاسفة فيهما حيث زعموا أن الفلكيات قديمة، سوى الحركات والأوضاع من الجزئية، والعنصريات قديمة بموادها وصورها الجسمية نوعاً، والنوعية جنساً، وللمتقدمين منهم في الذوات خاصة، حيث زعموا أن هناك مادة قديمة على اختلاف آرائهم في أنها جسم. وهو العناصر الأربعة، أو الأرض أو النار أو الماء أو الهواء، والبواقي تتلطف وتتكشف والسماء من دخان يرتفع منه، أو جوهرة غيرها أو أجسام صغار صلبة كرية أو مختلفة الأشكال أو ليست بجسم، بل نور وظلمة أو نفس وهيولي، أو وحدات تحيزت فصارت نقطاً واجتمعت النقط خطاً والخطوط سطحاً، والسطوح جسماً
He said: Among them (the rulings) is that bodies are originated in their essences and attributes according to the milliyun, that bodies are originated in their essences and attributes, as is the view of the milliyun, contrary to the later philosophers in both, for they claimed that celestial bodies are eternal, except for particular movements and positions, and that elemental bodies are eternal in their matters and corporeal forms as a species, and specific forms as a genus; and for the earlier philosophers among them, regarding essences specifically, for they claimed that there is an eternal matter, differing in their views as to whether it is a body: it is the four elements, or earth, fire, water, air, and the rest become subtle or become dense, and the sky is from smoke rising from it, or a jewel other than them, or small solid spherical bodies of different shapes, or not a body but light and darkness or soul and hyle, or units that became localized and became points, and the points gathered to form a line, and lines a surface, and surfaces a body.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is the matn (original text) that the author is commenting on. It presents the view of the milliyun (people of religions) that bodies are originated in both essence and attributes. It contrasts with two groups of philosophers: later philosophers (like Aristotle and his followers) who held that celestial bodies are eternal except for particular accidents, and elemental bodies are eternal in matter and corporeal form species-wise but generic specific forms; and earlier philosophers who differed on the nature of an eternal matter, listing various opinions.

Key Terms
محدثة
ذوات
صفات
مليون
فلكيات
عنصريات
قديمة
مادة
عناصر
هيولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All philosophers agree that the universe is eternal.
Correction: Philosophers differ widely; later philosophers considered celestial bodies eternal and elemental bodies in some respects, while earlier philosophers posited an eternal matter with different descriptions.

Checkpoint

A) Only celestial bodies
B) Elemental bodies only
C) Celestial bodies and elemental bodies in matter and corporeal form
D) The four elements only

Key Takeaway
The milliyun affirm full origination of bodies, while philosophers propose eternal matter in various forms—understanding these differences is key to Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This passage shows that many philosophers hold different views about the eternity of matter. Do you truly believe that everything, including your own existence, is completely created by Allah from nothing, or do you sometimes assume certain things are 'just there'? How does this belief affect your gratitude and reliance on Allah today?

The milliyun affirm that bodies are originated in both essence and attributes. Does your daily life reflect that you are a created being, or do you act as if you are autonomous? What specific habit can you change to remind yourself of your origination and dependence on Allah?
?
The enumeration of various philosophical opinions highlights confusion without revelation. Are you content with the clarity of Islamic teachings, or do you sometimes feel attracted to intellectual speculation that undermines divine truths? What step can you take to strengthen your conviction in the revealed word?

Understanding Non-Existence and the Privative
Sanusi defines non-existence and privative, distinguishing relative from absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and theological definitions of non-existence (al-adam) and privative (al-adami), and why they are relative rather than absolute.

Core Idea
Non-existence is the relative absence of perfections, not absolute nothingness.

In Islamic theology, precise definitions of terms like 'non-existence' and 'privative' are essential for discussing the creation of the world and divine attributes. This lesson unpacks these foundational concepts as defined by Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on non-existence
Start by reading the definition of al-adam: it is the lack of what a thing’s nature requires. Note that it is relative (idafi), not absolute.
Absolute non-existence would be pure nothingness, but this is not intended here.
2
Examine the logicians' usage
Notice how logicians apply non-existence to negative propositions like 'Zayd is not sitting.' This shows it’s about absence of a non-essential predicate.
3
Understand the privative (al-adami)
Read the definition of al-adami: it applies to terms indicating lack of a required quality (e.g., blindness) and to things destined to perish (e.g., the sky, wealth).
This connects to the transient nature of the world.
4
Reflect on theological relevance
Think about why this distinction matters: the world’s non-existence before creation is relative, not absolute, making it possible for God to bring it into being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدم فقدان الشيء ما تقتضيه طبيعته من الكمالات الثابتة لنوعه وطبيعته وهو عدم إضافي لا عدم مطلق ويطلق عند المنطقيين على وقوع النسبة بين محمول وموضوع ليس من شأنه أن يكون له ذلك المحمول، ولا أن يؤدي انتفاؤه عنه الى نقص في ماهيته كقولنا (ليس زيد جالساً) والعدمي هو المنسوب الى العدم، ويطلق على كل حد يدل على فقدان الشيء لاحدى الصفات التي تقتضيها طبيعته كالعمى للانسان وكل شيء مصيره الى الزوال كالسماء المظلة، والأرض، والمال والجاه والملك فهو عدمي.
Non-existence (al-adam) is the lack by a thing of what its nature requires of perfections established for its type and nature, and it is relative non-existence, not absolute non-existence. It is used by the logicians for the occurrence of a relation between a predicate and a subject that is not such that it should have that predicate, nor does its absence entail a deficiency in its essence, like our saying 'Zayd is not sitting.' And the privative (al-adami) is that which is attributed to non-existence, and it is applied to every term that indicates the lack by a thing of one of the qualities that its nature requires, like blindness for a human, and everything whose fate is to pass away, like the overshadowing sky, the earth, wealth, status, and dominion—it is privative.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: al-adam (non-existence) and al-adami (privative). It begins by defining non-existence as the lack of perfections that a thing's nature requires. This is a relative (idafi) non-existence, not absolute non-existence (i.e., non-existence pure and simple). The logicians use it for a negative proposition where the predicate is not essential to the subject. The privative (al-adami) is what is attributed to non-existence; it refers to terms indicating the lack of a quality that is natural to a thing, and also to anything that is destined to perish, such as the sky, earth, wealth, etc. This definition is important in kalam for discussions about the world's non-existence before creation.

Key Terms
عدم
عدمي
عدم إضافي
عدم مطلق
منطقيون
نسبة
محمول
موضوع
ماهية
كمالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming 'non-existence' means absolute nothingness or pure non-being.
Correction: Non-existence in this context is relative absence of specific perfections, not absolute non-being.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'al-adami' (the privative) apply to?
A) Only to negative propositions.
B) To terms indicating lack of a natural quality and things destined to perish.
C) Only to things that perish.
D) To any attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is relative absence, and privative describes either a lacking attribute or a transient entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the wealth, status, or dominion you hold—perhaps your job, reputation, or possessions. If you truly believed they are privative (adami) and destined to pass away, how would your attachment to them change starting today? What concrete step can you take to loosen your grip on one of them this week?
?
Non-existence here is the lack of what your nature requires. Think of a perfection you are currently lacking—patience, humility, honesty—that your nature as a human being demands. Why have you not pursued it earnestly? What is the cost to your soul of leaving that lack unaddressed?
?
The passage teaches that even the sky and earth are privative, soon to vanish. You live under this sky and walk on this earth daily. How does this truth force you to re-evaluate what you consider 'real' and 'permanent' in your life? What would it mean to invest your heart only in what is not privative?

Definition of Muhaddid al-Jiha: The True Determinant of Direction
Defines muhaddid al-jiha and argues for the encompassing convex as determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of muhaddid al-jiha and why the encompassing sphere's convex is the true determinant of direction.

Core Idea
The encompassing sphere determines direction, not the earth or center.

This lesson dives into a key concept from Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid: what determines directions like 'above' and 'below'? The passage clarifies that the true determinant is the convex surface of the encompassing sphere, not the earth or its center. Understanding this is crucial for later discussions on the nature of the universe in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without jumping to analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms: محدد الجهة, فاعل, قابل, محيط, مركز.
2
Understand the core definition
The passage opens by stating that 'muhaddid al-jiha' is what determines the position of a direction. This is the central concept. Write it down in your own words.
The definition is: 'ما يتعين به وضع الجهة'.
3
Follow the argument about agent and recipient
The author clarifies that the agent (فاعل) does not have to be a body, and the recipient (قابل) is unique. Then he contrasts the above (a point on the sphere) with the below (from the earth), highlighting that the earth does not determine direction.
The key point: the encompassing sphere determines its center, not vice versa.
4
Identify the true determinant
The passage concludes that the true determinant is the convex of the encompassing sphere, while its concave is below. Some scholars hold the entire encompassing sphere as above by essence.
Pay attention to the phrase: 'ويكون المحدد بالحقيقة هو محدب المحيط'.
5
Address the implicit question
The passage answers an objection: if 'mere direction' means the agent, it doesn't require position; if it means the recipient, the determinant of up/down is not existent. The answer is that by direction we mean what determines it.
This clarifies the scope of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بمحدد الجهة ما يتعين به وضع الجهة قال ثم معنى تحديده الجهات تعيين أوضاعها به وإلا فالفاعل لا يلزم أن يكون جسماً، والقابل ليس إلا واحداً، لأن العلو نقطة من الفلك، والسفل من الأرض، لكن من حيث أنها مركز للمحيط، ومتحددة به المحيط بتعين مركزه ولا عكس. ولهذا لم يكن للأرض دخل في التحديد، وإنما تعين المحيط بالكل، لأن المحاط قد يمتد الإشارة منه، فلا يكون هو المنتهى، وعلى هذا يكون المحدد بالحقيقة، هو محدب المحيط، ويكون مقعره تحت كما في سائر الأفلاك بحسب الأجزاء المفروضة، وبعضهم على أنه نفس المحيط حتى يكون كله فوق لذاته ] . جواب سؤال تقريره أن المراد بمجرد الجهة إن كان فاعلها، فلا نسلم لزوم كونه ذا وضع فضلاً عن الإحاطة، وإن كان قابلها، فمحدد العلـو والسفـل لا يكون واجداً ضرورة أن المركز لا يقوم بالمحدد وتقرير الجواب : أن المراد به ما يتعين به
What is meant by 'muhaddid al-jiha' (determinant of direction) is that by which the position of the direction is determined. He said: Then the meaning of its determining the directions is the specification of their positions by it; otherwise, the agent does not have to be a body, and the recipient is only one. For the 'above' is a point of the sphere, and the 'below' is from the earth, but insofar as it is a center of the encompassing, and it is determined by it; the encompassing determines its center, not vice versa. For this reason, the earth has no role in determination; rather, the encompassing is determined by the whole, for the encompassed may have indication extended from it, so it is not the end. On this basis, the true determinant is the convex of the encompassing, and its concave is below, as in other spheres according to the assumed parts. Some hold that it is the encompassing itself so that all of it is above by essence. The answer to a question whose purport is: If what is meant by mere direction is its agent, then we do not accept that it must be something with position, let alone encompassing; and if its recipient, then the determinant of up and down is not existent necessarily, for the center is not established by the determinant. And the exposition of the answer: that what is meant is that by which [direction] is determined.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines 'muhaddid al-jiha' as that which determines the position of a direction. It then explains that the true determinant is the convex surface of the encompassing sphere (the muhaddid). The agent (fāʿil) does not have to be a body, and the recipient is unique. The 'above' is a point on the sphere, 'below' from the earth, but the earth does not contribute to determination; rather the encompassing sphere determines its center, not the reverse. The real determinant is the convex of that encompassing sphere, while its concave is below relative to hypothetical parts. Some scholars hold that the encompassing itself is the determinant, making the entire sphere 'above' in essence. Finally, the passage answers an implicit question: if 'mere direction' refers to the agent, it does not require position or encompassing; if it refers to the recipient, the determinant of up and down is not something that exists, because the center is not established by the determinant. The answer concludes that the intended meaning of muhaddid al-jiha is that by which direction is determined.

فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the earth or its center determines spatial directions like 'above' and 'below'.
Correction: The passage argues that the encompassing sphere's convex is the true determinant; the earth has no role.

Checkpoint

B) The concave of the encompassing sphere.

D) The agent that creates direction.

Key Takeaway
The true determinant of spatial direction is the convex of the encompassing sphere, not the earth or its center.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are you letting the 'center'—your self-interest, limited perspective, or immediate desires—determine your direction, or are you submitting to the 'encompassing' truth of Allah's decrees and the greater reality? Name one specific decision this week where you can consciously shift from self-centered determination to being shaped by what transcends you.
?
The lesson teaches that the earth (center) has no role in determining direction; the true determinant is the encompassing convex. Where have you mistakenly assumed your own status, possessions, or personal plans are the determinant of your success or direction? What would change if you truly believed that ultimate direction comes from outside yourself—from Allah's encompassing will?
?
The source says the agent (fāʿil) of direction does not have to be a body. How does that challenge your reliance on physical signs, leaders, or tangible results to guide your life? Are you ready to trust a non-material Guide (Allah) to set your course, even when you cannot see Him directly? What is one step you can take today to seek His guidance over your own.
