5 min
The Four Elements from Primary Qualities
Defines the four elements from primary qualities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classical philosophers concluded that there are exactly four elemental simples based on the necessary pairing of the primary qualities heat, cold, moisture, and dryness.

Core Idea
The four elements are the only possible combinations of two opposite pairs.

This lesson presents the foundational proof for the four classical elements: fire, air, water, and earth. By observing that all elemental bodies possess two of the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness), and that no simple body has only one or three of these, philosophers deduced that only dual combinations are possible. This leads necessarily to exactly four elements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's reasoning.
Notice how the argument builds from observation to conclusion.
2
Identify the four primary qualities
The passage mentions heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Understand that these are considered basic and irreducible.
These qualities are sensed directly.
3
Understand why only two can coexist
The author argues that having only one quality is not observed, and three or four cannot combine due to opposition (heat vs cold, moisture vs dryness). So only pairs are possible.
This is the key logical step.
4
Map each element to its pair
The four possible pairs are: hot-dry (fire), hot-moist (air), cold-moist (water), cold-dry (earth). Commit these to memory.
This classification is the foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القسم الثاني في البسائط العنصرية، وفيه مباحث: المبحث الأول: لما وجدوا الأجسام العنصرية لا تخلو عن حرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة، من غير اقتصار على واحد أو اجتماع لثلاثة، ذهبوا إلى أن العناصر أربعة بحسب الازدواجات الممكنة حار يابس هو النار وحار رطب هو الهواء، وبارد رطب هو الماء وبارد يابس هو الأرض، وبنوا طرق الحصر على هذه الكيفيات الأربع، ولوازمها، مثل الخفة والثقل على الإطلاق أو الإضافة، ومثل الاجتماع والافتراق بسهولة أو عسر، والتعويل على الاستقراء. المعول عليه من أقوال الفلاسفة، أنها أربعة النار والهواء والماء والأرض، لأن الشواهد الحسية والتجربة والتأمل في أحوال التركيبات والتحليلات، قد دلت على أن الأجسام العنصرية بسائطها ومركباتها لا تخلو عن حرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة، ولم يوجد في البسائط ما يشتمل على واحدة فقط، ولم يمكن اجتماع الأربعة أو الثلاثة لما بين الحرارة والبرودة، وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة من التضاد. فتعين اجتماع اثنتين من الكيفيات الأربع في كل بسيط عنصري، فالجامع بين الحرارة واليبوسة هو النار، وبين الحرارة والرطوبة هو الهواء، وبين البرودة والرطوبة هو الماء، وبين البرودة واليبوسة هو الأرض، ومبنى ما ذكروا في بيان الحصر على هذه الكيفيات الأربع كما يقال: العنصر إما حار أو بارد، وكل منهما إما يابس أو رطب، أو على لوازمها، كما يقال العنصر إما خفيف أو ثقيل، وكل منهما إما على الإطلاق أو على الإضافة، أو يقال لا بد في تركيب الممتزجات من لطيف أو كثيف، فاللطيف إما بحيث يحرق ما يلاقيه وهو النار أو لا وهو الهواء، والكثيف إما سيال وهو الماء أو لا وهو الأرض، أو يقال لا بد من قبول الأشكال، وجمع وتفريق للأجزاء، فالعنصر إما قابل للأشكال بسهولة، أو بعسر، وكل منهما إما أن يكون له قوة جامعة أو مفرقة. هذا والتعويل على الاستقراء.
He said: The second section on elemental simples. It contains investigations: The first investigation: When they found that elemental bodies are not free from heat, cold, moisture and dryness, without restriction to one or combination of three, they held that the elements are four according to the possible pairs: hot dry is fire, hot moist is air, cold moist is water, cold dry is earth. They built methods of enumeration on these four qualities and their concomitants, such as lightness and heaviness absolutely or relatively, and like assembling and separating with ease or difficulty, and reliance on induction. What is relied upon from the sayings of the philosophers is that they are four: fire, air, water, earth, because sensory evidence, experiment, and reflection on the states of compositions and decompositions have indicated that elemental bodies, their simples and compounds, are not free from heat, cold, moisture and dryness. Nothing was found among the simples that contains only one, and it is not possible for four or three to combine because of the opposition between heat and cold, and between moisture and dryness. So it is determined that two of the four qualities combine in every elemental simple: the combiner of heat and dryness is fire; of heat and moisture is air; of cold and moisture is water; of cold and dryness is earth. The basis of what they mentioned in explaining the enumeration is on these four qualities, as it is said: The element is either hot or cold, and each of them is either dry or moist; or on their concomitants, as it is said: The element is either light or heavy, and each of them is either absolutely or relatively; or it is said: In the composition of mixtures there must be something fine or dense, the fine either burns what it meets—that is fire—or not—that is air; the dense either is fluid—water—or not—earth; or it is said: There must be acceptance of shapes, and gathering and separating of parts, so the element is either easily shapeable or with difficulty, and each of them either has a uniting or separating power. This, and reliance is on induction.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the four classical elements: fire, air, water, and earth. It begins by stating that elemental bodies always exhibit two of the four primary qualities: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Since no simple body has only one quality, and three or four cannot combine due to opposition, the only possibilities are pairs. These pairs define the four elements: hot-dry (fire), hot-moist (air), cold-moist (water), cold-dry (earth). The passage then discusses various methods of enumeration: one based on the qualities themselves (hot/cold paired with dry/moist), another based on concomitants like lightness/heaviness (absolute or relative), another based on fine/dense (burning or not, fluid or not), and another based on shape acceptance and gathering/separation. The final basis is empirical induction. The author presents the view of the philosophers as relying on observation and experience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four elements are arbitrarily chosen by philosophers.
Correction: They are derived necessarily from the observable pairing of opposite qualities.

Checkpoint
Why are there exactly four elements according to this passage?
A) Because each element has one primary quality
B) Because the qualities oppose one another, allowing only two-pair combinations
C) Because philosophers declared it by revelation
D) Because there are four directions

Key Takeaway
The four elements arise necessarily from the impossible coexistence of opposite primary qualities, giving four unique pairs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that sensory evidence and experiment confirm the four elements. When you observe the world around you—a flame, a breeze, a raindrop, a handful of soil—can you see these four qualities at work? How does this classification affect the way you view ordinary objects?
?
The author insists that the elements are not arbitrary but follow from necessary combinations of opposites. Think of a time you assumed something in your faith or life was arbitrary. How might a deeper inquiry show that it is, in fact, rooted in a necessary truth?
?
If every material thing is a mix of these elements, what does that tell you about the temporary and composite nature of your own body? How should this awareness shape your priorities today?

Enumeration Methods for the Four Elements
Enumerates multiple classification methods for elements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various logical methods philosophers used to enumerate the four elements and why induction is the ultimate basis.

Core Idea
The four elements can be enumerated by qualities, lightness/heaviness, fineness/density, or shape acceptance.

This lesson explores the multiple logical methods that philosophers employed to arrive at the same four elements. Each method—based on primary qualities, on physical properties like lightness, or on behavior like shapeability—converges on the same elements. The passage also emphasizes that all these methods ultimately rely on empirical induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on enumeration methods
Focus on the section beginning with 'ومبنى ما ذكروا في بيان الحصر...' and read it together with the translation.
This part presents alternative classification systems.
2
Identify the first method: based on primary qualities
Notice how elements are classified as hot/cold and dry/moist, creating a binary tree.
This corresponds to the earlier pairing.
3
Identify the second method: based on concomitants
Elements are classified as light or heavy, absolutely or relatively. This is a different starting point.
Concomitants are properties that necessarily accompany the qualities.
4
Identify the third method: fine vs dense
Elements are divided into fine (fire, air) and dense (water, earth), with further subdivisions.
This method uses behavior like burning or fluidity.
5
Note the reliance on induction
The passage concludes that all these methods ultimately depend on inductive reasoning (استقراء), not pure logic.
This grounds the classification in observation.

Definition of Mizaj and Its Qualifiers
Al-Taftazani defines mizaj and explains why 'intermediate' and 'similar' are used.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mizaj (temperament) and the specific purposes of its qualifiers 'intermediate' and 'similar' in Islamic philosophical terminology.

Core Idea
Mizaj is an intermediate quality—neither purely hot nor cold—defined to exclude its accidental effects.

This lesson unpacks Al-Taftazani's precise definition of temperament (mizaj). He carefully chooses the terms 'intermediate' and 'similar' to avoid confusion with accidental properties and to ensure exactness. We will examine why these qualifiers are necessary and how they shape the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Taftazani uses a very methodical approach.
2
Identify the two qualifiers
The definition includes 'intermediate' (mutawassita) and 'similar' (mutashabiha). Find where they appear and note the reasons given for each.
The objection-and-answer structure highlights the purpose of 'intermediate'.
3
Understand the purpose of 'intermediate'
The passage states that 'intermediate' excludes accidentals like colors and tastes. Understand what intermediateness means: being closer to each opposite than to its opposite.
Example: hot relative to cold part, cold relative to hot part.
4
Understand the purpose of 'similar'
'Similar' is for exactness (tahqiq) in the definition, not for exclusion. It adds precision.
The author also notes that using 'tangible' alone would suffice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شروع في رابع الأقسام التي رتب عليها الكلام في فصل مباحث الأجسام على التفصيل وهو في المركبات التي لها مزاج، وفيه مقدمة لبيان حقيقة المزاج وأقسامه، وثلاثة مباحث للإشارة إلى الأقسام الثلاثة للمتزج أعني المعدن والنبات والحيوان ـ وقد سبق أن الكلام في ذلك مبني على قانون الفلسفة، وإنما آثر في تفسير المزاج، طريق التفريع على طريق التعريف بأن يقول هو كيفية متوسطة متشابهة حادثة من تفاعل العناصر المجتمعة المتصغرة الأجزاء بقواها المنكسرة، سورة كل من كيفياتها الأربع ، لأن ذكر المتوسطة والمتشابهة، إنما يحس بعد ذكر أجزاء العناصر واجتماعها وكيفياتها، وفي رعاية ذلك فوات حسن انتظام اللفظ ووضوح المعنى. فإن قيل : أي حاجة إلى ذكر المتوسطة . قلنا : الاحتراز عن توابع المزاج كالألوان والطعوم والروائح . لأن معنى التوسط أن يكون أقرب إلى كل من الكيفيتين المتضادتين مما يقابلها . بمعنى أن يستحر بالقياس إلى الجزء البارد ويستبرد بالقياس إلى الجزء الحار، وكذا في الرطوبة واليبوسة. وأما ذكر التشابه على ما سيجيء من معناه ، فللتحقيق دون الاحتراز، ولو ذكر بدلهما الملموسة لكفي وحسن التحديد.
Beginning of the fourth section upon which the speech in the chapter on investigations of bodies in detail is arranged, and it is concerning compounds that have a temperament (mizaj). It contains an introduction to explain the reality of temperament and its divisions, and three investigations to indicate the three divisions of the tempered [things], namely mineral, plant, and animal. It has preceded that the speech about that is based on the law of philosophy, and he preferred in explaining temperament the method of derivation (tafri') over the method of definition (ta'rif) by saying: it is an intermediate (mutawassita) similar (mutashabiha) quality (kayfiyya) arising from the interaction (tafa'ul) of the assembled elements (anasir) with reduced parts, by their broken powers, the form of each of its four qualities, because the mention of 'intermediate' and 'similar' is only appreciated after mentioning the parts of the elements, their assembly, and their qualities, and in observing that there is a loss of good order of expression and clarity of meaning. If it is said: What need is there to mention 'intermediate'? We say: to guard against the accidentals of temperament (tawabi' al-mizaj) such as colors, tastes, and odors. Because the meaning of intermediateness is that it be closer to each of the two opposing qualities than its opposite. Meaning that it is hot relative to the cold part and cold relative to the hot part, and similarly in moisture and dryness. As for the mention of 'similar' according to what will come of its meaning, it is for exactness (tahqiq) not for exclusion, and if instead of them [intermediate and similar] he had mentioned 'tangible' (malmusa), it would have sufficed and the definition would have been good.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'mizaj' (temperament) and explains the necessity of the two qualifiers 'intermediate' (mutawassita) and 'similar' (mutashabiha) in its definition. The author begins by reminding that this section deals with compounds having a temperament, and he states that the explanation of temperament will follow the method of derivation (tafri') rather than direct definition (ta'rif). He then gives the definition: temperament is an intermediate, similar quality arising from the interaction of elements with reduced parts through their broken powers. He explains that the terms 'intermediate' and 'similar' are best understood after describing the elements and their qualities. Then an objection is raised: why mention 'intermediate'? The answer is to exclude the accidentals of temperament (tawabi'), such as colors, tastes, and odors, which are not the temperament itself but consequences. Intermediateness means being closer to each of the two opposing qualities than to their opposites; for example, the mixture is hot relative to cold and cold relative to hot. As for mentioning 'similar', it is for exactness (tahqiq) in the definition, not for exclusion. The author notes that if the definer had used 'tangible' instead of both 'intermediate' and 'similar', it would have sufficed and produced a good definition.

Key Terms

كيفية متوسطة
كيفية متشابهة
توابع المزاج
تفاعل
عناصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The qualifiers 'intermediate' and 'similar' are redundant or purely stylistic additions.
Correction: The passage shows they serve distinct purposes: 'intermediate' excludes accidentals, and 'similar' adds exactness to the definition.

Checkpoint
Why is the qualifier 'intermediate' (متوسطة) used in the definition of mizaj?
A) To indicate that the temperament is a quality shared equally by all parts.
B) To exclude the accidental properties that result from temperament, such as colors and tastes.
C) To show that temperament is a mixture with no opposing qualities.
D) To emphasize that temperament arises from the interaction of elements.

Key Takeaway
Mizaj is defined as an intermediate, similar quality to precisely distinguish it from its accidental effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own character, do you often mistake your outward reactions (like anger or patience) for your underlying temperament? How can the precision in this lesson help you distinguish essence from accident in your self-accountability?
?
The definition uses 'intermediate' and 'similar' for exactness. Are you content with vague understandings of key beliefs like tawheed or iman, or do you seek the same exactness as al-Taftazani? What is one belief you have been settling for a fuzzy definition of?
?
Mizaj is balanced and intermediate. In your daily life, what is one area where you tend to extremes (e.g., in work, rest, or relationships)? What concrete step will you take this week to move towards the middle path, seeking the 'intermediate' quality that this lesson describes?

The Definition of Mizaj and Its Logical Critique
Definition of mizaj with logical critique of its conditional structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mizaj (temperament) as a quality arising from interaction of opposites, and the logical issues in its conditional formulation.

Core Idea
The definition of mizaj is not simply a formula; its logical structure requires careful scrutiny.

The author presents the definition of mizaj as a quality from interaction, but then immediately subjects it to a rigorous logical critique. He questions the placement of the conditional clause and whether the definition inadvertently includes extraneous elements. This lesson unpacks that critique and shows how precise wording matters in philosophical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of mizaj and its translation. Notice the conditional clause 'when they interact...'
The definition is complex but precise.
2
Identify the logical problems
The author points out two ways to read the conditional clause: either as a description of elements (causing redundancy) or as part of the definition (causing the defined to be mentioned).
Pay attention to the different interpretations.
3
Understand the proposed solution
The author suggests the conditional is actually a separate explanatory comment (tafri') after the definition is complete.
This is the author's preferred interpretation.
4
Consider the objection about concomitants
An objection is raised: does this definition include the 'concomitants' of mizaj? The author responds that even if the conditional were part of the definition, the phrase 'similar across all' excludes them, though he finds this forced.
This shows the ongoing refinement of definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المزاج كيفية تحدث عن تفاعل كيفيات متضادة موجودة في عناصر متصغرة الأجزاء، لتماس أكثر كل واحد منها أكثر الأجزاء إذا تفاعلت بقواها بعضها في بعض حدثت عن جملتها كيفية متشابهة في جميعها هي المزاج، فسلك طريق التعريف منحرفاً إلى طريق التفريع ، وحينئذ فالشرطية (١) . أعني قوله إذا تفاعلت الخ أن كانت صفة العناصر وقع تكرار لا حاجة إليه، وكان قوله هي المزاج أجنبياً لا يرتبط بما سبق إلا بأن يجعل صفة كيفية متشابهة ، فيذكر المحدود في الحد، وإن جعل الظرف متعلقاً بقوله يحدث ، كان الواقع في معرض الجزاء أجنبياً لا معنى له . والظاهر أن قوله إذا تفاعلت الخ أخذ في طريق التفريع بعد تمام التعريف، وأسند التفاعل في التعريف إلى الكيفيات على ما هو ظاهر نظر الصناعة، وفي التفريع إلى العناصر بواسطة القوى التي هي الكيفيات أو الصور النوعية على ما هو أقرب إلى التحقيق الفلسفي.
فإن قيل : فيدخل في التعريف توابع المزاج.
قلنا : وكذلك إذا جعلنا الشرطية من تمام التعريف، لأن إخراجها بقيد المتشابهة تفسيراً لها بما فسروا به المتوسطة تعسف على ما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى، ثم لا بد لتوضيح المقام من الكلام في مواضع :
(١) القضية الشرطية هي التي تتركب من قضيتين ويحكم فيها على تعلق أحد طرفيها بالآخر، وهي إما متصلة وإما منفصلة، فالشرطية المتصلة هي التي توجب أو تسلب لزوم قضية أخرى. كقولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود والشرطية المنفصلة : هي التي توجب أو تسلب عناد قضية لاخرى كقولنا إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً.
Mizaj is a quality that arises from the interaction of opposing qualities existing in elements with small parts, due to the contact of more of each one of them—many parts, when they interact through their powers with each other, there arises from their totality a quality similar in all of them, which is mizaj. So the author took the path of definition deviating to the path of dedifferentiation. Then the conditional (proposition) (1): I mean his statement 'when they interact' etc.—if it was a description of the elements, there occurs a repetition not needed, and his statement 'it is mizaj' is extraneous, not connected to what preceded except by making it a description of a similar quality, so the defined is mentioned in the definition. And if the adverb is made dependent on his statement 'arises', then what occurs in the position of the consequent is extraneous, meaningless. The apparent is that his statement 'when they interact' etc. is taken in the path of dedifferentiation after the completion of the definition, and he attributed the interaction in the definition to the qualities in apparent view of the art, and in the dedifferentiation to the elements via the powers which are the qualities or the specific forms, which is closer to philosophical investigation. If it is said: Then the concomitants of mizaj enter into the definition. We say: Likewise if we make the conditional part of the definition, because excluding them by the condition of similarity—interpreting it as they interpreted the intermediate—is forced, as will come, God willing. Then for clarifying the position, we must speak on points: (1) The conditional proposition is that which is composed of two propositions and in which is judged the dependency of one part on the other, and it is either conjunctive or disjunctive. The conjunctive conditional is that which affirms or negates the implication of another proposition, like our saying: If the sun is rising, then daytime exists. The disjunctive conditional is that which affirms or negates the opposition of a proposition to another, like our saying: Either this number is even or it is odd.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a definition and logical analysis of the concept of mizaj (temperament/mixture). The author begins by defining mizaj as a quality that results from the interaction of opposing qualities in elements with very small parts, leading to a uniform quality. He then critiques the wording of the definition, pointing out that the conditional clause 'when they interact...' could be redundant or improperly placed if treated as a description of the elements, and that the term 'mizaj' is used awkwardly in the definition. He suggests that the author of the definition intended the conditional as a separate explanatory comment after the definition proper. An objection is raised: does this definition inadvertently include the 'concomitants' (tawabi') of mizaj? The author responds that even if the conditional were considered part of the definition, the exclusion of those concomitants by the phrase 'similar across all of them' would be forced, and he postpones further discussion. The passage concludes by stating the need for further clarification on several points. A footnote defines conditional propositions (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) into conjunctive and disjunctive types, which is relevant to the earlier logical analysis.

Types of Conditional Propositions in Logic
Definition of conditional propositions into conjunctive and disjunctive types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of conditional propositions: conjunctive and disjunctive, as defined in classical Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are the building blocks of logical implication and opposition.

In the course of analyzing the definition of mizaj, the author introduces a footnote on conditional propositions (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya). This lesson focuses on that footnote, which defines the two main types: conjunctive (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or). Understanding these is crucial for following the logical analysis in the main text.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of conditional proposition
Read the Arabic and translation for the definition of qadiyya shartiyya. Note its composition from two propositions and judgment of dependency.
2
Differentiate between conjunctive and disjunctive
The conjunctive conditional affirms or negates implication; the disjunctive affirms or negates opposition. Note the examples.
Conjunctive example: If sun is rising then daytime exists. Disjunctive example: Either number is even or odd.
3
Relate to the mizaj discussion
Consider why the author brought this up. The earlier critique involved analyzing the conditional structure of the definition.
The footnote provides the formal logical framework for that analysis.

The Second Mixture: When Does the Definition Include It?
Sanusi analyzes whether the second mixture enters the definition based on its agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the two scenarios for the second mixture and how that affects its inclusion in the definition of mizāj.

Core Idea
The second mixture is included only if its agent is the elemental forms, not the compound forms.

Sanusi presents a subtle but important distinction about the second mixture, such as gold formed from mercury and sulfur. Whether this type of mixture is covered by the general definition of mizāj depends on what causes the mixture to occur. This passage clarifies that there are two possibilities, and only one qualifies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the two scenarios starting with 'إن جعلنا'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Find the two cases: one where the agent is the forms of simple bodies, and one where it is the specific forms of compounds.
3
Examine the evidence for the first scenario
The author mentions crushing and distillation of meat as proof that elemental forms are preserved and active in the mixture.
This empirical evidence supports inclusion.
4
Understand why the second scenario is excluded
If the mixture arises from the specific forms of the compounds themselves, it does not involve the interaction of elemental powers, so it falls outside the definition.
5
Reflect on the key principle
The definition depends on the causal agent of the mixture. This is a foundational distinction in kalam physics.
Think about how this applies to other mixtures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ قال ( ثم) التعريف يتناول المزاج الثاني) فما فوقه كما في الذهب من الزئبق (۱) والكبريت، إن جعلنا الفاعل فيه صور البسائط على ما يظهر بالقرع والانبيق لا الصور المعارضة على المركبات باعداد الكيفية المزاجية ] . أعني الحاصل من امتزاج الأجزاء المتصغرة للمركبات، كمزاج الذهب الحاصل من امتزاج الزئبق والكبريت، إن جعلناه حادثاً من انكسار الكيفيات بصور البسائط العنصرية المحفوظة في الممتزج على ما يظهر بالقرع والانبيق، فإنا إذا وضعنا فيه قطعة من اللحم مثلاً تميزت إلى جسم مائي قاطر، وأرضى متكلس. وأما إذا جعلنا حدوثه بواسطة الصور النوعية للمركبات بأن تعد الكيفية المزاجية الأجزاء الممتزجة لفيضان صورة نوعية عليها غير صور العناصر، ثم تتفاعل الممتزجات المختلفة بواسطة صورها ،وكيفياتها، فتحدث كيفية متوسطة متشابهة في الشكل (۱) كما في الذهب بواسطة الصورة الزئبقية والكبريتية، فلا يدخل في التعريف، لأنها لم تحدث من تفاعل العناصر بقواها أي صورها أو كيفياتها.
He said: 'Then the definition includes the second mixture' and above that, as in gold from mercury and sulfur, if we make the agent in it the forms of the simple bodies as appears by crushing and distillation, not the contrary forms on the compounds by preparing the temperamental quality. I mean that which results from the mixture of the minute parts of compounds, like the temperament of gold resulting from the mixture of mercury and sulfur, if we make it occurring from the breaking of qualities by the forms of the elemental simple bodies preserved in the mixture as appears by crushing and distillation, for if we place in it a piece of meat, for example, it separates into a watery dripping body and a calcined earthy body. But if we make its occurrence by means of the specific forms of the compounds such that the temperamental quality prepares the mixed parts for the emanation of a specific form upon them other than the forms of the elements, then the different mixtures interact by means of their forms and qualities, and an intermediate quality similar in shape occurs, as in gold by means of the mercurial and sulfuric forms, then it does not enter into the definition, because it did not occur from the interaction of the elements with their powers, i.e., their forms or qualities.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of whether the definition of mizāj (temperament/mixture) covers the 'second mixture' (al-mizāj al-thānī), such as the temperament of gold resulting from the combination of mercury and sulfur. The author presents two scenarios. First, if the agent is the forms of the simple bodies (ṣuwar al-basā'iṭ) — as shown by the fact that when you crush and distill a piece of meat placed in gold, it separates into elemental components — then the temperament is produced by the elemental forms and thus enters the definition. Second, if the temperament occurs by means of the specific forms of the compounds (al-ṣuwar al-naw'iyya li-l-murakkabāt) — i.e., the mercurial and sulfuric forms — then the resulting temperament does not come from the interaction of the elemental powers and therefore is not included in the definition. The key distinction is whether the agent is the elemental forms or the specific compound forms.

صور البسائط
صور نوعية
زئبق
كبريت
قرع
انبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All mixtures are the same and always included in the definition.
Correction: The definition depends on whether the mixture's agent is elemental or compound-specific. The second mixture is only included if its agent is the elemental forms.

Checkpoint
When does the definition of mizāj include the second mixture (e.g., gold from mercury and sulfur)?
A) When the agent is the specific forms of the compounds
B) When the agent is the forms of the elemental simple bodies
C) Always, because all mixtures are the same
D) Only when the mixture is not perceptible

Key Takeaway
Whether the second mixture is included in the definition of mizāj depends on whether its production involves the elemental simple bodies as agents or the specific compound forms.

?
The source shows that even gold – valuable and refined – can be produced by a wrong agent that excludes it from the definition. ?
Sanusi's precision about definitions guards against confusing one kind of mixture with another. Mizaj as a Distinct Tangible Quality
Sanusi defines mizaj and presents varying opinions on elemental forms and qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines mizaj as a distinct type of tangible quality and the different opinions about its relation to elemental forms and qualities.

Core Idea
Mizaj is a quality, not a substance; it transforms qualities while preserving forms.

This lesson examines the nature of mizaj (temperament), a key concept in philosophical physics and medicine. Sanusi clarifies that mizaj is a tangible quality distinct from the pure qualities of elements, and he reports several views on what happens to elemental forms and qualities during mixture. We'll focus on the majority Peripatetic position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's meaning.
Notice the multiple 'qīla' (it was said) which introduce different opinions.
2
Identify the reported opinions
List the four different views about what happens to elemental forms and qualities during mixture. Pay attention to the distinctions.
The first opinion says forms remain but qualities change; the second says both remain; the third says forms become intermediate; the fourth says entirely new specific forms arise.
3
Understand the majority view
Read the sentence after 'qad ‘ulima' which gives the author's own position. Note that the majority holds that forms remain while qualities transform into an intermediate quality.
This view is attributed to the majority of the Peripatetics (mashshā'īn).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: mizaj is a quality (kayfiyya), not a substance, and it pervades every part of the mixture.
This clarifies the ontological status of mizaj in philosophical cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال فالمزاج نوع آخر) من الكيفيات الملموسة حاصلة في كل جزء حتى البسائط، وهي على صورها دون كيفياتها . وقيل وكيفياتها. وإنما تحس بالمتوسطة للامتزاج . وقيل : بل زالت صورها إلى صورة متوسطة بينها . وقيل : بل صورة أخرى من النوعيات] . قد علم مما سبق أن المزاج كيفية ملموسة مغايرة بالنوع ، لما في العناصر من الكيفيات الصرفة حاصلة في كل جزء من أجزاء الممتزج حتى الأجزاء البسيطة العنصرية، وهي باقية على صورها ،النوعية وإنما استحالت من كيفياتها الصرفة، إلى الكيفية المتوسطة وهذا رأي جمهور المشائين (٢) .
He said: 'So the temperament is another type of tangible qualities, occurring in every part, even the simple bodies, and they remain on their forms without their qualities. And it was said: and their qualities. And it is perceived as intermediate due to mixture. And it was said: rather their forms have ceased to an intermediate form between them. And it was said: rather another form from the specific forms.' It has been known from what preceded that the temperament is a tangible quality different in type from the pure qualities in the elements, occurring in every part of the mixture, even the elemental simple parts, and they remain on their specific forms, and they have only transformed from their pure qualities to the intermediate quality, and this is the view of the majority of the Peripatetics.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage defines mizāj (temperament) as a distinct type of tangible quality (kayfiyya malmūsa) that is different in kind from the pure qualities of the elements. It occurs in every part of the mixture, including the simple elemental parts, while the elemental forms (ṣuwar) remain. The author then reports three different opinions: (1) the elements retain their forms but not their qualities; (2) they retain both forms and qualities; (3) the forms themselves change into an intermediate form; (4) another specific form emerges. The author then clarifies the view of the majority of the Peripatetics (jumhūr al-mashshā'īn): the temperament is a tangible quality different in type, present in every part, while the elemental specific forms persist, and only the pure qualities have transformed into the intermediate quality. This is the established position.

Key Terms
مزاج
كيفية ملموسة
صور نوعية
كيفيات صرفة
مشاءون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that mizaj is a substantial form or a substance itself.
Correction: Mizaj is explicitly a quality (kayfiyya), not a substance. It is a tangible quality that arises from the mixture of elements while their forms remain intact.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Peripatetics, what happens to the elemental forms and qualities during mizaj?
A) Both forms and qualities are replaced by new ones
B) Forms remain, qualities change to an intermediate quality
C) Forms change, qualities remain
D) Both forms and qualities remain unchanged

Key Takeaway
Mizaj is a tangible quality that pervades the mixture, preserving elemental forms while transmuting their qualities into an intermediate state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is a mixture of many influences: family, culture, education, desires. The Peripatetics held that your essential form (your fitrah) remains unchanged, but your qualities become intermediate. Are there areas where you have allowed your qualities to drift so far from the pure qualities of faith that your form itself is in danger? What specific quality will you work to purify this week?
?
The passage teaches that in a mixture, each element keeps its specific form while sharing an intermediate quality. Consider your relationships: when you interact with others, do you compromise your core identity (your form) for the sake of harmony, or do you maintain it while adapting your outward qualities? How can you be more intentional about preserving your Islamic identity in your daily interactions?
?
If mizaj is an intermediate quality, it is neither purely one thing nor another. Are there areas in your belief or practice where you are in an intermediate state—neither fully committed nor fully rejecting? What step can you take today to move closer to the pure quality of certainty (yaqīn) that the elements originally had?

The Four Digestions: Stages and Definitions
Enumerates the four digestions and defines each

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four stages of digestion as defined in classical medical theory and the evidence for the first stage starting in the mouth.

Core Idea
The four digestions transform food step by step from mouth to organs.

This passage from a classical medical text introduces a foundational concept in humoral medicine: the four stages of digestion. The author carefully defines each stage based on the organ involved and the type of transformation, then offers a curious piece of evidence to show that the first digestion actually begins in the mouth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Let the technical terms and the logical flow become familiar.
Pay attention to the words for chyme (كيلوس), mesentery (ماساريقا), and humor (خِلط).
2
Identify the four digestions
The passage lists digestions: mouth/stomach, liver, veins, organs. Notice how each is defined by what the food becomes at that stage.
The first digestion turns food into chyme, the second into humor, the third into second moisture, the fourth into organic form.
3
Study the evidence for the mouth's role
The author gives two reasons: the effect of chewed wheat on abscesses, and the change of food stuck between teeth. Understand why he considers mouth and stomach one continuous surface.
This is an early reference to anatomical continuity.
4
Reflect on the classification system
Notice that the author classifies digestions based on whether the food loses its form entirely or not. This logical division is typical of medieval scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وابتداؤها من الفم، ثم الكبد، ثم في العروق، ثم في الأعضاء. فإن الغذاء يستحيل في المعدة جوهراً شبيهاً بماء الكشك الثخين يسمى كيلوسا، فيندفع كثيفه من طريق الأمعاء، وينجذب لطيفه إلى الكبد من طريق ماساريقا. يعني أن الغذاء من ابتداء المضغ إلى حين يصير جزءاً من العضو يعرض له في كل آن تغير واستحالة من غير أن يكون ذلك محصوراً في عدد، إلا أنهم نظروا إلى أعضاء الغذاء والعضو المغتذي، وإلى ظهور التغيرات في الغاية. فقالوا: هضم الغذاء إما أن لا يلزمه خلع صورته، وذلك هو الذي به يتغير إلى أن يصير كيلوسا، وهو هضم المعدة وابتداؤه من الفم أو يلزمه خلع صورته، فإما أن يلزم من كمال ذلك النضج حصول الصورة العضوية وهو الهضم الرابع، ويكون في كل عضو أو لا يلزمه حصول الصورة العضوية، فإما أن يلزمه حصول التشبه بها في المزاج، وذلك هو الذي به يصير رطوبة ثانية وهو أن يكون في العروق، أو لا يلزمه ذلك، وهو الذي به يصير خلطاً ويكون هذا في الكبد. ويستدل على كون ابتداء الهضم المعدي في الفم، بأن الحنطة الممضوغة تفعل في إنضاج الدماميل ما لا تفعله المدقوقة المبلولة بالماء، أو المطبوخة فيه، وبأن ما يبقى من الطعام بين الأسنان يتغير وتنتن رائحته ويصير له كيفية مثل كيفية لحم الفم. والسبب في ذلك أن سطح الفم متصل بسطح المعدة، بل كأنهما سطح واحد بشهادة التشريح، ولذلك يجعل ما في الفم والمعدة، هضماً واحداً.
And their beginning is from the mouth, then the liver, then in the veins, then in the organs. For food is transformed in the stomach into a substance resembling thick porridge water called chyme (kaylūs), its thick part is pushed out through the intestines, and its thin part is attracted to the liver through the mesentery (masārīqā). He means that food from the beginning of chewing until it becomes part of the organ undergoes at every moment change and transformation without that being limited to a number, except that they looked at the organs of nutrition and the nourished organ, and at the appearance of changes in the end. So they said: The digestion of food either does not require shedding its form, and that is that by which it changes until it becomes chyme, and it is the digestion of the stomach and its beginning is from the mouth; or it requires shedding its form. Then either from the completion of that ripening results the attainment of the organic form, and that is the fourth digestion, and it occurs in every organ; or the attainment of the organic form does not result. Then either the attainment of resemblance to it in temperament results, and that is that by which it becomes a second moisture, and it occurs in the veins; or that does not result, and it is that by which it becomes a humor (khilt), and this occurs in the liver. And evidence that the beginning of gastric digestion is in the mouth is that chewed wheat acts on ripening abscesses in a way that crushed wheat moistened with water or cooked in it does not, and that what remains of food between the teeth changes and its odor becomes putrid and it acquires a quality like the quality of the flesh of the mouth. And the reason for that is that the surface of the mouth is connected to the surface of the stomach, rather they are like a single surface according to the testimony of anatomy, and therefore what is in the mouth and stomach is made one digestion.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage enumerates and defines the four stages of digestion according to classical medical theory. The author begins by stating the sequence: mouth, liver, veins, organs. He then explains that food turns into chyme in the stomach, with thick waste exiting via intestines and thin liquid drawn to the liver via the mesentery. The transformation is continuous, but scholars categorize it into four digestions based on the organs involved and the type of change. The first digestion (mouth and stomach) changes food into chyme without shedding its form entirely. The second (liver) turns it into a humor (khilt). The third (veins) turns it into a second moisture resembling the organ's temperament. The fourth (organs) achieves the organic form. He then provides evidence that the first digestion begins in the mouth: chewed wheat is more effective on abscesses, and food stuck between teeth changes to resemble flesh. The reason is the continuity of the mouth and stomach surfaces as shown by anatomy, so they are considered one digestive stage.

Key Terms
هضم
كيلوس
خِلط
ماساريقا
صورة عضوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first digestion is only in the mouth or only in the stomach.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the first digestion (producing chyme) is the digestion of the stomach and its beginning is from the mouth, and they are considered one digestion due to the continuity of their surfaces.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the first digestion?
A) Only in the mouth
B) Only in the stomach
C) Both mouth and stomach together
D) In the liver

Key Takeaway
The four digestions progressively transform food: first into chyme (mouth/stomach), then humor (liver), then second moisture (veins), and finally organic form (organs).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals the continuous, careful transformation of food into your very flesh. When you next eat a meal, how will you consciously acknowledge that every bite is being 'digested' through four stages to sustain you? What конкретное (specific) thought or prayer will you incorporate to turn eating into an act of gratitude?
?
The author uses the example of chewed wheat healing abscesses to show that even a small initial change (chewing) has consequences beyond what we see. In your spiritual life, what 'small chew'—a fleeting thought, a brief prayer, a minor act of obedience—might have deeper transformative effects on your heart? Will you commit to tracing that effect today?
?
Food undergoes shedding of form to become part of you. What attachments, habits, or assumptions in your character might need to 'shed their form' so that you can spiritually grow into a person more pleasing to Allah? What is the first concrete step you will take toward that shedding this week?

Correcting Misunderstandings about Digestive Stages and Their Wastes
Correction of a misconception about digestion numbering and explanation of wastes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct classification of the four digestions and why each produces wastes, based on the author's refutation of a common error.

Core Idea
Digestion is transformation into organ substance; wastes arise because digestion cannot convert all food.

The author begins by correcting a widespread misconception about the sequence of the four digestions. He then explains why each digestion produces waste, focusing on the first and second digestions. This passage clarifies the relationship between digestion stages and the body's waste removal mechanisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common error
Read the opening line where the author refutes the idea that the first digestion is only in the mouth, the second in the stomach, etc. Note his correction that the first digestion encompasses both mouth and stomach.
The error is to separate mouth and stomach into different digestions.
2
Understand the definition of digestion
The author defines digestion as the change of food into the substance of the organ. This is the criterion for distinguishing stages.
3
Follow the explanation of each digestion's waste
The passage then details the wastes of the first and second digestions, and begins discussing the third and fourth. Note the reasons for the quantity of waste in each.
The first digestion has much waste due to voluntary intake of mixed food; the second has little waste mostly exiting via urine.
4
Reflect on the incomplete portion
The passage cuts off while discussing the third and fourth digestions. Consider what might follow about natural vs. non-natural expulsion.
The author likely completed the classification, but the text is missing here.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا كما يسبق إلى بعض الأوهام من أن أول الهضوم في الفم. والثاني في المعدة والثالث في الكبد والرابع في العروق، خطأ لما هو العمدة، والغاية في الهضم. أعني التغيير إلى جوهر هذا العضو عن درجة الاعتبار، وأما جعل الهضم الكبدي واحداً، مع أن ابتداءه في الماساريقا. وجميع أعني العروق الدقيقة الصلبة الواصلة بين الكبد، وبين أواخر المعدة، الأمعاء، وليس لها اتحاد بالكبد، فلأنه لا نظير فيها للطيف الكيلوس المنجذب إليها تغير يعتد به وحالة متميزة عن الكيلوسية التي حصلت في المعدة والخلطية التي تحصل في الكبد، ثم لكل من هذه الهضوم، فضل تندفع. ضرورة أن الهاضمة لا يمكنها إحالة جميع ما يرد إليها من الغذاء، إما لكثرته، وإما لأن من أجزائه ما لا يصلح أن يصير جزءاً من المغتذي فالهضم الأول له فضل كثير، لأنه وهو باق على طبيعته وأجزائه الصالحة وغير الصالحة، وعلى كثرته الواردة على المعدة، باختيار من الحيوان سيما الإنسان المفتقر باعتدال مزاجه إلى تنويع الأغذية وتكثيرها بالتركيب وغيره لا بمجرد انجذاب طبيعي للنافع وحده، كما في باقي الهضوم، وكما في غذاء النبات، فلذا احتاج إلى منفذ يسع كثرة الفضلات وهو المخرج، والهضم الثاني تكون فضلاته قليلة لطيفة، لأن ما يرد إليه بجذب طبيعي، ومن منافذ ضيقة جداً، فيخرج أكثرها بالبول، والباقي من طريق الطحال والمرارة. وأما الهضم الثالث والرابع، فاندفاع فضولهما إما أن يكون خروجاً طبيعياً أو لا، والثاني إما أن يكون باقياً على خلطيته من غير
Not as occurs to some imaginations that the first of the digestions is in the mouth, the second in the stomach, the third in the liver, and the fourth in the veins — an error regarding what is the main point and goal in digestion: I mean the change into the substance of this organ without consideration of degree. And as for making the liver digestion one, although its beginning is in the mesentery — and all [meaning] the small, hard veins connecting between the liver and the ends of the stomach and intestines, and they have no union with the liver — because there is in them no comparable change in the thin chyme attracted to them that is worthy of consideration nor a state distinct from the chymic state that occurred in the stomach and the humoral state that occurs in the liver. Then for each of these digestions there is waste that is expelled. Of necessity, because the digestive faculty cannot convert all the food that comes to it, either due to its abundance or because among its parts are those unsuitable to become part of the nourished [body]. So the first digestion has much waste, because it acts on food while it remains in its natural state with its suitable and unsuitable parts, and due to its abundance arriving at the stomach by the animal's choice, especially humans who, due to their balanced temperament, need to diversify foods and increase them by combination and otherwise, not merely by natural attraction of the beneficial alone, as in the remaining digestions and as in the nutrition of plants. Hence it needed a passage that can accommodate much waste, which is the anus. The second digestion has little, subtle waste because what comes to it is by natural attraction and from very narrow passages, so most of it exits through urine, and the rest via the spleen and gallbladder. As for the third and fourth digestions, the expulsion of their wastes is either a natural exit or not, and the second [type] is either remaining in its humoral state without...
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage first corrects a common misconception: some think the first digestion is in the mouth alone, second in the stomach, third in the liver, fourth in the veins. The author says this is an error because the chief goal is transformation into the substance of the organ, and the mouth and stomach are one continuous digestion. He then discusses why the liver digestion is considered one despite starting in the mesentery: the small veins connecting liver to intestines do not significantly change the chyme, so no distinct stage. Next, he explains that each digestion produces waste because the digestive faculty cannot fully convert all food due to abundance or unsuitable parts. The first digestion produces much waste because it involves voluntary intake and mixed suitable/unsuitable parts, requiring a large exit (the anus). The second digestion produces little, subtle waste, mostly exiting via urine, with some via spleen and gallbladder. The third and fourth digestions have minimal waste; the author begins to classify their expulsion as natural or not, but the text is cut off.

Correction: The first digestion actually includes both mouth and stomach as a continuous transformation; the liver digestion is one stage despite beginning in the mesentery.

Definition and Scope of the Growth Power
Author defines the growth power and distinguishes it from other bodily changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of the growth power and how it is distinguished from obesity, swelling, and rarefaction.

Core Idea
The growth power increases the body's dimensions proportionally by inserting food between its parts.

This lesson unpacks a key concept in natural philosophy: the growth power (القوة النامية). Before we examine how living organisms develop, we must clarify what this power does and what it does not. The author defines it precisely, then carefully excludes other types of bodily change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get the author's own definitions and distinctions.
Notice the technical terms and how they are used.
2
Identify the core definition
Find how the growth power is defined: it inserts food between body parts to increase dimensions (length, width, depth) proportionally.
Also note its initial dual role with nutritive power.
3
Understand the exclusions
List the three excluded phenomena: obesity, swelling, and rarefaction. Understand why each is excluded based on the definition.
Pay attention to the reasons given for each exclusion.
4
Examine the scholars' additional point
Notice that most scholars also exclude obesity because it does not affect the original organs from semen, only later growth.
This refines the definition further.
5
Reflect on the importance
Consider why such precise definitions are necessary in Islamic theology and philosophy, especially when discussing natural powers in relation to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها) النامية وهي التي تدخل الغذاء بين أجزاء الجسم، فتزيد في أقطاره بنسبة طبيعته، وقد يقال إنها الغاذية، إلا أنها في الابتداء تفي بإيراد البدل والزيادة، لفرط القوة، وصغر الجثة، وكثرة الرطوبة، وفي الآخر تعجز عن ذلك. أي ومن القوى الطبيعية النامية، وهي التي تزيد في أقطار الجسم، أعني الطول والعرض والعمق، على التناسب الطبيعي، بما تدخل في أجزائه من الغذاء، فخرج ما يفيد السمن، لأنه لا يكون زيادة في الطول وفيه نظر، والورم لأنه لا يكون على التناسب الطبيعي. أي النسبة التي تقتضيها طبيعة ذلك الشخص، والتخلخل، لأنه لا يكون بما يدخل في الجسم، بل بانبساط جرمه، وأما التخلخل بمعنى الانتفاش. أعني مداخلة الأجزاء الهوائية. فلو سلّم تناول الجنس أعني القوة الطبيعية لما يفيده، لخرج بقيد الغذاء، لظهور أن الأجزاء الهوائية، ليست غذاء للمتنفس والأكثرون على أن قيد مداخلة الغذاء في أجزاء الجسم، يخرج السمن أيضاً، لأنه لا يدخل في جوهر الأعضاء الأصلية المتولدة من المنى، بل في الأعضاء المتولدة من الدم، ومائيته كاللحم.
He said: And among them is the growth power (al-nāmiya), which is that which inserts food between the parts of the body, increasing its dimensions according to its natural proportion; and it may be said that it is the nutritive power, except that initially it suffices for the replacement and increase, due to excess of strength, smallness of the body, and abundance of moisture, and later it becomes incapable of that. That is: And among the natural powers is the growth power (al-nāmiya), which increases the dimensions of the body—meaning length, width, and depth—according to natural proportion, by means of what it inserts of food into its parts. Thus it excludes that which produces obesity, because it does not involve increase in length—and there is consideration in this; and [excludes] swelling, because it is not according to natural proportion, meaning the proportion required by the nature of that person; and [excludes] rarefaction, because it does not occur by something entering the body, but by the expansion of its substance—as for rarefaction in the sense of inflation, meaning the intercalation of airy parts. So even if the genus—meaning the natural power—were conceded to include what it [i.e., rarefaction] produces, it would be excluded by the condition of food, because it is evident that airy parts are not food for the breather. And most scholars hold that the condition of inserting food into the parts of the body also excludes obesity, because it does not enter into the substance of the original organs generated from semen, but rather into the organs generated from blood and its moisture, like flesh.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the growth power (القوة النامية) as one of the natural powers. It explains that this power inserts food between the body's parts to increase its dimensions (length, width, depth) proportionally. The author then distinguishes it from other phenomena: obesity (السمن) because it does not increase length, albeit with hesitation; swelling (الورم) because it lacks natural proportion; and rarefaction (التخلخل) because it occurs through expansion or airy parts rather than food insertion. The definition's conditions also exclude obesity, as it does not affect original organs from semen but only later growth from blood. The passage clarifies the precise scope of the growth power.

الورم
التخلخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Growth power is the same as the nutritive power.
Correction: The growth power is distinct; it initially also handles replacement and increase, but later only growth; it is defined by increasing dimensions proportionally via food insertion, while nutritive power focuses on replacement.

Key Takeaway
The growth power is precisely defined as the faculty that directs food into body parts to increase dimensions proportionally, and must be distinguished from obesity, swelling, and rarefaction.

Three Opinions on the Generative Power
Al-Taftazani classifies three opinions on the definition of the generative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on whether the term 'generative power' refers to all three natural powers (nutritive, augmentative, generative), only the producing power, or both producing and articulating powers.

Core Idea
The generative power's definition varies among scholars.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents a scholarly disagreement about the scope of the generative power. He explains that some consider it to include all three powers responsible for growth and reproduction, while others limit it to the producing power or include both producing and articulating. This classification helps clarify the terminology used in key philosophical works like al-Shifa and al-Qanun.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three powers
Learn what the nutritive, augmentative, generative, formative, producing, and articulating powers are. The passage explains that the formative power gives each organ its specific form, the articulating power acts in the womb, and the producing power creates the seed.
These powers are part of Islamic natural philosophy.
2
Identify the three opinions
Note the three views on what the generative power includes: (1) all three powers together, (2) only the producing power, or (3) both producing and articulating powers.
The first opinion is from al-Shifa and al-Isharat, the second from some ancient thinkers, the third from the majority.
3
Examine the evidence
See how each opinion is supported by authoritative sources. Al-Shifa defines the generative power as taking a part of the body and transforming others. Al-Qanun divides the preserving power into generative and formative.
Notice that the commentator of al-Isharat uses 'generative' in both a broad and narrow sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة تلبس كل عضو صورته الخاصة به، فيكمل وجود الأعضاء، وهذه تخص باسم المصورة ومحلها المنى، كالمفصلة وفعلها إنما يكون في الرحم، وكلام القوم متردد في أن المولدة اسم للقوى الثلاث جميعاً)، أو للمحصلة وحدها، أو لها وللمفصلة معاً. والأول هو المفهوم من الشفاء والإشارات حيث حصر القوى الطبيعية في الغاذية والنامية والمولدة من غير تعرض للمصورة ولذا قال الشارح للإشارات أن المولدة للمثل تنقسم إلى نوعين : مولدة ومصورة، والمولدة إلى نوعين : محصلة ومفصلة ، فأراد بالمولدة أولاً المتصرفة لحفظ النوع ، ليعم الأقسام، وثانياً المتصرفة لا على وجه التصوير ليكون أخص . بل كلام الشفاء صريح فيما ذكرنا . لأنه قال المولدة قوة تأخذ من الجسم الذي هي فيه جزءاً هو شبيه بالقوة فتفعل فيه باستمداد أجسام أخرى تشبه به من التخليق والتمزيج ما يصيره شبيهاً به بالفعل، وقال: للمولدة فعلان : أحدهما تخليق البذر وتشكيله وتطبيعه . الثانية . صورها من القوى والمقادير والأعداد والأشكال والخشونة والملاسة، وما يتصل بذلك متسخرة تحت قدرة المتفرد بالجبروت عز شأنه، والثاني: أعني كون المولدة للمحصلة، مذهب بعض الأقدمين، وبه يشعر ما نقل عن ابن سينا أن القوة المولدة يخدمها القوتان اللتان إحداهما المفصلة، والأخرى المصورة والثالث: أعني كونها اسماً لما يعم المحصلة والمفصلة. مذهب الجمهور والمصرح به في القانون حيث قال : إن القوة المتصرفة لبقاء النوع تنقسم إلى نوعين إلى المولدة والمصورة والمولدة نوعان نوع يولد المنى في الذكر والأنثى، ونوع يفصل القوى التي في المنى فيمزجها تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو.
And in summary, every organ puts on its own form, so the existence of organs is completed, and this is specifically named the formative power, and its location is the semen, like the articulating power, and its action is only in the womb. And the speech of the people is undecided about whether the generative power is a name for the three powers together, or for the producing power alone, or for it and the articulating power together. The first is what is understood from al-Shifa and al-Isharat, where he limited the natural powers to the nutritive, the augmentative, and the generative, without reference to the formative. Hence the commentator of al-Isharat said that the generative for preserving the species divides into two types: generative and formative, and the generative divides into two types: producing and articulating. So he meant by 'generative' first the disposing power for preserving the species, to encompass the divisions, and second, the disposing power not in the way of forming, to be more specific. Rather, the speech of al-Shifa is explicit in what we mentioned, because it said: the generative power is a power that takes from the body in which it is a part that is similar to the power, and it acts upon it by seeking aid from other bodies similar to it, from the creation and mixing, making it similar to it in actuality. And it said: for the generative there are two actions: one is the creation of the seed and its shaping and naturalizing; the second: it forms from the powers, quantities, numbers, shapes, roughness, smoothness, and what is connected to that, subjugated under the power of the Unique in majesty, exalted is His status. And the second: that the generative is for the producing alone, which is the doctrine of some ancient thinkers, and it is indicated by what was transmitted from Ibn Sina that the generative power is served by two powers: one is the articulating, the other the formative. And the third: that it is a name for what encompasses the producing and the articulating, which is the doctrine of the majority and what is explicitly stated in al-Qanun, where he said: the power disposing for the preservation of the species divides into two types: the generative and the formative, and the generative is two types: one type produces semen in the male and female, and a type separates the powers in the semen and mixes them with mixtures according to each organ.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage classifies the three opinions regarding the definition of the generative power (al-quwwa al-muwallida). The author first explains the formative power (al-musawwira), which gives each organ its specific form, located in the semen, similar to the articulating power (al-mufassila) that acts in the womb. Then he states that scholars disagree whether the generative power is: (1) a name for all three powers (nutritive, augmentative, generative) together, (2) only the producing power (al-muhassila), or (3) includes both the producing and articulating powers. The first opinion is supported by al-Shifa and al-Isharat, where natural powers are limited to three without mentioning the formative. The commentator of al-Isharat (Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) notes that the generative, in the sense of preserving the species, divides into generative (muhassila) and formative (musawwira), and then the generative divides into producing (muhassila) and articulating (mufassila). This shows how the term 'generative' can be used broadly or narrowly. The author then quotes al-Shifa to show that the generative power takes a part of the body similar to itself and transforms other bodies into its likeness through creation and mixing, and it has two actions: creating the seed and forming powers, quantities, etc., all subjugated under God's power. The second opinion, that the generative is only the producing power, is attributed to some ancients and hinted at in Ibn Sina's statement that the generative power is served by two powers: articulating and formative. The third opinion, that it encompasses both producing and articulating, is the majority view and is explicit in al-Qanun, which divides the power for preserving the species into generative and formative, and divides the generative into what produces semen and what separates and mixes powers in the semen for each organ.

Key Terms
القوة المولدة
القوة المصورة
القوة المحصلة
القوة المفصلة
الشفاء
الإشارات
القانون
ابن سينا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-muwallida always refers to all three powers (nutritive, augmentative, generative).
Correction: The passage shows that the term is used differently: it can be a name for all three, only the producing, or both producing and articulating, depending on the scholar.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which opinion does the majority hold regarding the generative power?
A) It is a name for the nutritive, augmentative, and generative powers together.
B) It is a name for the producing power alone.
C) It is a name encompassing the producing and articulating powers.
D) It is a name for all five natural powers.

Key Takeaway
The term 'generative power' is used differently by different scholars; understanding these distinctions is crucial for interpreting philosophical and medical texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars disagreed on the precise meaning of 'generative power,' and each opinion required careful reasoning from evidence. In your own study of Islam, have you ever accepted a definition or idea without questioning its sources or considering alternative perspectives? Where in your faith today might you be settling for a comfortable but imprecise understanding? What will you do this week to seek the truth with the same rigor these scholars showed?
?
The passage ends by reminding that all these powers are 'subjugated under the power of the Unique.' When you observe natural processes such as growth, reproduction, or healing, do you truly see them as directly under God's control, or do you functionally act as if they operate on their own? How would your daily life change if you consciously remembered that every detail of existence is upheld by Him every moment?
?
This lesson shows that even respected authorities like Ibn Sina offered multiple interpretations. Are you willing to humbly examine your own long-held beliefs, even if they were taught by trusted teachers, and possibly revise them in light of stronger evidence? What specific belief are you currently most resistant to re-examining, and what would it cost you to test it against the sources?

Classification of Perceptual Faculties: External and Internal Senses
The author classifies perceptual faculties into external and internal senses, each five, and addresses a related objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perceptual faculty is classified into external and internal senses, each comprising five specific senses, and the role of experience and demonstration in establishing them.

Core Idea
Perception is divided into two families of senses, each with five members.

This lesson introduces the basic classification of the perceptual faculty (al-mudrika) according to the author. He distinguishes between external senses (the familiar five) and internal senses, each set being five in number. However, he immediately notes that this number is not absolutely necessary—other senses may be possible but unrealized. The lesson also addresses a contention about whether pleasure and pain should be considered a separate faculty.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification statement
Read the first part of the passage that states the two divisions of the perceptual faculty and the number five.
2
Note the author's qualification
Observe that the author immediately adds that another sense is not impossible because a possible thing may not become actual if its conditions are absent.
3
Analyze the pleasure/pain objection
Understand the objection that pleasure and pain constitute a separate force, and the author's answer that these are perceptions, not separate perceivers.
The author limits the discussion to forces shared between humans and animals.
4
Grasp the genus relation
See how each division (external and internal) is a genus for five forces, just as the perceptual faculty is a genus for the two divisions.
External senses are known by introspection; internal senses require demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أما المدركة فالحواس الظاهرة والباطنة : وكل منهما خمس حسب ما ثبت بالوجدان ، والبرهان، وإن لم يقع الجزم بامتناع الغير لجواز أن لا يحصل للشيء بعض ما هو ممكن له لانتفاء شرط ، وجعل بعضهم مدرك اللذة والألم، بل جميع الوجدانيات قوة أخرى، لما نجد عند تحققها من حالة مغايرة لتعقلها، أو تخيلها . والجواب : إنها إدراكات لا مدركات . لأن الكلام في القوى التي يشترك فيها الانسان وغيره من الحيوانات، وأما القوة النطقية المدركة للكليات، فستأتي في بحث النفس وكل منهما. أي من قسمي القوة المدركة جنس أو بمنزلة الجنس ، لقوى خمسة . كما أن المدركة جنس أو بمنزلة الجنس للقسمين، وذلك ظاهر في الحواس الظاهرة لما أن كل أحد يجد من نفسه تلك الإدراكات، وتعقلها بما يخصها من الآلات، وأما الباطنة. فتثبت بالبرهان كما سيأتي على التفصيل، ثم لا جزم للعقل، بامتناع حاسة سادسة من الظاهرة أو الباطنة. إذ الممكن قد لا يوجد لانتفاء شرط من شرائط الوجود وما يقال أن الطبيعة لا تنتقل من درجة الحيوان إلى درجة فوقها، إلا وقد جميع ما في تلك الدرجة، فلو كان في الامكان آخر، لكان حس حاصلاً للإنسان، لأنه أعدل ما في هذا العالم ضعيف، وكذا ما يقال أن الإدراك استكملت
He said: As for the perceptual faculty, it consists of the external and internal senses: each of them is five according to what has been established by experience and demonstration, although there is no certainty that something else is impossible, because it is possible that a thing does not obtain some of what is possible for it due to the absence of a condition. And some have made the perceiver of pleasure and pain, indeed all affectivities, another force, because we find upon their realization a state different from understanding or imagining them. The answer: They are perceptions, not [separate] perceivers. Because the discussion is about the forces in which humans and other animals share. As for the rational power that perceives universals, it will come in the discussion of the soul. Each of them, i.e., each of the two divisions of the perceptual force, is a genus or like a genus for five forces, just as the perceptual is a genus or like a genus for the two divisions. That is apparent in the external senses because everyone finds in himself those perceptions and understands them by their specific instruments. As for the internal senses, they are established by demonstration as will come in detail. Then the intellect is not certain about the impossibility of a sixth sense, whether external or internal, because the possible may not exist due to the absence of a condition from the conditions of existence. And what is said that nature does not pass from the degree of animal to a degree above it unless it has completed all that is in that degree, so if there were another possible sense, it would be present in humans because he is the most balanced in this world—weak. Likewise what is said that perception has been perfected.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage classifies the perceptual faculty (al-mudrika) into two main divisions: external senses (al-ḥawāss al-ẓāhira) and internal senses (al-ḥawāss al-bāṭina). Each division is stated to consist of five senses, as established by experience (al-wijdān) and demonstration (al-burhān). However, the author immediately adds a qualification: there is no definitive certainty in the intellect that another sense is impossible, because it is possible that some potentialities remain unrealized due to missing conditions. The passage then addresses a specific claim: some philosophers consider the perception of pleasure and pain, and indeed all affectivities (wijdāniyyāt), as a separate force on the grounds that these experiences involve a distinct state different from conceptual understanding or imagination. The author answers that these are simply perceptions (idrākāt) and not separate perceivers (mudrikāt). The discussion is limited to forces shared between humans and other animals; the rational power that perceives universals (al-quwwa al-nuṭqiyya) is set aside for a later discussion on the soul. Each of the two divisions (external and internal) is like a genus for five specific forces, just as the perceptual faculty itself is a genus for the two divisions. The author notes that for external senses, this is obvious from introspection, while internal senses require demonstration. Finally, the author reiterates that the intellect cannot assert the impossibility of a sixth sense (external or internal) because a possible thing may not exist simply due to the absence of a necessary condition. The author briefly mentions and dismisses two arguments: (1) that nature cannot ascend from the animal level without completing all that is in that level (implying humans would have all possible senses), but this argument is weak; (2) that perception is perfected. The passage ends abruptly with 'استكملت' (has been perfected), likely part of the second argument.

المدركة
الحواس الظاهرة
الحواس الباطنة
الوجدان
البرهان
القوة النطقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure and pain constitute a separate perceptual force.
Correction: The author argues they are perceptions (idrākāt) within the existing faculties, not separate perceivers (mudrikāt).

Checkpoint
According to the author, why should pleasure and pain not be considered a separate perceptual force?
A) Because they are not real perceptions.
B) Because they are perceptions, not separate perceivers.
C) Because they are part of the rational power.
D) Because they are internal senses.

Key Takeaway
The perceptual faculty is divided into external and internal senses, each five in number, but this number is not absolute; additional senses may be possible but unrealized.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the five senses are known by experience and demonstration, but not necessary—other senses could exist. When have you assumed that your perception of reality is complete? What would change if you accepted that your senses might be missing something, and that Allah's knowledge is far greater? Will you humbly admit the limits of your own perception today?
?
The author distinguishes between the perception of pleasure/pain and a separate perceiving power. Think of a recent moment of intense pleasure or pain: did you treat it as a separate force controlling you, or as a perception that you can observe? How does seeing it as a perception rather than a perceiver change your relationship with your emotions? Commit to pausing before acting on a strong emotion this week and reminding yourself, 'This is a perception, not an independent power.'
?
This lesson shows that even the classification of senses is based on experience and demonstration, not on necessity. In your own religious understanding, are there beliefs you hold as 'necessary' that might actually be based on incomplete evidence? Are you willing to re-examine them with the same honesty the author shows when he says 'there is no certainty that something else is impossible'? What one assumption about your faith are you ready to test against the sources?

Possibility of Extra Senses and the Limits of Rational Certainty
The author argues against the impossibility of a sixth sense and refutes two counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intellect cannot assert the impossibility of a sixth sense and how the author refutes arguments that humans must have all possible senses.

Core Idea
The intellect cannot rule out a sixth sense; what is possible may not exist.

In this lesson, we examine the author's argument that the number of senses is not necessarily fixed. He explains that the intellect cannot be certain that a sixth external or internal sense is impossible, because a possible thing may not exist if its conditions are absent. He then briefly mentions and dismisses two opposing arguments, one based on the idea that nature completes all possibilities at each level, and another that perception is perfected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the statement that the intellect is not certain about the impossibility of a sixth sense.
2
Understand the reason
The possible may not exist due to absence of conditions.
3
Examine the first argument
Read the argument about nature not ascending without completeness, and note the author's dismissal as weak.
4
Consider the second argument
The argument that perception is perfected; note it's cut off but implies rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
