What Is Kalam?
beginner
5 min
The Perceptive Faculty: External and Internal Senses
al-Taftazani divides the perceptive faculty into external and internal senses, each five.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's classification of the perceptive faculty into five external and five internal senses, the basis for this division, and the response to the objection about pleasure and pain.

Core Idea
The perceptive faculty is two branches of five senses each, grounded in experience and proof.

In this passage, al-Taftazani establishes the framework for understanding how humans and animals perceive. He divides the perceptive faculty into external and internal senses, each numbering five, and clarifies that while this is based on observation and reasoning, we cannot be absolutely certain that no other senses exist. He also addresses an alternative view that treats pleasure and pain as a separate sense, offering a precise distinction to uphold the established classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: division, basis, objection, and answer.
The passage uses technical terms like الحواس الظاهرة (external senses) and الحواس الباطنة (internal senses).
2
Identify the two main divisions
The passage states that the perceptive faculty (المدركة) is divided into external and internal senses, each five in number. Look for the phrase 'وكل منهما خمس'.
3
Understand the basis and caveat
The division is based on empirical experience (الوجدان) and proof (البرهان). However, note the caveat: we are not certain that no other sense is possible, because something may not occur due to missing conditions.
This shows the careful epistemological approach of the author.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
Some scholars propose a separate sense for pleasure, pain, and affective states. Find the objection and the author's answer: these are perceptions (إدراكات), not percepts (مدركات), so they do not constitute a separate sense.
The answer refines the discussion to powers shared by humans and animals.
5
Grasp the genus-species relation
Each division (external/internal) is a genus for five specific sense powers, just as the perceptive faculty is a genus for these two divisions. This hierarchy is obvious for external senses, while internal senses require proof.
This sets up the detailed discussion of internal senses later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أما المدركة فالحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، وكل منهما خمس حسب ما ثبت بالوجدان، والبرهان، وإن لم يقع الجزم بامتناع الغير لجواز أن لا يحصل للشيء بعض ما هو ممكن له لانتفاء شرط، وجعل بعضهم مدرك اللذة والألم، بل جميع الوجدانيات قوة أخرى، لما نجد عند تحققها من حالة مغايرة لتعقلها، أو تخيلها . والجواب : إنها إدراكات لا مدركات. لأن الكلام في القوى التي يشترك فيها الانسان وغيره من الحيوانات، وأما القوة النطقية المدركة للكليات، فستأتي في بحث النفس وكل منهما. أي من قسمي القوة المدركة جنس أو بمنزلة الجنس ، لقوى خمسة . كما أن المدركة جنس أو بمنزلة الجنس للقسمين، وذلك ظاهر في الحواس الظاهرة لما أن كل أحد يجد من نفسه تلك الإدراكات، وتعقلها بما يخصها من الآلات، وأما الباطنة. فتثبت بالبرهان كما سيأتي على التفصيل.
He said: As for the perceptive faculty, it is the external and internal senses, and each of them is five according to what is established by empirical experience and proof, although there is no certainty regarding the impossibility of another (sense) because it is possible that some of what is possible for a thing does not occur due to the absence of a condition. And some have made the perceiver of pleasure and pain, rather all affective states, another power, because we find, when they are realized, a state different from conceptualizing them or imagining them. The answer: They are perceptions, not percepts. Because the discussion concerns the powers shared by humans and other animals. As for the rational power that perceives universals, it will come in the discussion of the soul. And each of the two divisions of the perceptive power is a genus or in the position of a genus for five powers. Just as the perceptive power is a genus or in the position of a genus for the two divisions. This is clear in the external senses, because everyone finds in themselves those perceptions and conceptualizes them by their specific instruments. As for the internal senses, they are established by proof, as will come in detail.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage defines the perceptive force (al-mudrika). It is divided into external and internal senses, each five in number, based on empirical experience and logical proof. However, the author notes we cannot be absolutely certain that no sixth sense exists, because some possible things may not occur due to missing conditions. He then addresses a brief objection: some consider pleasure, pain, and all affective states as a separate power. The answer is that these are perceptions (idrakat) not percepts (mudrakat), so they do not require a separate sense. The discussion here is limited to powers shared by humans and animals; the rational power for universals will be treated later. The passage then explains that each of the two divisions (external and internal) is a genus for five specific senses, just as the perceptive faculty is a genus for these two divisions. This is obvious for external senses, while internal senses will be proven later in detail.

Misconception: Pleasure, pain, and affective states constitute a separate sense faculty.
Correction: They are perceptions (إدراكات) not percepts (مدركات), so they do not require a separate sense. The discussion here concerns powers shared by animals, not the rational faculty.

The Imagination Faculty: Definition and Two Proofs
Defines imagination faculty with two proofs from distinction of functions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the imagination faculty (al-khayāl) and the two rational proofs that establish it as a distinct internal sense.

Core Idea
Imagination is the storehouse of sensory forms after common sense stops perceiving them.

In this passage, the author defines the imagination faculty (al-khayāl) and then presents two proofs for its existence as a distinct power. These proofs rely on the distinction between reception and preservation, and between absent-mindedness and forgetting. Understanding this faculty is essential for the broader theory of internal senses in classical Islamic psychology and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic definition: imagination is the faculty that preserves the forms of sensibles after they leave the common sense. Note the key verb 'يحفظ' (preserves).
The common sense is the faculty that receives sensory impressions.
2
Examine the first proof: preservation vs. reception
Identify the argument: reception and preservation are distinct acts, so they require distinct principles. Their combination in imagination is possible due to matter and power. The diversity of common sense perceptions comes from multiple transmission pathways, like the soul's various powers.
This proof is based on the principle of distinction of functions.
3
Examine the second proof: absent-mindedness vs. forgetting
Consider the second argument: a form in common sense may partially fade (absent-mindedness) but still be recoverable, unlike total forgetting. This implies a storage power separate from the receiving power.
If there were no separate storage, absent-mindedness would be identical to forgetting.
4
Reflect on the two proofs together
Notice that both proofs rely on distinguishing between two related but different states: reception vs. preservation, and partial loss vs. complete loss. This method is common in kalam for establishing distinct faculties.
The passage ends by noting both proofs are considered weak by some, but they are still presented as valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (منها الخيال) وهي التي تحفظ صور المحسوسات بعد غيبتها عن الحس المشترك ويدل عليها وجهان: الأول: أن الحفظ غير القبول، فلا بد له من مبدأ خاص واجتماعهما في الخيال يجوز أن يستند الى المادة والقوة، وتنوع إدراكات الحس المشترك يستند إلى كثرة طرق التأدية، كما أن إدراكات النفس وأفعالها يستند الى القوى. الثاني: أن الصورة المرتسمة في الحس المشترك قد تزول لا بالكلية كما النسيان، بل مع إمكان الاستحضار بأدنى التفات وهو الذهول، فلولا أنها مخزونة في قوة أخرى، لكان الذهول نسياناً وكلاهما ضعيف.
He said: 'Among them is imagination (al-khayāl), which preserves the forms of sensibles after their absence from the common sense. Two proofs indicate it: First, that preservation is not the same as reception, so it must have a specific principle, and their combination in imagination may be attributed to matter and power; and the diversity of perceptions of the common sense is attributed to the multiplicity of modes of transmission, just as the perceptions and actions of the soul are attributed to the powers. Second, that the form imprinted in the common sense may depart, not entirely as forgetting, but rather with the possibility of recall with the slightest attention—this is absent-mindedness (al-dhuhūl). So if it were not stored in another power, then absent-mindedness would be forgetting, and both are weak.'
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the imagination faculty (al-khayāl). It begins by quoting the original text: 'Among them is imagination, which preserves the forms of sensibles after their absence from the common sense.' This is a definition: imagination is the retentive faculty that stores sensory images once the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) is no longer perceiving them. Two proofs are given. First proof: Preservation is distinct from reception. The common sense receives impressions, but storing them requires a different principle. The combination of both receiving and storing in one faculty (imagination) is possible due to matter and power (substrate and disposition). The variety of common sense perceptions comes from multiple transmission pathways, just as the soul's perceptions and actions come from its various powers. Second proof: A form imprinted in the common sense may fade partially—not entirely like forgetting, but such that it can be recalled with minimal effort. This state is called 'absent-mindedness' (al-dhuhūl). If the form were not stored in another power, then absent-mindedness would actually be forgetting; but they are distinct. This proves a separate preservative faculty. The passage concludes by noting that both proofs are considered weak by some, but they are presented here.

Key Terms

الحس المشترك
الحفظ
القبول
الذهول
النسيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That imagination is merely a repetition of the common sense, not a distinct faculty.
Correction: The passage shows imagination is distinct because it preserves forms after common sense stops perceiving them, and absent-mindedness proves a separate storage power.

Checkpoint
What is the main function of the imagination faculty (al-khayāl)?
A) To receive sensory impressions
B) To preserve forms of sensibles after they leave the common sense
C) To judge between universals and particulars
D) To transmit sensations to the brain

Key Takeaway
Imagination is the internal sense that stores sensory forms after common sense perception ends, proven by the distinct acts of preservation and reception and by the phenomenon of absent-mindedness as distinct from forgetting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You receive countless sensory impressions and knowledge every day. Which of these have you truly preserved in your heart, and which have you merely received and then lost? What will you do today to ensure that the most important truths—especially those about Allah and your faith—are stored securely within you?
?
Think of a moment when you were absent-minded about a spiritual duty or a verse of the Quran you had learned. You knew it was there but could not recall it without effort. Does this not show that you have not truly preserved it? What steps will you take to move that knowledge from the edge of forgetting into the deep storage of your soul?
?
The proof shows that preservation requires a distinct faculty and effort. Your material state—your body, habits, distractions—affects how well you retain divine knowledge. Are your daily routines helping or hindering your preservation of what matters most? Name one concrete change you will make this week to protect your heart's storehouse.

The Wording of the Soul's Definition
Presentation of variant definitions and a grammatical clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key variant wordings in the definition of the soul and the grammatical issues that arise from misplacing the adjective 'natural'.

Core Idea
The precise wording of the soul's definition avoids confusion between natural and artificial perfections.

This lesson begins with Abu al-Barakat's proposed modification to the classic definition of the soul, replacing the term 'organic' with 'having life potentially'. The author then clarifies a common grammatical confusion about whether 'natural' describes 'perfection' or 'body'. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly grasping the definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two wordings
Read Abu al-Barakat's wording and the ancients' wording. Notice how 'natural' (طبيعي) is used differently in each.
Abu al-Barakat uses 'having life potentially' instead of 'organic'.
2
Understand the restriction of 'natural'
The author explains that 'natural' restricts the perfection to natural rather than artificial perfections, like shapes made by humans.
3
Analyze the grammatical confusion
Some scholars mistakenly raise 'natural' as an adjective for 'perfection' and then lower 'organic' for 'body'. The author says this is extremely ugly and clarifies the correct reading.
The correct reading is that 'natural' is an adjective for 'perfection' and 'organic' for 'body'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب أبو البركات إلى أنه إنما يذكر عوض قولهم : آلي فيقال : كمال أول طبيعي لجسم ذي حياة بالقوة وعبارة القدماء : كمال أول طبيعي لجسم آلي، واحترز بطبيعي عن الكمالات الصناعية، كالتشكيلات الحاصلة بفعل الإنسان . ثم قال : وقد يقال كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي بتأخير طبيعي، وهو إما غلط في النقل، وإما مقصود به المعنى الذي ذكرنا. فظهر أن ما يقال من أن بعضهم رفع طبيعي صفة لكمال، ليس معناه أنه يرفع مع التأخير صفة لكمال، ويخفض بعده آلي صفة لجسم، فإنه في غاية القبح ، وكذا لو رفع حينئذ آلي أيضاً، صفة مع ذكر ذي حياة صفة لجسم ، بل معناه أنه يقدم فيرفع على ما قال الإمام أن بعضهم جعل الطبيعي صفة للكمال فقال: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم أول آلي . فإن قيل : فعلى ما ذكر من أن قيد ذي حياة بالقوة لإخراج النفس السماوية يكون قولنا : كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي معنى شاملاً للأرضية والسماوية صالحاً لتعريفهما به. وقد صرحوا بأن إطلاق النفس عليهما بمحض اشتراك اللفظ. إذ الاولى باعتبار أفعال مختلفة، والثانية باعتبار فعل مستمر على نهج واحد، وأنه لا يتناولهما رسم واحد، إذ لو اقتصر على مبدائية فعل، ما دخلت صور البسائط والعنصريات، وإن اشترط القصد والإرادة خرجت النفس النباتية. وإن اعتبر اختلاف الافعال خرجت الفلكية . قلنا : مبني هذا التصريح على المذهب الصحيح . وهو أن لكل فلك نفساً وليس للنفوس السماوية اختلاف أفعال وآلات على أنه أيضاً موضع نظر لما ذكر في الشفاء من أن النفس اسم لمبدأ صدور أفاعيل ليست على وتيرة واحدة، عادمة للإرادة ولا خفاء في أنه معنى شامل لهما صالح لتعريفهما به على المذهبين، لأن فعل النفس السماوية أيضاً، ليس على نهج واحد، عادم للإرادة، بل على أنهاج مختلفة على رأي، وعلى نهج واحد مع الإرادة على الصحيح .
Abu al-Barakat held that instead of saying 'organic' (آلي) one says: 'first natural perfection for a body having life potentially' (كمال أول طبيعي لجسم ذي حياة بالقوة), and the expression of the ancients: 'first natural perfection for an organic body' (كمال أول طبيعي لجسم آلي). He restricted by 'natural' against artificial perfections, like shapes produced by human action. Then he said: 'It may be said: first perfection for a natural organic body with delaying 'natural', and this is either a mistake in transmission or intended with the meaning we mentioned.' Thus it appears that what is said—that some raise 'natural' as an adjective for 'perfection'—does not mean that with the delay he raises 'natural' as an adjective for 'perfection' and lowers after it 'organic' as an adjective for 'body', for that is extremely ugly; likewise if he also raised 'organic' at that time as an adjective while mentioning 'having life' as an adjective for the body. Rather, its meaning is that he precedes and raises, according to what al-Imam said: that some made 'natural' an adjective for 'perfection' and said: 'first natural perfection for a first organic body' (كمال أول طبيعي لجسم أول آلي). If it is said: Based on what was mentioned—that the restriction 'having life potentially' is for excluding the celestial soul—our statement 'first perfection for a natural organic body' would be a meaning encompassing the earthly and celestial ones, valid for defining both. And they have stated explicitly that applying 'soul' to them is mere homonymy (اشتراك اللفظ), since the first (earthly) is considered with different actions, and the second (celestial) with a continuous action on one pattern, and that no single definition (رسم) encompasses both, because if one restricts to originating an action, the forms of simple and elementary bodies would not be included; if one stipulates intention and will, the vegetative soul would be excluded; if one considers difference of actions, the celestial would be excluded. We say: This statement is based on the correct doctrine (المذهب الصحيح), which is that every sphere has a soul, and celestial souls have no difference in actions and instruments, although it is also subject to investigation, because of what is mentioned in al-Shifa: that 'soul' is a name for a principle of issuing acts not on one uniform pattern, lacking will, and it is clear that this meaning includes both and is valid for defining both according to both schools, because the action of the celestial soul also is not on one pattern, lacking will, but rather on different patterns according to one view, and on one pattern with will according to the correct view.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage begins by discussing variations in the definition of the soul (nafs). The author reports that Abu al-Barakat preferred saying 'first natural perfection of a body having life potentially' (كمال أول طبيعي لجسم ذي حياة بالقوة) instead of 'organic body' (جسم آلي). He explains that 'natural' restricts against artificial perfections. Then he notes that some scholars read the definition with different case endings, but the correct reading is that 'natural' is an adjective for 'perfection', not for 'body'. Then an objection (فإن قيل) is raised: if the phrase 'having life potentially' is meant to exclude the celestial soul, then the definition 'first perfection for a natural organic body' would include both earthly and celestial souls, yet scholars say the term 'soul' is applied to them only by homonymy (اشتراك اللفظ), because earthly souls have varied actions while celestial souls have a single uniform action. They claim no single definition can cover both. The answer (قلنا) is that this claim is based on the correct doctrine that every sphere has a soul and that celestial souls do not have varied actions or instruments. However, the author points out this is debatable, citing al-Shifa where the soul is defined as 'a principle of issuing acts not on one uniform pattern, lacking will'. This definition includes both kinds, because the celestial soul's action is also not on one pattern if one considers different opinions, or it is on one pattern with will according to the correct view. Thus the definition remains valid.

Key Terms
نفس
كمال أول
جسم آلي
ذي حياة بالقوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is wrong with raising 'natural' as an adjective for 'perfection' and then lowering 'organic' as an adjective for 'body'?
A) It changes the meaning of the definition
B) It is extremely ugly (في غاية القبح)
C) It is a valid variant reading
D) It was never transmitted

Key Takeaway
The correct wording of the soul's definition uses 'natural' as an adjective for 'perfection' and 'organic' as an adjective for 'body'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever slip into careless wording when explaining your beliefs? What small change in a phrase could lead someone to misunderstand the soul? Will you commit today to checking the accuracy of your words before you speak?
?
The author calls a misreading 'extremely ugly' — not just wrong, but repulsive to the intellect. When you encounter a mistaken belief or sloppy thinking in yourself, do you feel that same distaste? Or do you tolerate it? What will you do this week to uproot one mental sloppiness?
?
Precision in defining the soul ultimately affects your understanding of yourself and your purpose before Allah. How does the exact definition of the soul impact your daily life and your relationship with your Creator? Write one concrete step you will take to live in light of this truth.

Celestial Souls and the Homonymy Issue
Objection and answer regarding the inclusion of celestial souls in the soul's definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection about celestial souls is resolved by the correct doctrine that a single definition can encompass both earthly and celestial souls.

Core Idea
The term 'soul' is not mere homonymy; a unified definition works for both earthly and celestial souls.

This lesson tackles an objection: if the definition restricts 'having life potentially' to exclude celestial souls, then the alternative wording 'first perfection for a natural organic body' would include them, contradicting the claim that 'soul' is applied only by homonymy. The author answers by referring to the correct doctrine that every sphere has a soul and that celestial souls do not have varied actions, and he cites al-Shifa to support a unified definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that if the restriction 'having life potentially' excludes celestial souls, then the definition 'first perfection for a natural organic body' would include them, yet scholars say 'soul' is homonymous.
The objector concludes no single definition can cover both.
2
Examine the answer
The author says this claim is based on the correct doctrine (المذهب الصحيح) that every sphere has a soul and celestial souls have no difference in actions or instruments.
This implies a unified definition is possible.
3
Note the nuance from al-Shifa
The author adds that according to al-Shifa, the soul is defined as a principle of issuing acts not on one pattern, lacking will, which includes both earthly and celestial souls.
This supports the unified definition even if celestial souls have a different pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب أبو البركات إلى أنه إنما يذكر عوض قولهم : آلي فيقال : كمال أول طبيعي لجسم ذي حياة بالقوة وعبارة القدماء : كمال أول طبيعي لجسم آلي، واحترز بطبيعي عن الكمالات الصناعية، كالتشكيلات الحاصلة بفعل الإنسان . ثم قال : وقد يقال كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي بتأخير طبيعي، وهو إما غلط في النقل، وإما مقصود به المعنى الذي ذكرنا. فظهر أن ما يقال من أن بعضهم رفع طبيعي صفة لكمال، ليس معناه أنه يرفع مع التأخير صفة لكمال، ويخفض بعده آلي صفة لجسم، فإنه في غاية القبح ، وكذا لو رفع حينئذ آلي أيضاً، صفة مع ذكر ذي حياة صفة لجسم ، بل معناه أنه يقدم فيرفع على ما قال الإمام أن بعضهم جعل الطبيعي صفة للكمال فقال: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم أول آلي . فإن قيل : فعلى ما ذكر من أن قيد ذي حياة بالقوة لإخراج النفس السماوية يكون قولنا : كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي معنى شاملاً للأرضية والسماوية صالحاً لتعريفهما به. وقد صرحوا بأن إطلاق النفس عليهما بمحض اشتراك اللفظ. إذ الاولى باعتبار أفعال مختلفة، والثانية باعتبار فعل مستمر على نهج واحد، وأنه لا يتناولهما رسم واحد، إذ لو اقتصر على مبدائية فعل، ما دخلت صور البسائط والعنصريات، وإن اشترط القصد والإرادة خرجت النفس النباتية. وإن اعتبر اختلاف الافعال خرجت الفلكية . قلنا : مبني هذا التصريح على المذهب الصحيح . وهو أن لكل فلك نفساً وليس للنفوس السماوية اختلاف أفعال وآلات على أنه أيضاً موضع نظر لما ذكر في الشفاء من أن النفس اسم لمبدأ صدور أفاعيل ليست على وتيرة واحدة، عادمة للإرادة ولا خفاء في أنه معنى شامل لهما صالح لتعريفهما به على المذهبين، لأن فعل النفس السماوية أيضاً، ليس على نهج واحد، عادم للإرادة، بل على أنهاج مختلفة على رأي، وعلى نهج واحد مع الإرادة على الصحيح .
Abu al-Barakat held that instead of saying 'organic' (آلي) one says: 'first natural perfection for a body having life potentially' (كمال أول طبيعي لجسم ذي حياة بالقوة), and the expression of the ancients: 'first natural perfection for an organic body' (كمال أول طبيعي لجسم آلي). He restricted by 'natural' against artificial perfections, like shapes produced by human action. Then he said: 'It may be said: first perfection for a natural organic body with delaying 'natural', and this is either a mistake in transmission or intended with the meaning we mentioned.' Thus it appears that what is said—that some raise 'natural' as an adjective for 'perfection'—does not mean that with the delay he raises 'natural' as an adjective for 'perfection' and lowers after it 'organic' as an adjective for 'body', for that is extremely ugly; likewise if he also raised 'organic' at that time as an adjective while mentioning 'having life' as an adjective for the body. Rather, its meaning is that he precedes and raises, according to what al-Imam said: that some made 'natural' an adjective for 'perfection' and said: 'first natural perfection for a first organic body' (كمال أول طبيعي لجسم أول آلي). If it is said: Based on what was mentioned—that the restriction 'having life potentially' is for excluding the celestial soul—our statement 'first perfection for a natural organic body' would be a meaning encompassing the earthly and celestial ones, valid for defining both. And they have stated explicitly that applying 'soul' to them is mere homonymy (اشتراك اللفظ), since the first (earthly) is considered with different actions, and the second (celestial) with a continuous action on one pattern, and that no single definition (رسم) encompasses both, because if one restricts to originating an action, the forms of simple and elementary bodies would not be included; if one stipulates intention and will, the vegetative soul would be excluded; if one considers difference of actions, the celestial would be excluded. We say: This statement is based on the correct doctrine (المذهب الصحيح), which is that every sphere has a soul, and celestial souls have no difference in actions and instruments, although it is also subject to investigation, because of what is mentioned in al-Shifa: that 'soul' is a name for a principle of issuing acts not on one uniform pattern, lacking will, and it is clear that this meaning includes both and is valid for defining both according to both schools, because the action of the celestial soul also is not on one pattern, lacking will, but rather on different patterns according to one view, and on one pattern with will according to the correct view.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage begins by discussing variations in the definition of the soul (nafs). The author reports that Abu al-Barakat preferred saying 'first natural perfection of a body having life potentially' (كمال أول طبيعي لجسم ذي حياة بالقوة) instead of 'organic body' (جسم آلي). He explains that 'natural' restricts against artificial perfections. Then he notes that some scholars read the definition with different case endings, but the correct reading is that 'natural' is an adjective for 'perfection', not for 'body'. Then an objection (فإن قيل) is raised: if the phrase 'having life potentially' is meant to exclude the celestial soul, then the definition 'first perfection for a natural organic body' would include both earthly and celestial souls, yet scholars say the term 'soul' is applied to them only by homonymy (اشتراك اللفظ), because earthly souls have varied actions while celestial souls have a single uniform action. They claim no single definition can cover both. The answer (قلنا) is that this claim is based on the correct doctrine that every sphere has a soul and that celestial souls do not have varied actions or instruments. However, the author points out this is debatable, citing al-Shifa where the soul is defined as 'a principle of issuing acts not on one uniform pattern, lacking will'. This definition includes both kinds, because the celestial soul's action is also not on one pattern if one considers different opinions, or it is on one pattern with will according to the correct view. Thus the definition remains valid.

Key Terms
اشتراك اللفظ
المذهب الصحيح
نفس سماوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'soul' applies to earthly and celestial souls only by homonymy and that no single definition can cover both.
Correction: The author shows that the correct doctrine allows a unified definition, especially based on al-Shifa's broader definition.

Checkpoint
According to the author, on what is the claim that 'soul' is applied by mere homonymy based?
A) The opinion of al-Shifa
B) The correct doctrine (المذهب الصحيح)
C) The variation in wording
D) The grammatical analysis

Key Takeaway
A unified definition of the soul can encompass both earthly and celestial souls, contrary to the claim of mere homonymy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter differing opinions among scholars on matters of creed, do you tend to label them as mere word games, or do you dig deeper to find the underlying unity? This lesson shows that even a single term like 'soul' can generate real debate. What is one area of your faith where you have accepted a surface-level understanding without engaging with the depth of scholarly discussion? Will you commit this week to studying one such issue more thoroughly?
?
The objector in this passage believes that differences in actions make a unified definition impossible. Reflect on a relationship or situation in your life where you have assumed that differences between people or roles make common ground impossible. How might the author's insistence on a unified definition despite differences challenge your own assumptions? Are you willing to look for a deeper unity with someone you have written off as fundamentally different?
?
The correct doctrine here affirms that celestial souls have will, even though they act uniformly. This suggests that uniformity does not negate intention or purpose. Consider your own daily routines: do you perform them with conscious will and intention, or have they become mechanical? If your actions are uniform, does that mean your soul lacks will? This lesson forces you to examine the relationship between outward behavior and inner purpose. What one action will you perform today with renewed intention, breaking the pattern of unconscious habit?

Why Perfection Over Form and Power in Defining the Soul
Defense of 'perfection' over 'form' and 'power' in defining the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul is defined as 'perfection' (كمال) rather than 'form' (صورة) or 'power' (قوة), and how the author's reasoning defends this choice against objections.

Core Idea
Perfection is chosen because it directly refers to the soul's role in completing the species, while form and power are indirect or too material.

This lesson presents a classical objection to the definition of the soul as 'perfection' and the author's defense. The objector argues that 'form' and 'power' seem just as fitting. The author responds by clarifying that 'form' implies inherence in matter, which cannot apply to the abstract human soul, and that 'perfection' relates to the species—the very thing the soul completes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objector offers two alternatives: form (صورة) and power (قوة), each with a rationale.
The objection begins with 'فإن قيل'.
2
Identify the two alternative terms
The objector says the soul is a form because it is conjoined with matter, and a power because it is a principle of actions. Understand these two angles.
Form relates to composition with matter; power relates to efficient causality.
3
Study the answer regarding 'form'
The author gives two reasons: form literally means what inheres in matter, so it cannot apply to the abstract soul except figuratively. Also, form relates to matter, while perfection relates to the species—which is what the soul actually completes.
The key phrase: 'الكمال إلى النوع'.
4
Examine the brief treatment of 'power'
The author references al-Shifa and al-Imam to argue that referring to the species is more appropriate, implicitly answering the power objection by tying the principle of actions to the species completion.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: perfection captures both completion and active principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : النفس كما أنها كمال للجسم من حيث أنها به يتم ويتحصل نوعاً كذلك هي صورة له ، من حيث أنها تقارن المادة، فيحصل جوهر نباتي أو حيواني، وقوة له من حيث أنها مبدأ صدور أفعاله، فلم أوثر في تعريفهما الكمال على الصورة والقوة. وما ذكروا من أنا نجد بعض الأجسام يختص بصدور آثار مختلفة عنها، فيقطع بأن ذلك ليس بجسميتة المشتركة، بل لمبادىء خاصة نسميها نفساً، ربما يشعر بأن الأولى ذكر القوة . قلنا : أما إيثاره على الصورة فلأنها بالحقيقة اسم لما يحل المادة، فلا يتناول النفس الإنسانية المجردة، إلا بتجوز أو تجديد اصطلاح، ولأنها تقاس إلى المادة والكمال إلى النوع . ففي تعريف المعنى الذي به يتحصل الجسم فيصير أحد الأنواع ومصدر الأفعال، يكون المقيس إلى أمر هو نفس ذلك المتحصل أولى من المقيس إلى أمر بعيد لا يكون هو معه إلا بالقوة، ولا ينتسب إليه شيء من الأفاعيل. هذا ملخص كلام الشفاء. وتقدير الإمام أن المقيس إلى النوع أولى، لأن ما
If it is said: The soul is a perfection (كمال) for the body inasmuch as through it the body is completed and realizes itself as a species, likewise it is a form (صورة) for it inasmuch as it is conjoined with matter, so a vegetable or animal substance results, and it is a power (قوة) for it inasmuch as it is a principle of issuing its actions. So why was perfection favored over form and power in defining both? And what they mentioned—that we find some bodies characterized by issuing different effects from them, so we conclude that this is not due to their common corporeality, but to special principles we call 'soul'—perhaps suggests that mentioning power is more appropriate. We say: As for favoring it over form, it is because form is really a name for what inheres in matter, so it does not encompass the abstract human soul except figuratively or by new terminology, and because form is referred to matter while perfection is referred to the species. Thus in defining the meaning by which the body becomes realized and becomes one of the species and the source of actions, that which is referred to something identical with that realization is more appropriate than that which is referred to something distant that is only with it potentially and to which none of the actions belong. This is a summary of the words of al-Shifa. And al-Imam's estimation [is] that what is referred to the species is more appropriate, because...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (فإن قيل) and its answer (قلنا) regarding why the soul is defined primarily as 'perfection' (كمال) rather than 'form' (صورة) or 'power' (قوة). The objector notes that the soul can be considered in three aspects: as perfection (completing the body as a species), as form (combining with matter to form a substance), and as power (source of actions). They argue that since we infer the soul from observing different effects in bodies, 'power' seems more appropriate. The answer first explains why 'perfection' is preferred over 'form': form literally means what inheres in matter, so it cannot apply to the abstract human soul except metaphorically or by a new convention. Moreover, form relates to matter while perfection relates to the species—the essence of what the body becomes. In defining that by which the body becomes realized and becomes a species and source of actions, the term that directly refers to that realization (perfection) is more fitting than a term referring to something external and secondary (form). The passage then notes that this is a summary from al-Shifa, and adds that al-Imam also considered that referring to the species is more appropriate. The argument concludes with the idea that perfection is the primary term because it captures both the completion of the body's species and its active principle, while form and power are either too narrow or indirect.

Key Terms
كمال
صورة
قوة
نوع
مادة
النفس الإنسانية المجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'form' and 'power' are equally valid terms for defining the soul, and that the choice of 'perfection' is arbitrary.
Correction: The author shows that 'form' is inappropriate because it implies inherence in matter, which does not apply to the abstract soul, and 'power' is indirect because it focuses on actions rather than the soul's primary role of completing the species.

Checkpoint
Why is 'perfection' (كمال) preferred over 'form' (صورة) in defining the soul?
A) Because form cannot apply to the abstract human soul except figuratively
B) Because form is not mentioned in al-Shifa
C) Because power is more important than form
D) Because perfection is related to matter

Key Takeaway
The soul's definition as 'perfection' is preferred because it directly links to its function of completing the species, avoiding the material connotations of 'form' and the indirectness of 'power'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that the soul is best defined as 'perfection' rather than 'form' or 'power'. Think about your own life: Are you more concerned with your outward form (appearance, status) or the power you wield (abilities, achievements)? How does prioritizing the perfection of your soul—completing what you were created to be—change your daily decisions? Commit to one specific area where you will focus on inner perfection over outer form or power this week.
?
The passage criticizes defining the soul by 'power' because it is indirect and does not capture the essence. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes reduce your spiritual life to 'actions'—prayer, charity, etc.—without nurturing the inner completion of your soul? What is one step you can take this week to seek the perfection of your soul (kamal) rather than just performing acts?
?
The author insists that form is too material to describe the abstract soul. Have you ever allowed worldly forms—your job, your possessions, your relationships—to define your identity more than the state of your soul? What practical change can you make today to shift your self-definition from what you have or achieve to who you are becoming as a soul?

The Mutakallimun's View of the Soul
Taftazani states the mutakallimun's doctrine that the soul is a subtle body pervading the physical body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's conception of the human soul as a subtle body that courses through the physical body without changing or decomposing.

Core Idea
The soul is a subtle body that courses through the body, unchanged.

This passage presents the theological (mutakallim) view of the soul, distinguishing it from the physical body. Taftazani explains that while bodies are composed of homogeneous substances and subject to change, the soul is a subtle body that remains constant. This sets the stage for understanding the soul's nature in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice the contrast between the mutakallimun's view and the philosophers'.
The key term 'jism latif' is central.
2
Identify the definition of the soul
Find the sentence that defines the soul as 'jism latif sār fī al-badan' and understand its implications.
The text also offers an alternative: 'al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya al-bāqiya'.
3
Compare with the philosophers' view
The passage briefly mentions the philosophers' view of a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad). Consider how it differs from the mutakallimun's subtle body.
This contrast highlights the unique position of the mutakallimun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما عندنا) فاستناد الآثار إلى القادر، واختلاف الأجسام بالعوارض، بكونها من جواهر متجانسة، إلا أن النصوص شهدت بأن للإنسان روحاً وراء هذا الهيكل المحسوس الدائم التبدل والتحلل، وكادت الضرورة تقتضي بذلك، ولو بأدنى بنية، وهو المراد بالنفس الإنسانية، والمعتمد من آراء المتكلمين إنها جسم لطف سار في البدن لا يتبدل ولا يتحلل أو الأجزاء الأصلية الباقية، التي لا تقوم الحياة بأقل منها، وكأنه المراد بالهيكل المحسوس، والبنية المحسوسة: أي من شأنها أن تحس، ومن آراء الفلاسفة وكثير من المسلمين أنها جوهر مجرد متصرف في البدن، متعلق أولا بروح قلبي، يسري في البدن، فيفيض على الأعضاء قواها.
He said: 'As for our view, the attribution of effects to the Powerful, and the difference of bodies by accidents, is because they are of homogeneous substances. However, the texts testified that man has a spirit behind this sensible structure that is constantly changing and decomposing, and necessity almost demanded that, even with the least constitution, and that is what is meant by the human soul. And the relied-upon view among the mutakallimun is that it is a subtle body that courses through the body, not changing nor decomposing, or the original remaining parts, without which life is not established, and it seems that this is what is meant by the sensible structure and the sensible constitution: i.e., that it is capable of sensing. And among the views of the philosophers and many Muslims, it is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) managing the body, primarily attached to a cardiac spirit, coursing through the body, and directing its powers to the organs.'
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's view of the soul. It begins by stating that, in their view, effects are attributed to the omnipotent Creator, and bodies differ by accidents while being composed of homogeneous substances. However, the revealed texts indicate that man has a spirit (ruh) beyond the sensible body that constantly changes. The soul is defined as a subtle body (jism latif) that pervades the physical body and does not undergo change or decomposition. Alternatively, some say it consists of the remaining original parts without which life cannot be sustained. It is what is meant by the sensible structure that is capable of sensing. The passage then contrasts this with the philosophers' view that the soul is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) that manages the body through a cardiac spirit.

Key Terms
جسم لطف
أجزاء أصلية
هيكل محسوس
بنية محسوسة
جوهر مجرد
روح قلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the mutakallimun denied the existence of a non-material soul, but they affirmed a subtle body that is not subject to change.
Correction: The mutakallimun did not deny the soul; rather, they defined it as a subtle body (jism latif) that is distinct from the physical body and not subject to change or decomposition.

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view of the mutakallimun, what is the human soul?
A) A simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) that manages the body.
B) A cardiac spirit that directs the organs.
C) A subtle body (jism latif) that courses through the body without changing.
D) The physical body itself.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun define the soul as a subtle body that pervades the body unchanged, distinct from the constantly changing physical body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You have just learned that the mutakallimun describe your soul as a subtle, unchanging body that courses through your constantly decaying physical shell. If this is true, why do you spend almost all your energy on the shell that decays and almost none on the soul that remains? What one change in your daily routine would reflect that the soul is what truly matters?
?
The soul in this view is a subtle body that 'courses through' every part of you and is capable of sensing. Do you live as if your soul is alive and aware in every moment, or do you deaden it by heedlessness? When did you last feel your soul sensing something beyond the physical – and what did you do about it?
?
Taftazani says the soul does not change or decompose, yet you often act as if your spiritual state is at the mercy of your changing body – your mood, health, or circumstances. If the soul is truly unchanging in essence, then why do you let temporary physical states define your relationship with your Lord? Will you today treat your soul as the permanent reality it is, or continue to be ruled by the temporary?

Two Definitions of the Soul: Corporeal or Immaterial?
Sanusi presents the mutakallimun's definition and the philosophers' definition of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing definitions of the soul according to the mutakallimun (corporeal) and the philosophers (immaterial), and why this disagreement matters.

Core Idea
The soul: a subtle body or an immaterial substance?

Before debating proofs, we need to know what the soul is. The mutakallimun and philosophers give sharply different definitions—one sees it as a subtle body permeating the physical, the other as a non-physical substance managing the body. This lesson introduces both views.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the key terms: جسم لطيف and جوهر مجرد.
The definitions are presented consecutively without comment.
2
Compare the two views
Contrast the mutakallimun's definition (subtle body, unchanging) with the philosophers' (immaterial substance, acting via cardiac spirit). Note the difference in essence.
3
Reflect on implications
Ask yourself: what difference would it make if the soul is physical or non-physical? How might this affect arguments about the afterlife?
This helps connect to larger theological issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد من آراء المتكلمين . إنها جسم لطف سار في البدن لا يتبدل ولا يتحلل أو الأجزاء الأصلية الباقية، التي لا تقوم الحياة بأقل منها، وكأنه المراد بالهيكل المحسوس، والبنية المحسوسة : أي من شأنها أن تحس ومن آراء الفلاسفة وكثير من المسلمين، أنها جوهر مجرد متصرف في البدن، متعلق أولا بروح قلبي، يسري في البدن، فيفيض على الأعضاء قواها لنا وجوه : الأول: إنا نحكم بالكلي على الجزئي فيلزم أن ندركهما، ومدرك الجزئي منه هو الجسم، ليس إلا كما في سائر الحيوانات. الثاني : أن كل أحد يقطع بأن المشار إليه بأنا حاضر هناك وقائم وقاعد، وما ذلك إلا الجسم . الثالث : لو كانت مجردة لكانت نسبتها إلى الأبدان على السواء، فجاز أن ينتقل فلا يكون ريد الآن هو الذي كان، والكل ضعيف . الرابع : ظواهر النصوص، ولا تفيد القطع
The adopted view of the mutakallimun is that it (the soul) is a subtle body flowing in the body, not changing or dissolving, or the original remaining parts, which life does not subsist with fewer than them, and it is as if meant by the sensible structure, and the sensible constitution: i.e., that which perceives. And among the views of the philosophers and many Muslims is that it is an immaterial substance acting in the body, primarily attached to a cardiac spirit, flowing in the body, pouring forth onto the limbs their powers. For us are proofs: First: we judge the universal upon the particular, so it necessitates that we perceive both, and the perceiver of the particular is the body, as in other animals. Second: that everyone is certain that the referent of 'I' is present there, standing and sitting, and that is nothing but the body. Third: if it were immaterial, its relation to bodies would be equal, so it could transfer, and thus Zayd would not be the same as before, and all of them are weak. Fourth: the apparent meanings of the texts, and they do not yield certainty.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage defines the soul (nafs) and presents four proofs for its corporeality. Begin by stating the two main definitions: the mutakallimun say it is a subtle body permeating the physical body, or the original parts that remain from birth to death, while philosophers and many Muslims say it is an immaterial substance that manages the body, first attaching to a cardiac spirit. Then four arguments (wujuh) are given by those favoring corporeality. The first argument is that we judge universals upon particulars, so we must perceive both; the particular is perceived by the body, so the soul must be a body. The second argument is that everyone knows the 'I' refers to the body present here. The third argument is that if the soul were immaterial, it would relate equally to all bodies, so it could transfer, but we know Zayd remains the same person. The author notes 'all of them are weak' after the third. The fourth argument is from scriptural appearances, but the author immediately notes they do not yield certainty. The passage thus sets up the debate without endorsing these arguments.

Key Terms
نفس
جسم لطيف
جوهر مجرد
روح قلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all Islamic schools agree the soul is immaterial.
Correction: The passage shows that the mutakallimun (theologians) defined it as a subtle body, while philosophers and many Muslims defined it as immaterial.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, what is the soul?
A) An immaterial substance
B) A subtle body or original parts
C) The physical heart
D) The intellect

Key Takeaway
The soul is defined either as a corporeal subtle body (mutakallimun) or an immaterial substance (philosophers).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every day you act as if your soul is either a material thing or a spiritual thing. Which definition, if embraced fully, would make you more heedful of your sins and more hopeful of God's mercy? Write down one concrete change you will make today based on the correct understanding.

Four Rational Proofs for the Soul's Corporeality
Sanusi enumerates four rational proofs for the soul's corporeality and judges them weak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four arguments for the soul's corporeality and explain why the author considers them weak.

Core Idea
Four arguments for a bodily soul—all ultimately weak.

Now that we have both definitions, we turn to the evidence. Supporters of corporeality present four arguments, including one from perception and one from personal identity. However, the author notes that the third argument is 'weak' and the fourth from scripture is not decisive. Let's examine each.

Guided Path
1
Read the four proofs
Read the Arabic passage and translation for the four proofs (wujuh). Notice the author's comment after the third.
2
Understand proof 1 (universal-particular)
The first argument: we judge universals on particulars, so we perceive both; the particular is perceived by the body, so the soul must be bodily. Think about whether this follows logically.
3
Understand proof 2 (self-awareness)
The second argument: everyone knows that 'I' refers to the body present here, so the soul is the body. Reflect on common experience.
4
Understand proofs 3 and 4, and note weakness
The third argument: if immaterial, the soul could transfer, but identity stays; so it's corporeal. The author says 'all of them are weak' after this. The fourth is from scripture but not certain. Note the author's evaluation.
Consider why these arguments are considered weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد من آراء المتكلمين . إنها جسم لطف سار في البدن لا يتبدل ولا يتحلل أو الأجزاء الأصلية الباقية، التي لا تقوم الحياة بأقل منها، وكأنه المراد بالهيكل المحسوس، والبنية المحسوسة : أي من شأنها أن تحس ومن آراء الفلاسفة وكثير من المسلمين، أنها جوهر مجرد متصرف في البدن، متعلق أولا بروح قلبي، يسري في البدن، فيفيض على الأعضاء قواها لنا وجوه : الأول: إنا نحكم بالكلي على الجزئي فيلزم أن ندركهما، ومدرك الجزئي منه هو الجسم، ليس إلا كما في سائر الحيوانات. الثاني : أن كل أحد يقطع بأن المشار إليه بأنا حاضر هناك وقائم وقاعد، وما ذلك إلا الجسم . الثالث : لو كانت مجردة لكانت نسبتها إلى الأبدان على السواء، فجاز أن ينتقل فلا يكون ريد الآن هو الذي كان، والكل ضعيف . الرابع : ظواهر النصوص، ولا تفيد القطع
The adopted view of the mutakallimun is that it (the soul) is a subtle body flowing in the body, not changing or dissolving, or the original remaining parts, which life does not subsist with fewer than them, and it is as if meant by the sensible structure, and the sensible constitution: i.e., that which perceives. And among the views of the philosophers and many Muslims is that it is an immaterial substance acting in the body, primarily attached to a cardiac spirit, flowing in the body, pouring forth onto the limbs their powers. For us are proofs: First: we judge the universal upon the particular, so it necessitates that we perceive both, and the perceiver of the particular is the body, as in other animals. Second: that everyone is certain that the referent of 'I' is present there, standing and sitting, and that is nothing but the body. Third: if it were immaterial, its relation to bodies would be equal, so it could transfer, and thus Zayd would not be the same as before, and all of them are weak. Fourth: the apparent meanings of the texts, and they do not yield certainty.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage defines the soul (nafs) and presents four proofs for its corporeality. Begin by stating the two main definitions: the mutakallimun say it is a subtle body permeating the physical body, or the original parts that remain from birth to death, while philosophers and many Muslims say it is an immaterial substance that manages the body, first attaching to a cardiac spirit. Then four arguments (wujuh) are given by those favoring corporeality. The first argument is that we judge universals upon particulars, so we must perceive both; the particular is perceived by the body, so the soul must be a body. The second argument is that everyone knows the 'I' refers to the body present here. The third argument is that if the soul were immaterial, it would relate equally to all bodies, so it could transfer, but we know Zayd remains the same person. The author notes 'all of them are weak' after the third. The fourth argument is from scriptural appearances, but the author immediately notes they do not yield certainty. The passage thus sets up the debate without endorsing these arguments.

جسم
أدلة عقلية
كل
جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think these proofs are strong and conclusive.
Correction: The author explicitly labels the third argument 'weak' and says scriptural appearances do not yield certainty, indicating these are not decisive.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the third argument for the soul's corporeality?
A) It is strong
B) It is uncertain
C) It is weak
D) It is scriptural

Key Takeaway
The four arguments for corporeality are presented but all are considered weak or non-decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim 'I know this is true,' what are you anchoring that certainty on—an argument you've examined or a feeling you've inherited? How would you respond if that argument turned out to be weak?
?
The author dismisses these proofs as weak. Is there any belief you hold about yourself (your soul, your identity) that you've never tested with rational scrutiny? What would it cost you to examine it honestly?
?
You just read that even the apparent meanings of sacred texts do not yield certainty. How does that sit with you? Are you willing to hold your deepest convictions with humility, knowing they may rest on arguments that others—or even you—might later judge weak?

Refuting the Proof of the Soul's Specific Unity
Refutes the argument that a single definition of soul implies specific unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that a single definition of the soul implies all souls are essentially identical is weak, and how the author refutes it using the distinction between specific unity and generic unity.

Core Idea
A single definition does not prove specific unity; generic terms also have one definition.

The majority of theologians argued that since the soul has a single definition (e.g., 'an abstract substance attached to the body'), it must be that all souls share one essence. The author rejects this inference, pointing out that even generic categories like 'animal' have a single definition yet encompass many species. In this passage, he presents the majority's proof and his refutation, clarifying that a shared definition alone is not enough to establish specific unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repetition in the Arabic; it may be a typo in the source.
2
Identify the two sides
Note the majority's claim: a single definition of the soul implies all souls share one essence (specific unity). Then note the author's response: this is weak because generic terms also have a single definition.
The author cites Abu al-Barakat for the chosen view.
3
Analyze the refutation
Focus on the example of 'animal' as a generic term. Understand how a single definition (e.g., 'sentient body moving by will') does not mean all animals are one species. Then consider the second argument about differentiae and the author's answer about conceptual composition not contradicting abstraction.
The key insight: a definition answers 'what is it?' for a species, but not necessarily for every individual without a differentiator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب هو المختار عندنا. وإما بمعنى أن يكون كل فرد منها مخالفاً بالماهية لسائر الأفراد حتى لا يشترك منهم اثنان في الحقيقة . فلم يقل به قائل تصريحاً. كذا ذكره أبو البركات في المعتبر. احتج الجمهور بأن ما يعقل من النفس، ويجعل حداً لها معنى واحد ، مثل الجوهر المجرد المتعلق بالبدن، والحد تمام الماهية وهذا ضعيف لأن مجرد التحديد بحد واحد، لا التحديد بحد واحد، لا يوجب الوحدة النوعية. إذ المعاني الجنسية أيضاً كذلك كقولنا الحيوان جسم حساس متحرك بالإرادة وإن ادعى أن هذا مقول في جواب السؤال بما هو عن أي فرد، وأي طائفة تفرض فهو ممنوع، بل ربما يحتاج إلى ضم مميز جوهري، وقد يحتج بأنها متشاركة في كونها نفوساً بشرية، فلو تخالفت بفصول مميزة لكانت من المركبات دون المجردات . والجواب بعد تسليم كون النفسية من الذاتيان دون العرضيات : أن التركيب العقلي من الجنس والفصل لا ينافي التجرد ، ولا يستلزم الجسمية.
The doctrine is the chosen one according to us. Or in the sense that every individual of them is essentially different from the rest of the individuals so that no two of them share in the reality. So no one stated it explicitly. Thus Abu al-Barakat mentioned it in al-Mu'tabar. The majority argued that what is understood from the soul, and is made its definition, is a single meaning, like the abstract substance attached to the body, and the definition is the entirety of the essence. And this is weak, because mere definition by a single definition, not the definition by a single definition, does not necessitate specific unity. Since generic meanings are also like that, as we say 'animal is a sentient body moving by will'. And if it is claimed that this is said in answer to the question 'what is it?' concerning any individual, and any group you suppose, it is denied. Rather, it may need the addition of a substantial differentiator. And it may be argued that they share in being human souls, so if they differed by differentiating differentiae, they would be among composites, not abstracts. The answer, after conceding that soul-ness is among the essential attributes not the accidental ones, is that conceptual composition of genus and differentia does not contradict abstraction, nor does it necessitate corporeality.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument by the majority (جمهور) that the soul has a single definition and therefore all souls are of one specific type (specific unity). The author begins by stating that the chosen doctrine (المختار) is that individuals of the soul are essentially different. He cites Abu al-Barakat. Then he presents the majority's proof: the soul is understood as a single meaning (e.g., abstract substance attached to the body), and a definition expresses the entire essence; thus all souls share one essence. The author refutes this: a single definition does not guarantee specific unity because generic terms (like 'animal') also have a single definition but cover many species. If one claims that the definition answers 'what is it?' for any individual, that is denied; a differentiator may be needed. Another argument is that all souls share in being human souls, so if they differed by differentiae, they would be material composites. The author answers: even if soul-ness is an essential attribute, conceptual composition from genus and differentia does not prevent abstraction nor imply corporeality. The refutation is that a shared definition is not sufficient for specific unity.

التركيب العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single definition of the soul proves all souls are identical in essence (specific unity).
Correction: A single definition is also true for generic terms like 'animal', which cover many species. Therefore, a single definition does not guarantee specific unity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the majority's proof that the soul has specific unity?
A) Because the soul is not definable.
B) Because generic terms also have a single definition but cover multiple species.
C) Because the soul is a body.
D) Because the definition is incomplete.

Key Takeaway
A single definition does not guarantee that all individuals share the same essence; generic terms illustrate this, and conceptual composition from genus and differentia does not contradict abstraction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If everyone's soul is essentially different, how does that affect your responsibility towards others? Are you more or less accountable for how you treat them? Describe one concrete change you will make in how you view or interact with a specific person because of this truth.

Diverse Meanings of Wisdom in Early Islam
The author cites multiple scholars' definitions of 'al-hikmah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse early interpretations of the term 'al-hikmah' (wisdom) and how they encompass knowledge, piety, intellect, and prophethood.

Core Idea
Wisdom in Islam is not just intellect; it includes fear of God, understanding, and the Quran and Sunnah.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid collects a variety of definitions of 'al-hikmah' from early scholars. Each definition highlights a different aspect, showing that wisdom was understood broadly. As you read, notice how each scholar emphasizes a different element, from knowledge and understanding to piety and even prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get an overview of the different opinions.
Notice the range from knowledge to fear of God.
2
List each scholar's definition
Go through the passage and write down each definition: what did each scholar say wisdom is? Identify the speaker and the key term (e.g., 'ilm, fahm, khashyah, 'aql, etc.).
There are at least seven distinct opinions.
3
Categorize the definitions
Group the definitions into categories: those emphasizing knowledge (Quran, fiqh, understanding), those emphasizing piety (fear of God), those emphasizing intellect, and those emphasizing revelation (Sunnah, prophethood).
This helps see the multidimensional nature of wisdom.
4
Examine the hadith about Quran memorization
Reflect on the hadith: 'Whoever memorizes the Quran, prophethood has been inserted between his shoulders.' How does this relate to the definitions?
It connects wisdom to divine knowledge.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Consider how these definitions are not contradictory but complementary. Wisdom combines knowledge, practice, and spiritual insight.
This reflects the holistic Islamic worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرى بعض العلماء أنها ليست النبوة ولكنه العلم والفقه والقرآن، وقال أبو العالية : الحكمة: خشية الله فإن خشية الله رأس كل حكمة، وقد روى ابن مردويه من طريق بقية عن عثمان بن زفر الجهني عن أبي عمار الأساسي عن ابن مسعود مرفوعاً : رأس الحكمة مخافة الله . وقال أبو العالية في رواية عنه : المحكمة الكتاب والفهم. وقال إبراهيم النخبي :الحكمة: الفهم، وقال أبو مالك: الحكمة السنة، وقال ابن وهب عن مالك. قال زيد بن أسلم الحكمة العقل . قال مالك، وإنه ليقع في قلبي أن الحكمة هو الفقه في دين الله وأمر يدخله الله في القلوب من رحمته وفضله. وقال السدى الحكمة النبوة. وجاء في بعض الأحاديث: من حفظ القرآن فقد أدرجت النبوة بين كتفيه غير أنه لا يوحي إليه.
Some scholars hold that it is not prophethood but rather knowledge, jurisprudence, and the Quran. Abu al-‘Aliyah said: Wisdom is fear of God, for fear of God is the head of all wisdom. And Ibn Mardawayh narrated via Baqiyyah from ‘Uthman ibn Zafar al-Juhani from Abu ‘Ammar al-Asadi from Ibn Mas‘ud raised [to the Prophet]: 'The head of wisdom is fear of God.' And Abu al-‘Aliyah in another narration said: The firm [scripture] is the Book and understanding. Ibrahim al-Nakha‘i said: Wisdom is understanding. Abu Malik said: Wisdom is the Sunnah. Ibn Wahb reported from Malik, who said: Zayd ibn Aslam said: Wisdom is intellect. Malik said: It occurs to my heart that wisdom is understanding in the religion of God and a matter that God causes to enter hearts from His mercy and bounty. Al-Suddi said: Wisdom is prophethood. And it is reported in some hadith: 'Whoever memorizes the Quran, prophethood has been inserted between his shoulders, but he is not inspired.'
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage collects various early interpretations of the term 'al-hikmah' (wisdom) from leading scholars. Some say it is not prophethood but rather knowledge, jurisprudence (fiqh), and the Quran. Abu al-‘Aliyah emphasizes fear of God as the essence of wisdom, citing a hadith. Another view equates wisdom with the Book (Quran) and understanding. Ibrahim al-Nakha‘i defines it as understanding (fahm). Abu Malik identifies it with the Sunnah. Zayd ibn Aslam and Malik consider it intellect or deep understanding of religion. Al-Suddi says it is prophethood. A hadith states that memorizing the Quran bestows a measure of prophethood without revelation. This variety shows that wisdom was understood differently by different early authorities.

Key Terms

الفقه
القرآن
خشية الله
السنة
العقل
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wisdom is purely intellectual acumen or philosophical knowledge.
Correction: Early Islamic scholars defined wisdom broadly to include fear of God, understanding of religion, the Quran, the Sunnah, and even a share of prophethood.

Checkpoint

A) The Quran
B) The Sunnah
C) Intellect
D) Fear of God

Key Takeaway
The early scholars understood wisdom as encompassing knowledge, piety, intellect, and religious understanding, not merely intellectual acumen.

?

Definitions of Theoretical and Practical Wisdom
Defines theoretical and practical wisdom and their comprehensive interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of theoretical and practical wisdom according to the classical kalam tradition, and how wisdom is interpreted as the soul's perfection in knowledge and action.

Core Idea
Wisdom is both knowing reality and acting rightly.

The author begins by distinguishing two aspects of human perfection: theoretical wisdom, which is knowing things as they are, and practical wisdom, which is doing things as they should be. He then explains that wisdom encompasses both, as the soul's actualization of its potential in knowledge and action. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding the broader concept of wisdom in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the precise phrasing for each type of wisdom.
Identify the key terms: حكمة نظرية and حكمة عملية.
2
Understand the comprehensive interpretation
The author then says that wisdom was interpreted to include both, as the soul's emergence from potentiality to actuality.
This ties wisdom to both knowledge and action.
3
Reflect on the role of human capacity
Both definitions include the phrase 'بقدر الطاقة البشرية' – to the extent of human capacity. Consider why this limitation is important.
It acknowledges human limitation while affirming the pursuit of perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن كمال القوة النظرية معرفة أعيان الموجودات وأحوالها وأحكامها كما هي، أي على الوجه الذي هي عليه، وفي نفس الأمر بقدر الطاقة البشرية، وتسمى حكمة نظرية، وكمال القوة العملية القيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي. أي على الوجه الذي يرتضيه العقل الصحيح، بقدر الطاقة البشرية، وتسمى حكمة عملية، وفسروا الحكمة على ما يشمل القسمين، بأنها خروج النفس من القوة إلى الفعل، في كمالها الممكن علماً وعملاً، إلا أنه لما كثر الخلاف، وفشا الباطل والضلال في شأن الكمال، وفي كون الأشياء كما هي، والأمور على ما ينبغي لزم الاقتداء في ذلك بمن ثبت بالمعجزات الباهرة، أنهم على هدى من تعالى، وكانت الحكمة الحقيقية هي الشريعة، لكن لا بمعنى مجرد الأحكام العملية، بل بمعنى معرفة النفس ما لها وما عليها والعمل بها على ما ذهب إليه أهل التحقيق من أن الحكمة المشار إليها في قوله تعالى: ﴿ ومن يؤت الحكمة فقد أوتى خيراً كثيراً ﴾ هو الفقه، وأنه اسم للعلم والعمل جميعاً. وقد قسم المفسرة بمعرفة الأشياء كما هي إلى النظرية والعملية، لأنها إن كانت علماً بالأمور المتعلقة لقدرتنا واختيارنا فعملية، وغايتها العمل وتحصيل الخير، وإلا فنظرية وغايتها إدراك الحق، وكل منهما ينقسم بالقسمة الأولية إلى ثلاثة أقسام.
That the perfection of the theoretical faculty is knowledge of the essences of existents, their states, and their judgments as they are, i.e., in the manner they are in themselves, to the extent of human capacity, and it is called theoretical wisdom. And the perfection of the practical faculty is the undertaking of affairs as they ought to be, i.e., in the manner approved by sound intellect, to the extent of human capacity, and it is called practical wisdom. And they interpreted wisdom to encompass both divisions, as the soul's emergence from potentiality to actuality in its possible perfection in knowledge and action. But when disagreement increased and falsehood and error became widespread concerning perfection and concerning things being as they are and affairs being as they ought to be, it became necessary to follow in that those who were established by overwhelming miracles to be upon guidance from the Exalted. And true wisdom is the Sharia, not in the sense of mere practical rulings, but in the sense of the soul's knowing what is for it and against it and acting accordingly, according to what the people of verification held: that the wisdom referred to in His statement, the Exalted, 'And whoever is given wisdom has been given much good' (Quran 2:269) is fiqh, and that it is a name for knowledge and action together. And the interpreters divided knowledge of things as they are into theoretical and practical; for if it is knowledge of matters related to our power and choice, it is practical, and its goal is action and the attainment of good; otherwise it is theoretical, and its goal is the apprehension of truth. And each of them is divided by the primary division into three divisions.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage defines the two main branches of wisdom: theoretical and practical. Theoretical wisdom is the knowledge of existents as they truly are, to the extent possible for humans. Practical wisdom is the performance of actions as they should be, according to sound reason. The author notes that wisdom was interpreted to include both, and that true wisdom is ultimately the Sharia, not just theoretical knowledge but a combination of knowledge and action, as indicated by the Quranic verse on wisdom being 'fiqh'. The passage then states that both branches are each further divided into three parts, setting up the subsequent detailed classification.

Key Terms
حكمة نظرية
حكمة عملية
خروج النفس من القوة إلى الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wisdom is only theoretical knowledge about reality.
Correction: Wisdom includes both theoretical knowledge and practical action, as seen in the definitions and the comprehensive interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is theoretical wisdom?
A) Knowledge of existents as they are to the extent of human capacity.
B) Performing actions as they ought to be.
C) The soul's emergence from potentiality to actuality.
D) The Sharia itself.

Key Takeaway
Theoretical wisdom is knowing reality as it is, and practical wisdom is acting rightly; together they constitute wisdom as the soul's perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says practical wisdom is acting 'as ought to be, to the extent of human capacity.' Is there an area in your life where you know what you ought to do but are not doing it? What one step can you take today to align your action with what you know is right?
?
Wisdom is defined as the soul's perfection in both knowledge and action. Reflect on a recent moment of learning: did you stop at knowing, or did you put that knowledge into practice? If not, what held you back, and how will you ensure your next lesson ends in action?
